The Red page
Fiction
Ihe important question, “What makes a
man enlistV” is delightfully put and con-
vincingly answered by John Palmer, in The
King's Men (Seeker, per Dymock’s Library).
The author of this absorbing and important
piece of war literature takes a group of Eng-
land’s young men, and shows the leaven of
patriotism working in their strongly indi-
vidualised minds, until, one by one, the inner
compulsion of their Englishness prompts them
to enlist. He paints a faithful picture of
the English Attitude toward the intrusion of
that annoying, even aggravating, thing called
War upon the sacrosanct party politics of
ante-helium England. His group of young men
is chiefly composed of deliberate amateurs of
life, busy on the cultivation of their own
souls, and, here and there, one or two with
souls worth the cultivation. Yet to each
the impulse comes, usually in a strange dis-
guise. Of course, all these soul-struggles
might have been prevented if only England
had introduced conscription at once; but
then John Palmer, while not defending the
voluntary system, manages to get some good
out of it. “We haven’t your solemn league
and covenant of all Englishmen enforced by
the State. But every Englishman who mat-
ters is arriving at a covenant with himself.
In most cases he has had to face a strictly
personal and domestic crisis, and for this
reason alone the war must leave upon Eng-
land a deeper mark than upon any other
country in the w0r1d.... A system which dis
graces our leaders will be recognised in day
to come as exactly the school we needed fo
the recovery of our English virtue. Wha :
do our leaders matter? They stand for a
age in which our English citizenship wa
lost. But our political disgrace is bringin
its own painful remedy. There is hardl
a person in the country who by reason o :
the absence of leading in our Government
has not had to ask and to answer for him
self what his citizenship implies.” (No
John Palmer is not writing of Australia.)
It is necessary to add that the stories that
the author tells of his young men—anc
young women—are of absorbing interest, anc
that they are told in a delightful manner.
Whoever H. G. Dwight is, his name i
henceforth one to be looked for in fictio
lists. Stamboul Nights (Hodder and Stough
ton, per Dymock’s Library) is the work of
master of fiction. Doubtless it would b
wiser to wait until H. G. Dwight has con
firmed this assertion with other collection
of stories as remarkable as this; but it save
time to make the assertion now. Perhap
the easiest way to indicate the character oi:
these stories of modern Constantinople is t
quote the author: “They give one such an
idea of the extraordinary wreckage that ha
been left on the shores of that old Greek sea
....There is something more secret anc
ancient about it —more like Homer and th
Bible and The Arabian Nights .” For thes
stories are like Homer and the Bible and The
Arabian Nights. The author, who aeknow
ledges himself unknown, asserts that he lacks
the gift of invention, and that not one o
these stories could really be called his
“Several of them I put on paper almost ex
actly as they were told me. More of them
were pieced together out of odd bits of ex
perience and gossip.” But though they seen
like transcripts from some forgotten Arabia)
Nights, though he has invested them witl
the right atmosphere and scented them witl
the fragrance of romance, the stories are
indubitably his. He has style—the right
style, and his artistic cunning lies in what he
has left out. The things he leaves out
are infinitely more charged with mystery
than the mysteries he permits us to
glimpse. And the things he tells are
so impressively true. He knows the
minds of that polyglot population of Asia
Minor, that ironic tongue, as he says, thal:
Asia sticks out at Europe, and from his long
residence as an American in Constantinople
he has gleaned many a strange tale of mys-
tery. He had the flair for meeting those
“favored people to whom things happen. I
have never made up my mind just how they
differ from the rest of u 5.,.. All I know is
that there is a wireless telegraphy going On
about me, of which I haven’t the code. Per-
haps that is why I have such an inordinate
curiosity about people’s stories.” 11. G.
Dwight is wilfully deceiving us. His mind
vibrates to that wireless telegraphy: he is
one of the few writers who have the. code.
These stories are modern. They have to
do with American missionaries who dig
foundations for their churches and find
enormous bronze doors beneath the rubble
of centuries of superimposed civilisations;
with Turkish sailors who somehow navigate
a steamer that eventually to their surprise
arrives somewhere; with houses whose walls
are lined with looking-glasses (and with what
you see in those looking-glasses) ; with the
amorous adventure of the milk-brother of the
Sultan; with the gay American girl who
married a wowser missionary, and the face
she recognised when she looked down into the
newly-opened sarcophagus and made out the
figure of the young prince immersed (for
how many centuries!) in a clear pale-amber
liquid ; with the superlative rascalities of the
Armenian; with the beautiful faun who
smoked cigarettes; with prosperous, frock-
coated Turkish gentlemen who sold little
boys for eunuchs and beautiful Circassians
for slaves, and Bibles for—well, for a quite
curious purpose; with the wives of Ameri-
can consuls who bought bathing dresses for
the Sultan’s many wives (they would bathe
on the beach, and they thought the bathing-
costumes too pretty to wet) ; with the dear
old Pasha who had married a young French
wife and who didn't open the great chest in
which she seemed so interested. “What hap-
pened next? My dear fellow, you remind me
of those kids who will never let you finish
their story! Nothing happened next. That
was the beauty of it.’*
War
Another book by Patrick Macgill, The
Great Push (Jenkins), deals with the British
advance at Loos in this Irish writer’s grimly
realistic manner. Practically the whole of
the book was written in the trenches by this
stretcher-bearer, and the remainder in hospi-
tal after he was wounded. Macgill leaves
nothing oiit. He shows us the men writing
their addresses for each other in ease they
should be killed in the charge, and spending
the last of their mpney on Turkish cigar-
ettes and champagne; he' meticulously de-
scribes the charge of the London Irish and
his feelings as he charged; he supplies the
gruesome details of his work: “Men and
pieces of men were lying all over the place.
A leg, an arm, then again a leg, cut off at
the hip. A finely-formed leg, the latter,
gracefully putteed. A dummy leg in a
tailor’s window could not be more graceful.
It might be X; he was an artist in dress, a
Beau Brummel in khaki. Fifty yards further
along I found the rest of X.” And he tells
of the starving men in the trenches and the
loaf of bread that was soaked and red—three
of the rationing party had been wiped out on
the way. But more interesting than these
horrors are the remarks of the men in the
trench. Bill, reading a paper, says: “The
Bishop of says this is a ’oly war.
Blimey, ’e’s talkin’ through ’is ’at. ’Oly,
indeed, it’s ’oly ’e11... .’Ere’s somethin’ about
parsons in this paper. One is askin’ if a
man in ’oly orders should take up arms or
not.”
“Of course not,” said Pryor. “If the par-
sons take up arms, who’ll comfort the women
at home when we’re gone?” “The slackers
will comfort them,” someone remarked. “I’ve
a great respect for slackers. They’ll marry
our sweethearts when we’re dead.”....
“The married men say to the single men:
‘You go and fight, you slackers! We’ll stay
at ’oine; we ’ave our old women to keep !’
Sayin’ that, the swine!” said Bill angrily.
“Them thinkin’ that the single men ’ave
nothin’ to do but to go out and fight for
other men’s wives.”....
“We have no particular hatred for the
men across the way,” said Pryor. “My God,
the trenches tone a man’s temper. When I
was at home (on furlough) our drawing-
room bristled with hatred of some being
named the Hun. Good Heavens! You
should hear the men past military age revile
the Hun. If they were out here we couldn’t
keep them from getting over the top to have
a smack at the foe. And the women ! If
they were out here they would just simply
tear the Germans to pieces. I believe that
we are the wrong men, we able-bodied youths
with even tempers. It’s the men who are
past military age who should be out here.”
Komain Holland’s articles upon the war
have met with a mixed reception. The
author of that tremendous novel, John
Christopher, in which he compares the ideals
of France and Germany, now writes as a
cosmopolitan rather than a Frenchman. lie
does not regard Germany as a nation of bar-
barians. “I know the intellectual and moral
greatness of your mighty race,” he writes
to Hauptmann. “I know all that I owe to
the thinkers of old Germany.” His whole
life-work has been to bring the minds of
these two nations together; and he does not
despair, after the war, of effecting a recon-
ciliation. Naturally such an attitude offends
his own countrymen. But it is this lofty
view-point that, in the volume entitled Above
the Battle (Allen and Unwin, per Sydney
Book Club), proves Rolland a true patriot.
For the. bitter reproaches he permits him-
self against his old friends shows that he
has found them out. And though he has
hard things to say against Panslavism they
are like butter on a lordly dish compared to
the heavy brick he hurls at Pangermanism.
And, after all, it is only the old Germany
that he. loves. “What art have you pro-
duced since the monumental work of Wag-
per? What new and original thought can
you boast of since the death of Nietzsche,
whose magnificent madness has left its traces
upon you though we are unscathed by it?
What German writer can you set up against
Tolstoi and Dostoievsky, those giants of
poetic genius and moral grandeur? In
music Germany has only neurotic jug-
glers with orchestral effects, like Richard
Strauss, but not a single sober and virile
work of the quality of Boris Godunov. No
German musician has opened up new roads.”
But Rolland’s philosophic mind remains
above the battle. He enunciates pregnant
aphorisms: “The European thought of to-
morrow is with the armies. The furious
intellectuals in one camp and the other who
insult one another do not represent it at all.
The voice of the peoples who will return from
the war, after having experienced the ter-
rible reality, will send back into the silence
of obscurity these men....” He draws
attention to the fact that the criticism of
the war is merely that of the older genera-
tion. The younger voice has not yet been
heard above the cannon. And in a striking
article entitled “The Murder of the Elite”
he remarks: “Never, in any period, have we
seen humanity throwing into the bloody
arena all it? intellectual and moral reserves,
its priests, its thinkers, its scholars, its
artists, the whole future of the spirit—•
wasting its geniuses as food for cannon.”
Rolland is a philosophical Pacificist, but
he is no pro-German. His mind is above
the carnage, but his heart is with the
wounded with that most sorely wounded,
France.
Shaw
Another name on the casualty list, Private
Bernard Shaw, obstinately refuses to be dead.
In the dim days of the past, A.o. 11)12 —
which should really be: b.w. 2 (Before the
War) or, if you like the Latin, a.b. 2
Shaw wrote three plays, Androcles and the
Lion, Overruled Tind Pygmalion. Two of
these, at least, were produced to loud
applause and louder controversies: this
shrewd showman has long ago found out
that if you take care of your controvei'sies
the royalties will take care of themselves.
And all three appeared in various magazines.
Hence, for the average reader, the only point
of interest in the belated publication of these
plays in volume form lies in the inevitable
prefaces. But it may be remarked for the
uninfonned reader that Androcles is a de-
lightful comedy of the Early Christians, with
a pantomime lion chasing a comic-Caesar
amid, a coruscation of brilliant repartee, a
Lavinia who is much more alive than Mercia,
and a miracle much more convincing than
the miracle in The Sign of the Cross. And
incidentally Shaw makes the Early Chris-
tians first cousins to the Suffi’agettes. They
are all prepared to die for their faith, but
each apparently has a different faith, and
Lavinia, in the last analysis, discovers that
she hasn’t a faith at all.
The Captaijj : Are you then going to die for
nothing '!
Lavinia : Yes: that is the wonderful thing.
It is since all the stories and the dreams
have gone that I have now no doubt at
all that I must die for something gi’eater
than dreams or stoxues.
The Captain: But for what?
Lavinia : I don’t know. If it were for some-
thing small enough to know, it would
be too small to die for. I think I’m
going to die for God. Nothing else is
real enough to die for.
The Captain : What is God?
Lavinia: When we know that, Captain, we
shall be gods ourselves.
Shaw says that the persecution of the
Chi'istians by Rome was "not a conflict of a
false theology with a true, but an attempt
to suppress a propaganda that seemed to
threaten the interests of the established law
and order. . ...All my articulate Chi-istians
have different enthusiasms,' which they accept
as the same religion only because it involves
them in a common opposition to the official
religion and consequently in a common
doom.” But Androcles is certainly a more
joyous pantomime than any Australia has to
suffer at what should be her festive season,
and a play with more dramatic action and
more humbr in it than anything that has
been imported from the Neutral States.
J*
But in Shaw’s published plays the
preface’s the thing. Usually much longer,
too. The Androcles Preface “on the pros-
pects of Christianity” advances the quite
reasonable suggestion that the world ought
really to give Christianity a trial. And,
naturally, the temptation to re-wi’ite the
Gospels “without prejudice” was too much
for Shaw. The pith of this extraordinarily
interesting restatement of the Gospels is this:
“We must get rid of the idolatrous or mono-
graphic worship of Christ. By this I mean
literally that worship which is given to pic-
tures and statues of him, and to finished and
unalterable stories about him. The test of
the prevalence of this is that if you speak or
write of .Jesus as a real live person, or even as
a still active God, such worshippers are hor-
rified. .. .You may deny the existence of the
divinity of Jesus; you may doubt whether he
ever existed ; you may reject Christianity....
and the iconolaters, placidly contemptuous,
will only classify you as a freethinker or a
heathen. But if you venture to wonder how
Chi'ist would have looked if he had shaved
and had his hair cut, or what size in shoes
he took, or whether he swore when he stood
on a nail in the carpenter’s shop, or could not
button his robe when he was in a hurry, or
whether he laughed over the repartees by
which he baffled the priests when they tried
to trap him into sedition and blasphemy, or
even if you tell any part of his story in the
vivid terms of modem colloquial slang, you
will produce an extraordinary dismay and
horror among the iconolaters. You will
have made the pictura come out of its frame,
the statue descend from its pedestal, the
story become real, with all the incalculable
consequences that may flow from this terrify-
ing miracle. It is at such moments that
you realise that the iconolaters have never
for a moment conceived Christ as a real
person who meant what he said, as a fact, as
a force like electricity, only needing the
invention of suitable political machinery to
be applied to the affairs of mankind with
revolutionary effect.” Therefore he suggests
that it is up to us to give the Christianity of
Jesus a fair deal —and promptly cruels his
philosophic pitch by pointing out the remark-
able rosults that would follow such a revolu-
tionary suggestion. It would mean a sort
of Sosh compared to which the present Tiger
would be merely a tame poodle. It would cei'-
tainlyannoy Quite Respectable People. Which
is an excellent reason why it should be tried.
In his preface to the one-act Overruled
Shaw says: “This piece is not an ai’gument
for or against polygamy. It is a clinical
study of how the thing actually occurs among
quite ordinary people.” lie was apparently
impelled to write this farcical comedy because
all the other farcical comedies he has seen
shirked the last act. Granville Barker
observed that “if the third act of our farci-
cal comedies dared to describe the conse-
quences that would follow from the first and
second in real life, they would end as squalid
tragedies.” Which, Shaw adds, would
greatly improve these entertainments. Shaw
doesn’t want to hear the lies the husband
and the wife tell each other to conceal what
ffhey have done, but the truths they tell each
other when they have to face what they have
done without concealment or excuse. There-
fore he proffers Overruled as a model to all
future writers of farcical comedy. Just as the
Black Death was wiped out when society
faced its sanitary problems, so a worse
plague than the Black Death will disappear
when we face our sexual problems.
Lilt, despite this portentous preface,
Overruled will be found to be quite an innoc-
uous comedy. There are only four people
in it, two conventional wives and their con-
ventional husbands, each husband philander-
ing with the wrong wife. How these affairs
begin is delightfully explained. “Do you
know why half the couples who find them-
selves situated as we are now behave horri-
bly / Its because they have nothing else to
do, and no other way of entertaining each
other. You don’t know what it is to be
alone with a woman who has little beauty
and less conversation. What is a man to do?
She can’t talk interestingly He can’t
look at her: if he does he only finds out that
she isn’t beautiful. Before the end of five
minutes they are both hideously bored. There
is only one thing that can save the situation;
and that’s what you call being horrid. With
a beautiful, witty, kind woman there’s no
time for such follies. It’s so delightful to
look at her, to listen to her voice, to hear all
she has to say, that nothing else happens.
That is why the woman who is supposed to
have a thousand lovers seldom has one;
whilst the stupid, graceless animals of women
have dozens.” And there is a deep philo-
sophic truth hidden somewhere in the state-
ment of Mrs. Lunn, who being, as she ad-
mits “what people call rather a fine figure of
a woman,” is bored by all the men who seem
to think her a creature with volcanic
passions: “All the volcanic women I know
are plain little creatures with sandy hair.”
d*
Pygmalion, with an Australian audience,
would utterly lose its point. For it is a
joyous comedy of phonetics. In England
where every county speaks a different lan-
guage, or at least pronounces the same lan-
guage in a way that is hardly intelligible to
the inhabitants of the next county, and each
strata in society has its own unmistakable
dialect, a Professor of Phonetics might be
able not only to make a living but to take a
flower-girl and in six months make her speak
as well as a duchess. But in this continent
where the same language is spoken the same
way by every class of society, the Professor
of Phonetics wouldn’t be able to find any
subject for his miracle. Our flower-girls
speak as good Australian as do our rag-
emporium society leaders —our only substi-
tutes for duchesses. This is a sample of the
English flower-girl’s speech: “Ow, eez ye-ooa
san, is ’e? Wal, fewd dau y’ de-ooty bawmz
a matlier should, eed now bettern to spawl a
pore gel’s flahrzn than ran awy athaht
pyin. Will ye-oo py me f’tliem?” The
amazing thing is that a “young lady” in-
stantly understands and answers her. Yet
the stuff is excellent comedy, and in the part
of Mr. Doolittle, the flower girl’s father,
Shaw has added one more to his list of
authentic character-studies. ,
It will be remembered, perhaps, for the
echoes of the Pygmalion controversy reached
even our shores, that this is the play in
which the converted flower-girl, dressed like
a duchess, scandalises polite society by say-
ing “bloody.”
Freddy (opening tye door for her) : Are
you walking across the Park, Miss Doolittle?
If go
Liza: Walk! Not bloody likely. (Sen-
sation.) lam going in a taxi.
Evidently the Great Australian Adjective
has reached Bernard Shaw. England can
have it: it is getting rather out-moded here.
Shaw unconsciously admits that he failed
as a playwright in Pygmalion, since he finds
it necessary to add a narrative sequel, ex-
plaining what he omitted to make clear in
the play. The falling of the curtain leaves
the reader, as it did the audience, pretty
certain that Liza would go back to her Pro-
fessor. Shaw says that she didn’t; and ad-
vances various reasons to show that she
married Freddy. Surely that was the busi-
ness of the playwright and not of the writer
of prefaces? He could have made Liza’s
preference plain: he didn’t. And though
Shaw assures us that Liza married Freddy,
this page simply can’t accept his assurance.
And the -assertion that another character in
the comedy subsequently met H. G. Wells
this page also disbelieves. For surely if
Clare was going to meet Wells, Shaw
couldn’t have resisted the opportunity of
lugging H. G. Wells on to the stage and
letting us all see him, with “his pleasant
neatness and compactness, his small hands
and feet, his teeming ready brain, his un-
affected accessibility, and a certain fine ap-
■prehensiveness which stamped him as sus-
ceptible from his topmost hair to his tipmost
toe.” Perhaps Shaw is keeping Wells for
another play....that is, if the'war allows
Shaw to write another play. Somehow this
page can’t forget the sight of his name in the
casualty-list.

people holding Civil, Military, Social or Commercial posi-

other professionals, feel the strain almost to breaking point, the
nerves are found to be the weak spot in the chain of healthy life.
The ability and fitness to do things depend upon real good sound
nerves.

and give way.

brain that supplies power, and it helps to keep fit those who are fit

poisonous acid in the blood. This acid, just
like tiny pieces of broken glass with rough
edges, settles in the joints and muscles and
causes that awful agonising pain of back-
ache, rheumatism and gout, gravel, and the
burning sensation of inflammation of the
bladder, etc.

strengthen the kidneys and drive the

system.

To suffer with fortitude is
heroic, but to suffer needlessly
is idiotic. While most sufferers
regard pain as due to the
dispensation of Providence,
doctors see in it a certain
neglect—the neglect of simple
rules of health.
OF all rheumatic afflictions acute lumbago is the
most painful. It arises from a gradual accumu-
lation of excess uric acid in the system; the muscles of
the lower back become impregnated until the muscu-
lar fibres stiffen and every movement causes agony.
The simple habit of taking Kruschen Salts every
morning cleanses the system of excess uric acid, braces
the nerves and gives to the user the daily enjoyment
of natural vigorous health. Get the Kruschen habit.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Little House.
When my heart goes a-roving
’Tis the wide ways for me,
And the fields and the hills,
And the big, blue sea.
Then ’tis far, far I wander,
And ’tis little that you care,
With your wiles and your smiles
And your eyes and your hair.
But the dream of you follows,
Or it gleams at my side;
And I turn, turn about,
For the world seems too wide.
There’s a rose-mist about you
And ’tis sweet, sweet you are,
With your throat and your cheeks
And your face like a star.
When my heart comes a-homing
’Tis the little house I see,
Where you sit all alone
With a stool nigh for me.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Somewhere in France.
My heart is breaking, but I may not weep,
Nor ease my grief by word or sign or
glance —
Only a private, sleeping his last sleep
Somewhere in France!
None knew we loved; to mourn I had no
right, .
Save as one mourns a passing friend,
perchance;
Only he knows, who fell there in the fight,
Somewhere in France.
A puppet I, with jest to jest replying;
The strings are pulled—l nod and smile
and dance....
But all my heart beneath the sod is lying
Somewhere in France.
QUEENSLAND.
H. A. SMITH.
Heart Free.
So many loves have I: the changing sea,
Gold sunlight glinting on an outstretched
wing,
The slope of hills, the strong wind high and
free,
And everv crvstal note the lark may sing.
Gorse-perfumed winding roads, the budding
The gleam of lily by the river’s brim,
Soft swelling downs, the busy hum of bees
Mid columbines, and wallflowers Quaker-
prim.
Earth circled, can I miss your clasping
arm,
Kissed on the mouth by every breeze that
blows?
For your inconstancy what need of balm
While every hedge blooms with a fragrant
rose?
N.S.W.
NORA McAULIFFE.
Pot Plants.
In this, our stylish boarding-house,
Where’er a fellow turns,
Are shepherdesses stroking cows,
And beautiful epergnes.
The stairway is as wide as those
We find in tales that thrill,
Where Jack, the hero, cuddles close
And kisses sweetheart Jill;
And where the runner does not run
With leaps like startled hare’s,
Away from wind and rain and sun,
Are pot plants on the stairs.
Set out in random, careless ways,
They lift green, quiet fronds
Like palms that shield from solar rays
Some cool and silent ponds.
The rain may lash the dripping roof,
The wind may lose its breath,
The sunlight may be overproof—
They are as still as death.
Whene’er the world goes wrong with me
And life seems filled with cares,
I find calm courage when I see
Those pot plants on the stairs.
They look so darned respectable—
A boarder would be mad
To gaze on foaming flagons full
And come home feeling glad,
With muttering lips and tilted hat
And feet like hoofs of steel,
To seek to clamber to his flat
With all the world a-reel!
Oh! if he clutched and held awhile
Those waving palm-affairs. . ..
I think I see them primly smile,
Those pot plants on the stairs!
I’ve seen a slim maid water them
And wash their pampered stalks.
I’ve noticed how her short skirt’s hem
Flit-flutters as she walks.
Through an enamelled window pane
The light flashed varied hues
Upon her; but it tried in vain
To touch her with the blues.
For, as she plied her water-can,
She sang old-fashioned airs
About a stairway and a man
And kisses on the stairs.
* * #
A burglar called an hour ago;
He crept in like a thief,
And wandered through the rooms below
With cheek beyond belief.
Lulled by the sight of fair epergnes
In our swell boarding-house—
Of milkmaids at their china churns
And shepherds milking cows—
He started up, without a light....
I heard his burst of prayer
As he went crashing through the night
With pot plants in his hair.
MAORILAND.
WILL LAWSON.


Look
After Australian Industries.
CHANGE our old-fashioned
methods of always relying
upon some importer to
supply our requirements.
To keep on top Australia must
not import more than is exported,
yet during the six months ending
30th June, 1916, Australia im-
ported tyres and rubber goods to
the value of £528,691, two-thirds
of which came from other than
Allied countries.
, Australian Tyre Manufacturers
could easily have supplied these
tyres and rubber goods, thereby
keeping our money in the country
and giving constant employment
to thousands of Australian work-
people.
Think this over carefully.

HE PERDRIAU TYRE.—
There is a great deal in the
design of a motor tyre, but
added to that must be the relia-
bility that can come only from
workmanship that is accurate,
based on thorough knowledge of
the materials used.
The Rib feature is scientifically
correct and superior to studs or
projections of the usual non-skid
tyre—these cause abnormal strain
on the fabric under them, whereas
the Perdriau extra-thick three-rib
type, gripping the road with three
ribs, distributes the load evenly
on the full surface of the tyre,
thus positively eliminating tread
separation, fabric trouble and
skidding.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 14, 1916.
Pkice 6d.
THE HERO.
The total loan expenditure during the past six years had been £41,000,000. The loan expenditure of their predecessors, the Carruthers and Wade
Ministries, was £13,000,000, or an average of £2,000,000 a year. If those Governments had spent money at the rate of £4,000,000 a year, the Labor
Government would not have been confronted with the desperate arrears which met them when they took office.— N.S.W. Treasurer Holman's Financial Statement.
HOLMAN (to Police-Constable the Public): “Ish not my fault, conshabl. There wash Arshdeacon Boyce never had a drink all lash week; an’ Jimmy Marion,
he didn’t have one yesherday; an’ Arshbish’p Wright shon water-waggon; so I been very busy all evenin’ wipin' out their arrears."

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 14, 1916.
THE PRINCIPLES OF THE
CENSORSHIP.
The first difficulty which confronts any
democracy in getting to holts with an
enemy and trying to smash or choke him
into submission is the lack of a sufficiently
intimate and swift connection between the
brain centre and the limbs of the body
politic. When an autocrat, who is lord of
life and death over a nation, conducts war,
his action can be just about as swift as his
thought, and the secrecy with which he does
things as complete as the firm line of his
closed mouth. True, the effect of the thing
he does depends upon the goodwill of the
human tools with which he works; and the
mere fact that they are called upon for
blind, unintelligent service may take a lot
off the edge of their effort. Democracy, on
the other hand, is the keenest weapon in all
the armory .of humanity, once it is brought
to the point of united and intelligent action;
and if, in the nature of warfare, it is almost
inevitable that the autocrat should get in
the first blow, it is equally sure that
Democracy, once properly roused, will make
the most sustained, earnest and enduring
fight. But while the brain of the auto-
cratic system resides under one man’s hat,
that of Democracy is diffused over a whole
community; and many an accident may
happen to the nerve connections between
that diffused brain and the hitting parts of
the machine. No enemy can possibly get
inside the person of the autocrat to tap the
wireless messages which run from thought-
cell to fist: but it is quite possible by
espionage and treachery for the enemy to
get at the proposals and intentions of a
Democracy. For this reason the people of
any free land under popular rule must in
the event of war delegate some of thejr
power to representatives and leave those
representatives to set up a Censorship which
will keep the enemy’s fingers out of the
national pot. The Censorship thus becomes
an integral part of Democracy at war and
a safeguard against perils inseparable from
a political system which has the habit of
posting its inmost thoughts on the largest
hoarding available.
* * * *
At the outset of the present war a press
Censorship was seen to be necessary in Aus-
tralia. Enemy ships were abroad in Aus-
tralian waters. Enemy subjects in large
numbers mixed with the ordinary popula-
lation. War on enemy possessions to the
north of Australia had been undertaken by
Australian forces. There was a very real
need for secrecy lest the thoughts and plans
of the Commonwealth should reach those who
were seeking our hurt and enable them to
checkmate or counter our efforts, or at any
rate to delay and hamper them. There was
a moment when the city of Sydney was
threatened by the guns of the enemy, and
when the knowledge of the whereabouts of
our Australian fleet might have meant the
murder of citizens in their beds and the
destruction of their property. That the
press at such a time should be restrained
from telling the public things which it was
particularly anxious to know, but of which
it could not be informed without letting the
agents of the enemy into the secret also,
was of the utmost importance. No sane man
could grumble at a Censorship that pre-
vented his throat being cut or his suburban
roof removed by shell fire. How far the
same conditions still exist only those in
authority know. The general opinion is
that the danger is long past; but the con-
stantly-increasing extension of the Censor-
ship gives the lie to this. Remembering
always the sole purpose for which that Cen-
sorship exists, those who control it are
asserting loudly by its greater severity and
scope that the danger to Australia is now at
least 10 times more acute than it was before
the Sydney caught he Emden or the German
fleet was blown to extinction at the Falkland
Islands.
* * * *
It was recently suggested that the
Censorship, which is now nominally in
the hands of the Military power, should
be strengthened by the appointment of
an ex-journalist of very high standing.
But this is not at all sufficient. We have a
High Court which was called into being
to safeguard the liberties of the people.
Now, enormous possibilities of attacking and
undermining the rights and privileges of the
people lie in the Censorship. Ordinarily
the press is the guardian of the public
interest. If a wrong is being done, it
is to the press that the discoverer of the
wrong takes his knowledge. Or he tells his
tale upon a platform to half a dozen people
perhaps, and the press tells it to half a dozen
millions. The public does not know now the
things it is not permitted to know. That
is to say, newspapers are not permitted
to tell what things are censored, and
there can be no protest against it.
The public must not be informed that
a wrong is proceeding. This is a power
too vast to hand over to party politi-
cians. It is a power which at least should
be subservient to the High Court; and no
regulation or instruction should have any
force until it had the approval of a Judge
of the Court. No work of equal importance
confronts the distinguished Bench. It is
not judicial work, perhaps, in the ordinary
way; but it is work of exactly the same
character as that which the High Court was
created to perform.
WHAT A FRIVOLOUS PERSON CAN
DO WITH A CASHBOX.
Australia’s noblest spender started the
other day to make what he called “a state-
ment on the financial position of the coun-
try,” and devoted most of it to hiding the
position, pouring the oil of gladness over
himself and talking nonsense. As Treasurer
of N. S. Wales Holman’s latest yarn com-
menced with references to his “surplus,”
which is a mere superstition, went on to
assurances that he had carried the country to
a situation of great prosperity, added some
foolishness about the growth of bank
deposits, and ended with the mournful con-
fession that “I am financing from month to
month, not knowing what a day or an hour
may bring forth.” A brilliant genius attired
like Solomon in all his glory burst into Syd-
ney a few years ago and talked like a rich
uncle from Fiji. He may have had 2d., but
even that is doubtful. In a few months he
had a million or so, with palatial offices and
a reputation which commanded the respect of
everybody who wanted to sell a gold brick.
This paper is not quite clear where he is
to-day, but if there is 2d. about him it belongs
to someone else, and his reputation is mostly
a long, lingering smell. Holman ought to
read his history. It will put him wise to the
awful unreality of some surpluses.
* * * #
William Holman’s main claim to distinc-
tion is that, as leader of a party elected upon
a distinct pledge to restrict borrowing, he has
in six years touched Coiien for £41,000,000;
and he justifies himself by the comic explana-
tion that he was driven to do it because the
party which preceded him had in a similar
period borrowed only £13,000,000. “If they
had borrowed £4,000,000 a year,” he says,
“we would not have been confronted with the
arrears.” Coming from anyone else this fan-
tastic effort would sound like a cynical con-
fession of belief that the public is less intelli-
gent than a wallaby. Coming from llolman
it merely corroborates the evidence he has
already given that he handles his job in a
spirit of levity. Those Labor voters who
have not been corrupted by the crowd which
captured the machine will remember that this
Government came into existence, not to
borrow more, but with high hope of ending
a disastrous period of borrowing. That was
the excuse of Ministers for throwing out
their predecessors. When was the party
guilty of false prptences—‘formerly, when it
secured office on the ground that the Wade
crowd had borrowed too much, or now, when
it asks to be kept in office because the Wade
crowd hadn’t borrowed enough? Holman
and his friends can’t have it both ways. They
were frauds six years ago or they are frauds
now. But the Holman theory holds out in-
teresting possibilities for the future. The
next crowd will be encouraged to borrow
£100,000,000, offering the obvious explana-
tion that it was compulsory to do this
because the previous Government had left
them large arrears of borrowing to make up.
There is no special reason why anyone
should borrow £41,000,000. For every
pound of-“revenue” raised by taxation and
the sale of public lands, and derived from
railways and other services and State
enterprises, Holman has borrowed an
almost equal amount. Why not borrow
double that sum? The beginning, middle
and tail of the yarn is that the coun-
try’s financial position is “splendidly buoy-
ant,” as a result of borrowing £41,000,000.
Why not make it more splendidly buoyant
by borrowing £82,000,000? The present Gov-
ernment’s successor will certainly have just
as good an excuse for borrowing that much
as the present Government has for borrowing
£41,000,000.
* * * *
The cheerful Juggins assures the public
that his splodge has not increased taxation—
that the average taxation per head by the
State in the six years preceding his jag was
£2 10s. 10d., while during the period of the
boom that goes before the burst it has been
£2 Bs. sd. The figures are wrong; the basis
of the argument rotten. The averages for
the two periods are 15s. 2id. and £1 3s. 7d.
If Commonwealth taxation be added, the
average totals are: 1904-10, £3 ss. 5d.;
1910 16. £4 16s. lOd. But in this connection
averages mean nothing. It is the figures for
each successive year that count, and for
State taxation in N. S. Wales they are: —
s. d. £ b. d.
1904- .. 15 3 1910-11 .. 12 6
1905- .. 17 5 1911-12 .. 1 2 2
1906- .. 18 5 1912-13 .. 15 10
1907- .. 13 3 1913-14 .. 1 5 6
1908- .. 11 7 1914-15 .. 111 9
1909- .. 15 4 1916-16 .. 114 0
1c is no use pretending these figures mean
anything except that taxation has been more
than doubled since the Labor party came
into office. But there is nothing to be
ashamed of in increased taxation. The test
in such matters is not the figures themselves
but the benefits derived by the taxpayer. In
this instance the increase, levied in forms
which are passed on to the common herd,
represents the price of a spree on bor-
rowed cash. The Labor party made a good
beginning. Its members had theories of
economy and decency when they first took
office. Arthur Griffith was made Min-
ister for Works simply and solely because
he was an offensive person who was credited
with a large capacity for saying “No,” and
that was then regarded as the prime qualifi-
cation of a Labor Minister for Works. Of
their virtues they quickly purged themselves,
however, and went out on the jamboree. Now
they are wringing out of the public more
than twice as much as was wrung by the
Government whose exactions had originally
filled the Labor party with alarm and dis-
gust. For the current year’s excitement
and the further sustentation of the borrow-
ing orgy, additional taxation is imperative;
but because there is a general election at
hand Holman prefers to say nothing about
the subject. Meanwhile by paying 51 per
cent, to the Norton Griffiths syndicate
for money and 1 per cent, for underwriting
and 5 per cent, for spending, the Govern-
ment is able to keep the pot boiling.
•* * * *
As proof that we are prosperous the
young man tells us about the growth of
bank deposits. Of course bank deposits
have grown: the growth represents his
borrowings. So too the railways would
have revealed an immense loss had it not
been that they have been held up by the
borrowing; they have been earning revenue
by dragging about great masses of imported
goods acquired with the cash borrowed
from the benevolent Coiien. If a demented
so.uatter with a swag of coin obtained by
mortgaging his flocks turned up at Coota-
bidjibidgi and proceeded to paint the land-
scape red, one definite result would be a
large increase in the township’s bank
deposits. If the lunatic stayed long enough
and had sufficient currency, everybody
in Cootabidjibidgi would have an account
and be brandishing a cheque-book. And
the local carrier would do a thriving trade
carting up more booze than the population
ever heard of. In the same manner a State
that borrows millions cannot point to the
banking or business figures as evidence of
soundness. Victoria had never imported
so much nor had such dazzling banking
figures as in the days immediately before
the explosion and the discovery that behind
it all there were only a few finks. llolman
on his own admission is at the stage when
he doesn’t know whether there is even half
a fink to fall back on. What he does know,
though, is that the whole crazy superstruc-
ture of finance, and legislation, and wild-
cat State enterprise erected by him is’built
on sand. The king pin of it is the pawn
ticket, and the day he cannot pawn the
nation he will see his blessed “prosperity”
toppling over. He knows, too, that it is at
that point a defrauded people will have to
start and make good the arrears created by
his scandalous betrayal of the citizens.
WHEN AUSTRALIA VOTED “NO.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The unexpected had happened. Australia,
by a substantial majority, had voted “No
Conscription.” The single men training
in the camps were thereupon given an honor-
able discharge and in a body joined the
R.S.A. There were, here and there, a few
conscientious objectors, who obstinately held
to the belief that Australians should still
fight for Australia, and insisted on being
sent to the Front. It was held by the rest of
Australia that the continent would be well
rid of these irreconcilables. So they were
stealthily- shipped off to reinforce their
equally 'misguided comrades in France: and
as they sailed the steam-whistles gave them
three derisive hoots.
That is the point where this story begins.
All the preceding is ancient history. So here
it is necessary to introduce Bill Fitz-Jim.
lie had enlisted with the first contingent, he
was in the first landing at Anzac, and he was
one of the 854 soldiers who were absolutely
the last soldier to leave Gallipoli. Private
Bill Fitz-Jim, for all his bravery, remained
a private. When he arrived in France he
looked around and found how few of his
cobbers had survived with him, and a legiti-
mate ambition stirred in his honest soul that
some day he might become a lance-corporal.
But promotion passed him by for many
months, though soon, owing to his company’s
losses, he was the only remaining original
member. Nevertheless Fate had something
in store for Private Bill Fitz-Jim. If it
had not been for that Referendum he would
probably have remained a private to this
day. But when he learnt that Australia had,
by a substantial majority, refused to send
any more reinforcements to the Australian
army, except the negligible dribbles of the
last dregs of voluntary enlistment, he openly
rejoiced.
“You see,” he said, in answer to his com-
rades’ scandalised protests, “it’s this way.
It’s up to me to be made a lance-jack; but
my company has been wiped out and built up
again with drafts so often —you blokes is
only blanky drafts, anyway, and I’m the
whole bleeding company—that nobody knows
I’ve been ’ere so long. I’ve, had five C.O.’s
and 15 platoon-sergeants, and I’ve lost count
of adjutants and corporals and such. And
none of my superior officers ever stay long
enough to recernise me. They get killed or
wounded or transferred, and the new C.O.’s
and company-sergeants naturally prefer the
non-coms, in the drafts they came over with.
So here I am, Private Bill Fitz-Jim, first
in the Landing and last to hop off the blanky
Peninsula. But now, don’t you see, there
ain't no more drafts coming. We got to
carry on by our blanky selves. Australia
says she ain’t got no further interest in the
Anzacs. We got to carry on by our blanky
selves. See? No more drafts. That means
that as we gets casualtied some of us has got
to be promoted to fill their places. And
that’s where Private Bill Fitz-Jim comes
in.”
He possessed a logical mind. He waited
till the last of the privates was bombed, and
then, with a becoming modesty, wore the
single stripe on his sleeve. The first step,
as is proverbial, was the most difficult. The
day he got his stripe he led a raid on the
German trenches and returned a sergeant.
The next day, so rapidly had the army
dwindled without reinforcements, he sug-
gested an impossible counter-attack that
made him a colonel. But in a week he was
back in the ranks again. For the remnants
of this heroic, though minute, army had to
be drafted into a single platoon composed
entirely of officers of the higher commands.
Colonel Fitz-Jim was the only colonel in
the platoon; all the others were his superior
officers. They were commanded in person by
General Bibdwood, temporarily acting as a
platoon commander. And again it was
Colonel Fitz-Jim who. dissatisfied at his
subordinate post, led the last of the Anzacs
in a forlorn hope against the Hun. This
time he had inconceivable luck. He saw
every one of his superior officers shot down
by a German machine-gun, and struggling
on, untouched, stood a moment on the Ger-
man entrenchment and proudly promoted
himself, first to the position of Australian
Chief of Staff, and then to the command,
vice General Birdwood, wounded, of the
whole Anzac Army.
Then, carrying the last Australian flag, he
nobly surrendered. He had got what he
wanted. And as Australia, when it said
“No,” had surrendered in 1916, he saw no
reason why he should attempt to defeat the
collective vote of his countrymen.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Soaping the Stairs.
In the violent game of politics, as played
under party rules, the soaping of the front
stairs is a matter of tremendous importance.
To get to office is a great thing; to stick to
office a glad thing; but to so arrange matters
that, if possible, no successor shall have any
decent show of carrying on is the supreme
thing. If an employer found that one of
his workers so arranged matters that in the
event of his dismissal no one else could
take up his work and carry it on with
success, he would have that worker taught
that such things were not to be; but the
community, which is in effect the employer
of the politician, has been taught to regard
party as an excuse for pretty well anything
and never seems to realise the high price it
has to pay for the soap on the stairs and
the consequent fall. Masses of work undone
or underdone; promises of this, that and
the next thing; bills hustled through in
shapeless mass to make confusion, where,
under the old order, there was inequality
but something like plain system—these
things are not mere chance or due to clumsy
floundering of light-weight legislators in
heavy boots, but are often part and parcel of
the deliberate design to soap the stairs for
the next fellow’s fall. Here a railway is
started. It is a fool railway which runs
from nowhere to nowhere, but a section or
two is put in hand by the falling Ministry,
and a host of workers, speculators and
hangers-on are made interested in it. The
new Ministry, which comes up the soapy
staircase, must stop that railway construc-
tion in the interests of decency ; yet in doing
so it makes mortal enemies of all those
interested people, and the general public
opinion is divided between the statement
of the stair-climbers that the railway is no
good and the combined statements of those
who have just gone out of the back door
and the interested parties they have created
that the railway is the key to Paradise. Or
a Ministry fairly solidly seated sets out on
a financial campaign which involves 20
years of intense borrowing. Its own term
of office is only three years; but it
smiles in security, knowing how thoroughly
those prearranged visits to Cohen have
soaped the stairs which reform must face
before it can get in even a first cut with


[ 10 ? Xe ‘ ie P r ' ce ias always to be paid
f- u for the extensive ramifications
or the game; but it is so integral a part of
the_ whole theory of party government that
plain Edward Billings, of the one-vote-
pne-yawn brigade, never dreams that he
isn t getting value for his money or that
there are more business-like methods of
carrying on the work of the country. What
Billings and Smith and others" fail to
grasp is that the soap put on the stairs is
cumulative as well as concurrent, and that
between parties and terms of office nobody
gets in with a mop or bucket to clean up
the mess. The stairs grow more and more
slippery, the waste of soap is greater and
greater and the bill for the whole thing is
constantly mounting up. Continued to its
logical conclusion, the game must make the
staircase in such condition that nobody can
climb it at all; and the excess of slipperi-
ness running out into the street must brifig
down even those ordinary citizens with no
political ambitions who are merely attempt-
ing to hurry along the pavement to catch
U P with some overdue fragment of Aus-
tralia’s work. In a word, the party system,
if somebody doesn’t now and again rise a
little above party, works towards a final
and universal bump of a most disastrous
kind. In most countries that are cursed
with the party system, somebody has occa-
sionally risen above party and the stairs
have been partly cleaned; but in Australia
there isn’t the slightest indication of any
such good fortune. The alleged truce
owing to the w T ar notwithstanding, party
was never before so complete in its sway
or so virulent in its working, nor was the
stair-soaping industry ever more in evidence.
In fact, the state of the staircase is now
such that no one except a professional poli-
tical tumbler will make even an attempt
to climb it; and if any non-party reformer
should arise he will have to insist on put-
ting up a ladder and getting in by the attic
windows.
Another Aspect of the Seven-
Parliament Disease.
N.S.W. Premier Holman proposes that the
Ma State shall make provision for incapaci-
tated soldiers by supplementing the pension
by so much as will bring the allowance to £3
weekly. On the face of it, any project for
making easier the lot of the victims of war is
commendable. But there are very solid objec-
tions to the Holmans of Australia butting in
on the business. It is one wholly within the
competence of the Federal Parliament to deal
with, and no encouragement, should be given
that tribunal to think it can fob its respon-
sibilities on to the States. Soldiers enlisted
in N. S. Wales or any other State are not
entering the service of a State. They are
soldiers of the Commonwealth, and it is the
Federal authority alone that is responsible to
them and to the nation for protection against
the consequences of disablement. No reason-
able ground exists for supposing that this re-
sponsibility will be satisfactorily discharged
if seven Parliaments commence handling it.
The _ inevitable result will be confusion, re-
crimination and duplication of administration
and taxation. If Holman must provide pen-
sions for someone there are ample opportuni-
ties for getting rid of cash, and at the same
time doing work strictly within the State
domain. Soldiers’ widows, for instance, are
not the only widows. There are many
orphaned children and women deprived of
their breadwinners who deserve as well of the
community. Anyhow, their fathers and hus-
bands were in the callings’ of peace serving
their country as well as they knew how. For
example, to the widow of the man who loses
his life in a big mine the State makes the
munificent allowance of 2s. 6d. a week. To
the widow of the man who loses his life in a
small mine it makes no allowance at all. Is
there any reason for leaving it at that’ The
special responsibilities of N. S. Wales are big
and numerous enough without politicians
making additions to them as a cheap method
of attracting votes by securing momentary
reputation for benevolence.
Effective Protection—Enforced by the
Hun.
The moment extra-high duties are levied the
importer foams at the mouth, various people
wail moanfully about the iniquity of taxing
things that are not and cannot be produced
here, and Freetraders generally split the
firmament with lamentations over the folly
of highly-paid Australians expecting to com-
pete with cheap foreign labor, the impossi-
bility of successfully manufacturing with
such a small local market to supply, the
villainy of encouraging trusts and combines
by prohibitive duties, and so forth. Let us
see what the war has taught us in this
matter. The goods which we got chiefly
from Germany in the past included surgical
instruments and carbolic acid —articles on
which monumental duties would have been
levied if Andrew Fisher and vVilliam
Hughes had carried out their election
promises. They did nothing of the sort.
Neither did Joseph Cook, who made almost
similar promises and had an equal oppor-
tunity in 1913. But in August, 1914, Bill
of Berlin set about forcing the world to
use his surgical instruments at whatever
price he liked to charge, and a little later
the British navy restricted their use to a
small part of Europe. That is to say, by
December, 1914, Australia could get io
more scientific or surgical instruments from
the Huns, and precious few from England or
America, while carbolic acid was as scarce
as German ships on the high seas. The
prohibitive tariff was in full swing! And
nobody moaned —it wasn’t any use. Instead,
the Melbourne firm of Felton, Grimwade
and Co. set about supplying the demand as
speedily as possible. It took time, brains
and energy, but all were available; and
to-day Felton, Grimwade and Co. are utilis-
ing costly machinery and skilled workmen
in filling orders for these goods from all
parts of the Commonwealth and Maoriland.
The wages paid for this interesting work are
high, but they remain in the country; and
that is the most important thing of all. The
firm is also manufacturing much-needed car-
bolic acid and naphthaline from local tar.
The prohibitive tariff is still in working
order and will continue until the Hun has
quit the trenches and got back to the factory ;
after that he will dump his cheap goods here
again (through some neutral nation, if
necessary) and smash up the Melbourne fac-
tory and any others that may then exist,
unless Protective duties running if necessary
up to 500 per cent, replace the existing
“free” or “20 per cent. ad. val.”
Maoriland Converts War Profits into
Debt for the Kid.
Maoriland got thoroughly ashamed of its
Big Cadge a few weeks ago, and its Ministry
set about finding out what sort of war loan
the country could provide for itself. It
had collected £10,000,000 when The Bulle-
tin last heard the total mentioned and was
then, struggling for more. The story of
the Dominion’s borrowing, whether abroad
or at home, makes shameful reading.
Maoriland has been making enormous profits
out of the Empire’s disaster. In spite of
this and the obvious fact that war profits
should have been appropriated to the last
shilling for war purposes, the Ministry pro-
ceeded to borrow with both hands from
harassed John Bull, while Ward blithered
concerning “the necessity for building up a
strong position” and talked bunkum about
“keeping the rate of interest down.” Finally
—quite likely on a hint from J. Bull —it
resolved on this local loan, and the
net result of it all is that £10,000,000 of
mostly war profit has been converted
into permanent debt free of income tax.
The Government has not only handed on to
the citizen of to-day and to-morrow and the
day after a foreign blister of £12,000,000,
but has fastened upon him this local debt
of £10,000,000, and hopes to fasten on him
more of it later on. It is all a beautiful
illustration of how posterity can be taken
down before it arrives on the scene.
Firstfruits of Six O’Clock Closing.
The police report on the effect of six
o’clock closing in N. S. Wales suggests that
the situation is a bit mixed. Fewer arrests for
drunkenness is the bright feature of the
various district statements; but as this star
item is bracketed with the general observa-
tion that there is more sly-grog selling and
likely to be still more in the future, some
of its rays are dimmed. As it is per-
fectly plain that the sly-groggery for sheer
self-preservation must keep its drunks out
of sight, the fact that the city streets don’t
reel with the efforts of glad but uncertain
boots is no proof of added sobriety. Also,
it must be noted that the sly-groggery itself
is not likely to stay near the main streets
in the centre of the town where the “Come
along now” of the arresting constable was
wont to be heard. The most interesting
feature of the present situation, however, is
the plea of James Marion, secretary of the
N.S.W. Anti-liquor Alliance, for more
police. Again and again the anti-liquor
people have yelled to the voting public that
the closing of the pubs would mean a reduc-
tion of the number of police; in fact, they
have not hesitated to put down the whole
cost of the upkeep of the police to the fact
that Bung continued to trade. Now with
the hotel bars closed during what were
formerly their chief business hours, the
same crowd comes forward and asks for
more police! In the face of another of their
favorite contentions that the police cannot
control the liquor traffic, what would be
the use of the new appointments? Any-
how, wasn’t the six o’clock closing to help
the war along? How is it going to help
the war if men who should be enlisting
to smash the' Kaiser must be employed to
assist in throttling the sly-grog business
which the early closing has created? As
far as the existing pubs are concerned, the
police inspector-general does them proud.
Not a sign of liquor has been found any-
where after hours. It looks as if Bung,
though beaten in tactics by his shifty oppo-
nents. is playing the game more cleanly
than they.
Fluff and liung do a three-rounds
Exhibition Spar.
“Chug” writes with a view to securing a fair
balance of things:—
Beer has been cut off after six o’clock, partly on
the ground of war economy, partly because it appears
in the view of some citizens to be an ally of the
Kaiser, partly because a bunch of would-be moral
reformers continue to make a loud noise. What
about the fluff-shops—the places where the women of
the nation squander cash, time, energy and the rudi-
ments of intellect? Is it a decent thing that the
beer should be limited while the idle rags still flaunt
themselves on every breeze? Let them fight it out.
Round I.
Every charge that can be brought against the pub
can be proved against the fluff-shop, while not one
argument which is urged in favor of Bung’s estab-
lishment can be employed as an excuse for Fluff’s
show. The pub induces comradeship and friendly
feeling; the other only provokes envy and hate.
The pub satisfies a natural craving; the fluff-shop
unnaturally destroys all feminine contentment. The
pub supplies a stimulant which makes the world
look rosy; the fluff-shop provides an irritant which
reveals the unsatisfactory condition of the purse and
the household allowance.
The first round goes on moral points to the pub.
Round 11.
Man must drink, but he can get along with moderate
discomfort on water alone; woman must dress, but
she is more comfortable and far nicer to look at in
simple attire, for the purchase of which two visits
per year to the shop would be sufficient. Man
becomes intoxicated and loses control of himself
through over-indulgence in liquor; women entirely
loses control of herself and becomes an hysterical
maniac when the glitter of a few odds and ends of
colored rags, beads, feathers, scrapings from the hides
of animals and other savage adornments are spread
before her eyes. Men squander money in liquor;
women squander a great deal more money in idle
endeavors after bad taste and ugliness.
Round 11., after some even fighting, ends decidedly
in favor of Bung.
Round 111.
If it is necessary to drink less in order to win the
war, it is equally necessary to economise in other
directions. The most direct way in which such
economy can be effected is by limiting the display
of useless goods, reducing the hours of useless busi-
ness and forbidding a fluff-shopkeeper to serve a
woman when she is mad.
(Here somebody enters a protest on behalf of
Fluff on the ground that the latter is already com-
pelled to close his place of insanity at six o’clock.
As an impartial referee, I dismiss the protest. Closing
his shop at that time merely congests the trains and
trams with unnecessary giggle and petticoat during
the very hours when men who have been working are
trying to get home to a dinner that isn’t ready. The
pubs are closed during the man’s hours of liberty
from toil. As the majority of women take care to
be free at all hours from toil, it follows that it is
during those hours that the fluff-shop should close.
Fight on!)
The pub sells a large quantity of imported spirits
and ales during the day; but after six its main busi-
ness was the sale of Australian beer. The fluff-shop
sells as much imported shoddy as it can at all hours,
and it blows and brags about the fact that its wares
are not Australian in a fashion to encourage the
belief that all fluff-shops are anti-Australian in spirit.
War calls for an equality of sacrifice. Men at the
bidding, mostly of women, have laid Bung on the
altar. In the face of that bitter and bleeding sacri-
fice, what ought to be done to the other fellow ?
I pause to allow the antimacassar to be thrown
in from the lace and lingerie corner.
The Arbitration Tangle.
The tangle that Australia has got into
through having two distinct authorities at
work on the job of fixing wages was exposed
in Adelaide the other day. The learned
Judge Jethro Brown had to decide what
was a living wage for the metropolitan area,
and was up against the hard fact that Jus-
tice Higgins, of the Federal Court, had fixed
11s. 4d. as the minimum for unskilled labor
at Port Pirie in the same State. Brown
knew it was a large pill to swallow, but he
gulped it down somehow and explained, with
regard to the contention that Justice Htg
gins’s standard must be taken: “I cannot
conceive any greater disservice I could do
the employees than to grant this claim.”
which, he went on to say. would shut up
half the factories in the State. As a matter
of cold fact, the so-called living wage isn’t
a Jiving wage at all; and the sooner Judges
leave oil trying to delude themselves that it
is the better. The cost of living is onlv
remotely concerned with the matter. What
Judges are concerned with is what the indus-
try can afford to pay. Justice Higgins
persuaded himself that the Broken Hill in-
dustry, which keeps Port Pirie going, could
to Pay 11s. 4d., and that was called
the living wage. But if the Broken Hill
industry were only half as prosperous his
Honor would no doubt have found it possible
to persuade himself that life could be very
well sustained for half a crown a day less.
The so-called living wage therefore isn’t a
fixed quantity ; and Judge Brown did the
sensible thing in declining to accept the find-
ing of even a High Court Judge as a prece-
dent. _ It was a misfortune that he had to
put his figure at 95.; but the establishment
of the principle that no general rule can be
applied makes it possible for some other
crowd at some other time to get 12s. 6d.

“Exile” : The projected pleasure trip to
Kingomalleyland in search of efficiency for
Australian workmen is not likely to take
place. As one who has just been there I
declare that it shouldn’t, anyway. In every
large factory and warehouse time-clocks and
time-keepers and watchmen and shop-
walkers keep the workers going just the
same here as they do there. The rest of the
alleged efficiency of the U.S.A. consists of
the employment of more men trained to their
trade in technical schools. Here in Aus-
tralia we need more technical schools, and
then again more of them. If our Universities
dropped their habit of so poorly imitating
those two obsolete seats of learning, Oxford
and Cambridge, and shouldered their obvious
duty of linking up the State schools with
technical schools and the Universities, in a
very few years the Australian workman
would be not the equal but the superior of
the American. The root of the matter lies,
however, in the need for a proper Protective
tariff. We cannot expect to sret skilled work-
men, new industries, scientific research or
the right development of “our illimitable re-
sources” without the sort of Protective
tariff that the United States possess. The
Vic. Government New Industries Commis-
sion, the Federal Research Council, the
Efficiency Grand Tour —all are mere vanity
and vexation of spirit without a towering
tariff. Australia imports yearly £05,000,000
worth of goods which it is efficient enough to
mostly make for itself, and all the efficiency
of which human beings are capable is worth-
less and of no avail unless we start and do
things for ourselves and make things for our-
selves and stop sending abroad to ask in-
ferior people how to suck eggs and speed-up
our workers.
There is a deal of talk about new industries
for Australia. A Federal advisory council
is wandering at large getting evidence about
nothing in particular, and it has an
official abstractor and translator at
work in Melbourne copying bits out of
scientific publications. The impractical
Hughes talked in a large way of spend-
ing £500,000 per year on this “great
work.” Victoria also has a new industries
committee, but it seems likely to be afflicted
with the same vagueness and the vice of
looking through a telescope instead of get-
ting busy on the job-round the corner. New
industries are desirable, but it is more
urgent to make the old ones into real live
concerns. If Australia honestly sets to
work to develop as they ought to be
developed all the industries now struggling
on under the handicaps imposed on them in
the past by the Freetrader and the importer,
the Stinking-fish party and other obstruc-
tionists, she will have work enough for a
long time. There is a good field in one line
alone for a country that sends away 98$ per
cent, of the raw wool she grows and then
imports woollen manufactures to the amount
of millions per year. When we have
straightened that up it will be time to start
to boom the silk industry. Iron- and steel-
making and shipbuilding and other vital
industries are already right here, and the
thing for the nation to do is to put enough
brains, capital and muscle into them to make
them really adequate. Unfortunately, such
plain matter-of-fact business doesn’t appeal
to the alleged Protectionists who are afraid
of duties that will protect, or to the doc-
trinaires and faddists who think that the
present boom in the public feeling for Aus-
tralian industries and for scientific research
and development is specially designed by a
kind Providence to float their pet fads into
prominence.

“Oopabella” : There’s a hopeful sign now
and then that the people are getting a bit
sick of being driven in party blinkers. Two
years or so ago one Jenkins, sitting in the
Footscray Council as a Laborite, fell foul of
his league, partly for voting for the coun-
cil’s sitting representative on the Metrop.
Board of Works instead of for the league’s
nominee, a young and untried councillor.
For this, Jenkins was expelled from the
league, and became a pariah. This week he
had to face the electors for the first time
since his backsliding. The local league
cracked the whip vigorously. Members of
Parliament were imported into the cam-
paign, and Mavnr and councillors went on
the stump against the renegade. Jenkins
fought alone, but he fought willingly, and
Footscray was filled with wrath, crude
economics and threats of manhandling,
while the outcast was killed six nights a
week by the mouths of outraged standpat-
ters. And on polling day Footscray gave its
opinion of the party joss by whizzing Jen-
kins in by 7SG votes to 2G3!
“Coal Tar”: Re “Dyer” (B. 27/7/TG) and
his advocacy of Federal support for the pur-
pose of investigating Australian plants and
other material to ascertain their dye values.
Research on the lines he suggests might con-
tinue for years before sufficiently definite
results were obtained to justify a start with
the commercial preparation of the colors.
Meantime the close of the war would see the
German stuff on the market again. Practi-
cally all modern dyes are manufactured from
coal-tar derivatives. Australia possesses
plenty (even allowing for what is dumped
into the ocean U and also more than one

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Peace is the dream of the Wise ;
Peace is the story of Heaven ;
Peace is the Key of the Skies,
Bartered for blood
And for tears
And for sighs!
But the blood that is shed
Is not mine!
And the tears are the tears of the fighting
man’s dam,
And the sighs are the sighs of his widow,
His orphans, his dear ones,
His hearts that have bled,
As he bled in the field.
Bat I, nobler far is my mission! For snugly
ensconced
J[o I lie on my cushions of ease and declare
That war is a sin and a curse and a snare,
That Satan himself hath begotten it here,
And that damned are the sword and the
shield and the spear
Although I am sheltered behind them
And owe them my freedom
To prattle and prose of war and its woes,
Of the Blessings of Peace they have bloodily
bred
And brought home to my door.
For I, though the blood of my mother should
run
And the babes of my hearthstone should
perish.
Would spurn at the spear and revile at the
gun
And crouch with the cur till the battle were
done,
Then take as my due what my neighbors
had won,
The Peace, sweet Peace that I love.
And Peace, like a slime, should lie thick on
my soul
And I would grow fat at the board and the
bowl.
I could lie in the pasture and chew the
sweet cud
Of the calm that was bought by the rivers
of blood
Of the brothers who bled for my Peace,
Of the boys w T ho lie dead for my Peace!

From Plymouth Rock.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A. P. Gardiner (Massachusetts), in a speech in the
House of Representatives, denounced the Senate’s
retaliatory black-list legislation as a blow to sup-
port Germany’s ugly cause.
“You hypocrites,’’ he declared, “you claim you are
devoted to humanitv and despise commercialism.
Why do you pass amendments that will serve no pur-
pose except to stuff your sordid pockets with the
golden harvest of your fellow-men's misery.”— Cable.
Miles Standish spoke, his hand upon his
sword.
Ilis was no naltering to gain applause.
Lead bullets for the shiftv Indian horde —
He learned plain dealing fighting Freedom’s
cause.
The large stars dimmed, the world went on
its way,
Trick and finesse the far, clear hopes could
mock;
And yet, though tourist-worn, remained the
grey
Old sober bastion of Plymouth Rock.
Somewhere dwelt truth, for all the dollar’s
sheen,
For all the bray of noise and boast of mud.
In Massachusetts, whiskered, long and lean.
Drawled one who felt the throb of Pilgrim
blood.
He, of the race who first had slain a king
For treason to the royalty of Man,
Drove his straight furrow when the woods
were green,
And felt and hoped as when his task began.
Once in an age he rose. His trumpet blast
Shrilled from the brazen tube for worlds to
hear,
As, all the long, dull days behind him cast,
He stood erect, a freeman without fear.
Again he speaks. A Wilson with the pen
Or blatant Roosevelt is the large land’s
choice;
But from the State that long bred truth
and men
Sounds clear and high America’s own voice.

The Long Fight and the Hard Road.
One result of Roumania taking a hand in
the game of bash has been to again set the
tongue of the prophet on the wag. He had
a fairly good innings at the start and made
7402 guesses; but as the dreams broke down
one after another the prophets retired into
seclusion and nursed a bitter resentment
against the enemy until the situation began
to look more favorable for forecasts. These
are now being handed out at the rate of
several dozen per day, and vary from a tip
that the war will terminate in December of
next year to warnings that the end is to be
definitely in sight the day after to-morrow.
It all means—nothing. Of unmistakable
evidence that the struggle will be over
within a year there is not a trace. That the
Turk, the Bulgar and the Austrian will
have to be broken before the German is
finished with looks certain —and these per-
sons are still fighting. It is true, of course,
that against the Hun in Northern France
the armies of Britain and France are making
progress; but it is useless to pretend that up
to the present this represents the establish-
ment of any decisive military advantage.
The offensive, if sufficiently well backed,
may turn into a triumph for the allied arms
over a wide region. Or it may not —any
of a dozen things may get in the way. The
things still indisputable are that, before it
can turn into a triumph wide enough to
count, the cost will be heavy, and that to
put the German on his back the momentum
of the attacking armies must be maintained.
It is the side that is growing stronger actu-
ally and relatively that will decide the terms
of peace. Roumania’s entry upon the field
brings substantial assistance to the Allies
and changes to some extent the strategical
features of the campaign. But it is easy to
exaggerate both and to lose sight of the fact
that the cardinal objective of the Allies is
to bring the war to an end. It is to prevent
prolongation of the struggle and the hideous
wastage of fighting at less full strength
that every man and shell the Antr-s can com-
mand are urgently wanted. That is what the
Australian people—faced by their casualty
lists on the one side and their voluntary re-
cruiting figir-'S on the other —have to remem-
ber on October 28.
