“Cukse o’ Moses” lines up:—
When does my turn come with the Censor-
ship? I'm not greedy, but, as a citizen of
Australia, with the same rights as every
other citizen, I want my fair share of any good
things that happen to be going. This Censorship
thing appears to be about the softest snap anybody
has had on this planet since people stopped being
owned like cattle and pigs; and I’m standing in the
line waiting my turn to use it. Of course, Australia
hasn’t authorised me to use it, but then it hasn’t
authorised anybody else, and, under the circum-
stances, it’s only fair that the thing should go round.
One citizen is as good as another; and I want to
do a lot of things with the easy confidence that the
press won’t be able to say anything about them—l
want the right to do any dared thing I please with-
out anybody making a remark. If one chap has it
we all ought to have it; and by making it general
for a week there would be no time lost. Look how
that would puzzle the enemy 1
. Holman in his financial statement to the
N. S. Wales Leg. Assembly included in his
estimated expenditure from loan votes a
trifle of £20,000 for the equipment of rail-
way refreshment rooms which are to be run
as a State concern. That is real high
finance. In time of war and while “financing
from hour to hour” the State’s credit i.s
pledged to buy cupt? and saucers, jam dishes
and cruets to be smashed by the man of
to-day and paid for by the kid of to-morrow.
The Defence Department wants another
50 doctors for the Front; and that adds
ripeness to the time when the Federal Gov-
ernment should do what the Government of
India did years ago—offer a prize for a
substitute-doctor: a medical manual con-
veying simple instructions for the diagnosis
and treatment of various ailments and
injuries. A distinguished man, Sir William
Moore, won the Indian prize, and his work
was duly printed and handed round. Done
in Australia, for Australian conditions,
such a work would prove a blessing to many
an Outback battler and his family. It would
have to be circulated, of course: it wouldn’t
do any good to anybody stacked up in a
corner of the Government Printing Office
with cobwebs on it —like, for instance, those
important volumes, issued by various Aus-
tralian Governments, on “What to Do till
tlie Bailiff Comes” and “Dry Profit-Sharing
in Victoria.”
Cynics are beginning to wonder whether
Brother Snowball, the representative Vic-
torian Orangeman, isn’t a conscious joke.
He utters silly little tales of li.C.
bigotry, etc., which are obviously bound to
be refuted. Thus his recent story, told to
the State Parliament, of how a small boy
Protestant suffered from sectarian animo-
sity at a Christian Brothers’ school, carried
an effective contradiction on the face of it.
It wouldn’t be fair to lawyer Snowball to
accuse him of being a simpleton in making
these easily-refuted statements. It seems
much more credible that he makes ’em as a
mere matter of Orange-lodge business in
the first place and to please the Catholics
in the second place. The fact that the
rabid foe of Holy Church is on friendly
terms with many of its people, seems to be
fully explained by the fact that in his
capacity of Orange-lodge politician he fixes
up sectarian Aunt Sallys for Holy. Church
to shy at.
“Stapleton’s” problem:—
My man is willing to join the ranks, and, though it
will pinch, I am perfectly willing to try to manage
on a diminished income while he does his hit at
the Front. But he has his problem, too. The
majority of people seem to regard the enlisted man
with a big family as a dodger, and the opinion is
often openly stated that he has trouble at home.
Were conscription in force the single men would
go first, then the married men without children,
and finally the fathers of families. Thus all men
and women would understand and accept the posi-
tion. 1 will therefore welcome conscription, as it
will most certainly untangle some of the puzzles
that vex me to-day. Meanwhile, my man is torn
between two conflicting duties —his duty to the
State as its defender; his duty to his family as its
supporter.
Boot purveyors in Victoria who trade in
footgear with brown-paper soles may soon
find the business as unhealthy for themselves
as it has always been for their victims. A
Government Bill just circulated in the Leg.
Council provides that any person who manu-
factures, sells, or exposes for sale boots or
shoes of which the soles do not consist
entirely of leather will be liable to a fine
of £2O unless the other materials are speci-
fied ou the outer surface The Bill also
provides that boots must be stamped with
the name or registered trade-mark of the
seller or the manufacturer. That “or” is
the only mistake. Australia badly wants
to get into its head the names of a few
standard makes —of boots as well as other
things—and the best way to get it done is
to compel the manufacturer to put his brand
on all his goods. If he isn’t compelled by
law to do it, a hundred great and small
retailers will make him put up their names,
and, though that may be a small advt. for
a pretentious retailer, it is ho good for Aus-
tialian industry.
Two items were knocked out of the N. S.
Wales programme last week- —the Early
Closing Bill in the Assembly failing on a
point of order that the leave to introduce
did not cover the Bill as introduced, while
the Workmen’s Compensation Bill got it
on the point in the Upper-cut House. These
failures, coming on the top of others, caused
the Opposition to ask derisively, “Who’s
your draughtsman?” But the Opposition
had little to crow about. Wade’s legislation
became notorious for the space that was left
for bullock teams to be driven through it;
and there is on the Statute Book an Act of
which Geo. Feed was the author, dealing
with the width of streets, which is inopera-
tive because no penalties were prescribed
for breaches.
Died in Adelaide, at S 2, R. K. Threlfall, a
patron of athletics, especially cycling, and
for nearly 40 years manager of the D. and
W. Murray clothing factory. In fact, he
claimed to have been the originator of the
system which makes most Adelaide importers
large manufacturers as well.
“Toki”: One Morrison, an engineer on the
Tongariro when she had her fatal argument
with Bull Rock, between Napier and Gis-
borne (M.L.). was part of the crew of the
Clan McTavisli which put up the good fight
against the Moewe. After that bit of excite-
ment he was chief greaser on a submarine-
hunter in the Channel and drew £7O prize
money before a submarine retaliated by
sinking the hunter. Then he thought he
would try a quieter life, and shipped on the
Tongariro. Two hours after scrambling
ashore from the wreck he had taken a job in
a foundry. You can’t sink a foundry.
Farrer, M.L.A., complained that the Vic-
torian railways charged just as much to
carry wheat from Leopold to Geelong—
seven miles —as to drag it to Melbourne, 40
miles further. The State has a heavy deficit,
and the railroads are not paying; naturally,
therefore, this disclosure of waste electrified
the House. Electrified it so much, in fact,
that it yawned, and nobody showed the
smallest subsequent interest in the state-
ment. To save a loss by stopping plain
waste is much too prosaic.

The Gonsalez Opera Co. gave us “Un Ballo
in Maschera” at the Sydney Opera House
on Saturday night. It was a third-rate per-
formance with first-rate interludes. The
latter were supplied by the tuneful Cappelli,
baritone Scamuzzi, basso Cacialli and the
canary trills of Miss Visoni. Cappelli as
the beauteous Duke Riccardo, in love with
Amelia, was handicapped by the stage busi-
ness. This forced him too often into the
company of the soprano (Mrs. de Revers),
whose brassy top notes (double forte ) make
the atmosphere quiver. When Amelia’s
strident tones hit the rafters the good tenor
can only roll his eyes to Heaven and trust
to a kind Providence to send his words over
the footlights. Cacialli was a heavy vil-
“LUCIA DI LAMMERMOOR ,” AT
SYDNEY GRAND OPERA HOUSE.
Henry Ashton ( Belloni ) and Lucy Ashton
(Miss Gonsalez).
lain Tomaso, of admirable vocal quality.
As one Oscar, a merry page, Miss Visoni
scored another success. But it was to
Scamuzzi (secretary Renato, whose ’Melia
flirted her fan in the Duke’s ear) that the
highest honors of the evening fell. With
brilliant melody and fervid action he picked
up scenes that dragged. The house cheered
him to the echo, and lie deserved it.
Miss Russ was an unconvincing Sorceress
with an overworked tremolo, an obviously
acquired nose like an eaglet’s beak, and
clumsy drapery. As for the rest, the chorus
work was fair and the orchestra verb d
fiom bad to worse. For no good reason,
therefore, did the company drag conductor
Gonsalez on to the stage to bow to a
thimbleful of applause. This week’s qivck
changes give us “La Bohbme” and “Faust.”

If an Egyptian mummy sat up in its
coffin and started to mumble the bazaar
gossip of 2000 B.C. with dry, cracked lips,
the remarks wouldn’t sound more out-of-
date than the once-smart speeches of old
“Florodora” as they are now fluted again at
Her Majesty’s, Sydney. Florence Young, who
bounces through Lady Holyrood’s light-
weight job, seems to be quoting brightly
from the Bible instead of flinging out the
twinkling London repartee of 20 years ago
that Grace Palotta used to get laughs with.
Derek Hudson, in the spotless gear of the
stage tropics, isn’t as impressive as Brown-
low, and Reg Roberts is quite six months
too old for the boyish Captain Arthur
Donegal. Ethel Cadman makes a well-
dressed charity-fete Dolores without any
Spanish onions in the family. Phil Smith
(Tweedlepunch) scores his best hit with an
introduced oratorio burlesque. Leslie
Holland seems to regard his part of Cyrus
Gilfain without enthusiasm, refraining from
dancing in the second act where Hugh Ward
used to do his footlight shuffle. Minnie
Love chirps naturally as Angela Gilfain,
the millionaii’e’s beloved flapper daughter,
and Claude Bantock makes up well as
Leandro, the driver of beautiful chorus
slaves. “Florodora” —booked for 10 nights
and two matinges—ends a creditable quick-
change season of revivals that must have
meant a lot of hard work for all hands on
both sides of the footlights.

“Duncan McClure and the Poor Parson''
leave Sydney Royal after the show on
Saturday. Take it all in all, it’s been a
grand season. The crop went about 20
bushels of Commonwealth notes to the acre.
On Saturday Sydney Criterion will see
Edward Sheldon’s “Romance,” with Madge
Fabian and Frank Harvey in the leading
parts.
Musical Irving, who makes the ’cello sing
and dream with his left hand, is now in the
front trench at Sydney Tivoli. Violiniste
Zona Trador helps him to look after the
artistic side of the bill. Louis London, the
loud grandson of a dignified grandfather
called Standing Bear, roars when his num-
ber goes up, and O’Donnell and Blair pass
in a haze of falling tables and chairs. The
Artois acrobatic pair, Kelly and Fern and
the Dick Dorothy dancing trio are also
amongst those present.
The Strollers have hung out the “Last
Nights” sign at Sydney Palace. They move
out of there bn September 29, to make room
for the returning Allen Doone, who wants to
unload a drayload of shamrock sentiment
labelled “O’Shea, the Rogue.”
On the screen in Sydney this week: —
Hoyt’s: “Battle of Hearts,” a palatable Wm. Fox-
mixture of shipwreck, hate, revenge and self-sacrifice;
and the usual high-class comedy and topical titbits.
Lyceum: Three A 1 Triangle pictures—“ Honor
Thy Name,” “Little Meena’s Romance” and “His
Hereafter.”
Crystal Palace: “The Hand of Peril,” the film
of Arthur Stringer’s underworld thriller.
Lyric : Annette Kellerman as “Neptune’s Daughter.”
Empress: “The Closing Net,” a Pathe presentation
of a thrilling detective story.

The opening section of the Melba matinee
show at Melbourne Majesty’s last Friday
included the only items that were not fami-
liarly frivolous. Firstly came an intensely
melodramatic sketch, written by Frank
Harvey and replete with good old theatrical
devices. “The China Treaty,” as it was
called, thrilled the audience’s mind even as
one disturbs the surface of a stagnant pond
by gently flicking it with a whip. Harvey,
purporting to be the Chinese Ambassador
in London, had the manner of Mr. Wu and
the method of Sweeney Tod, the Demon
Barber. Just previous to the outbreak of
the present war he w-as arranging to sign a
treaty with Germany, which he would seal
with a signet ring. The ring was indispens-
able to the fixing of the alliance; therefore
emissaries from various Great Powers were
sent to sneak into the Chow’s room and
steal the jewellery. The emissaries were in-
variably murdered by the slow and stately
Ambassador. They used to come in at the
door like diplomatic flies, whereupon he

The high-pitched absurdities of the situa-
tions seemed to enthral some of the audi-
ence. Stirling’s suggestion that the chemist
might come to Europe’s aid with a bottle of
germs, which would spread sleeping sickness
among the teeming millions of China’s popu-
lation, must have struck his spellbound
hearers as a brilliant idea. The rest of the
discussion on the Yellow Peril was at least
calculated to set stupid people thinking.
But they missed the humor of this astound-
ing problem playlet. The joke lay in the
fact that it had escaped the Censor.

Melba’s contribution was the last act of
“Faust,” with Fritz Hart conducting a some-
what noisy orchestra. She acted almost as
feelingly as she sang, and her pearl-coated
notes were truly beautiful. When Mar-
guerite recalled, in her delirium, the words
she sang to Faust at her first meeting with
that seductive young man, the quality of
Melba’s voice was supei*b as ever. She
didn’t put it to the strain of outreaching a
high tenor in the finale, for the name-part
was adequately filled by Goossens, the mel-
lifluous Belgian baritone, whilst Fred Collier
was sufficiently ai-tistie and Satanic as the
evil basso from Down Under. A dozen
recalls and a pile of floral tributes com-
pleted a notable performance.

“The Girl in the Taxi,” which is running
at Melbourne Majesty’s until Friday, has
been improved by changes from the original
cast. Dorothy Brunton as Suzanne fully
accounts for the enthusiasm of the three
giddy males who state that they love her to
a man, and Greig does all that Rawlins did
without making the grimaces that marred
the other man’s performance. Transparent
hypoci’isy needs no facial emphasis, and
Greig is just a fat, jolly person in a jolly,
fat part. Cecil Bradley is a likeable
daughter', and Madge Elliott lopks very like
the sort of young matron who js open to be
adopted by a strange uncle provided that
he takes her to supper in room No. 6. Four
members of the co. —Workman, Fisher,
Maguire and Ethel Morrison —have always
been at their best in this gay piece of im-
propriety. Also the acrobatic waiter is an
intermittent shriek of glee, and even Paul
Plunket presents a gladsome appearance in
a sky-blue tunic with bright red coverings
elsewhere. The co. lapses into “High Jinks”
again on Saturday. The following Satur-
day the Royal Comics return, and “The
Geisha” will be awakened from a long Mel-
bourne sleep.

Fuller’s ’Murkan burlesque company broke
out in “Peaches on the Beaches” and “Lost
in a Harem” at Melbourne Princess’s on
Saturday night. These things are no more
entertaining than their predecessors, but
they are more liberally decorated by the
chorus, which makes some fresh disclosui’es of
its eharms. The costumes and their con-
tents are practically all that the perform-
ance has to offei% the work of the principals
amounting chiefly to interruption between
the various courses of girl. Murray Simons
and Lew Dunbar, the head comedians, are
no less noisy than before, and keep up a
tremendous play of gags that are mostly
revered classics in vaudeville circles. Simons
has improved somewhat since the opening
night, but Dunbar once or twice forgets
himself—and this paper can’t altogether
blame him. It tries to forget him itself.
The parts of a cullud lady and a stewardess
are thoi’oughly well filled by the sumptuous
Miss Mason; and Miss Moore is again a
petite young person who appears to be
growing out of her clothes. Miss Eileen
Barnsley warbles several songs passably;
but none of the others are of any signxii-
cance, barring the brilliant Le Groh trio of
acrobats.

Seeing that the Follies will be back at
Melbourne Tivoli on Saturday the writer
will not dwell at length on all his sad
memories of the latest accessions to the
bill. Prince and Deery, described as novel
singers and talkers, are an echo of innumer-
able mixed pairs from America, the lady
being apt to ask questions and the man
being bound to laugh loudest at the most
banal of his • own jests. If these sei’io-
chronic couples hadn’t a splendid audacity
and an ample experience of the vaudeville
game they couldn’t pass for entertainers.
The Parshleys are simply music makers
who obtain melody by the device of rubbing
the rims of glass vessels or beating blocks
of wood with little drumsticks. Their
specialty is glittering attire, especially on
the part of Mrs. P., who skips about her
business as though she were too happy for
words. But her high spirits are not in-
fectious, alas!

At The Playhouse (Melb.) the Repei'tory
Co. will produce “Hedda Gabler” next
Satui'day (16th). Melbourne has seen
nothing of Ibsen’s play since massive Nance
O’Neil misrepresented the heroine long
years ago. Eily Malyon will impei’sonate

At Hoyt’s big Melbourne house this week
Olga Petrova is vividly demonstrating that
many an experienced married woman can
be caught by a flow of hot .words over a
champagne suppei\ The film is entitled
“Playing with Fii-e,” and two or three pas-
sionate scenes are required to show the
extent of the conflagration. The other Hoyt
feature, “A Wife’s Sacrifice,” is more a
matter for the police than the fire brigade.
A couple of young swindlers figure iix the
production, and their manner of deluding a
blue-blooded family reveals once more the
infinite credulity of the nobly born.

“Under Fire,” now at Adelaide Royal,
would be more convincing if somebody liad
taken the trouble to translate it out of
Uncle Sam’s language into ours. In real
life, an English girl, confessing to a harm-
less fib, would no more di’eam of saying “I
lied” than would a British Admiral use
“Surely” (which in England is always either
argumentative or pleading) in its American
sense of hearty agreement. But the thrills
of the play bring the scent of the gun-
powder acx-oss the footlights. Julius Knight
revels in the part of the Irish hero, and
Lizette Parkes wastes no time worrying over
the fact that she has wedded a man who has
a wife already, but grabs such happiness as is
coming to her. Thei’e is more American
talk at the King’s. Harry Bui’gess and
Gus Franks provide most of the fun ; Lola
Hunt is a large, handsome and capable
comedian; and Lydia Carne gives a life-
like imitation of Daisy Jerome. It is funny
—and a bit pathetic, too—to hear a very-
American visitor declai’e, in a song obvi-
ously written for another four-syllabled con-
tinent, his love for Australia, and add the
gallant but reckless mis-statement, “There’s
a hundred million more like me.” But Jack
Kearns restores the Australian element all
right. At the' Tivoli Cyril Mackay is a
number of dashing heroes, and Fitzlierbert
a similar number of loathsome villains,
while capable Redgrave plays anything that
comes handy. Wish Wynne has gone from
the Majestic. Dan Thomas is here, also the
wire-dancing Maximo. Wirths’ Circus and
Dampier’s Dandies have been and gone,
Jack Y.M.C.A. Virgo’s lectures are crowded,
and pictures are everywhere.
Myers, the Jewish Minister of Supplies
for Maoriland, required many ephahs of
oats lately, and demonstrated his firm dis-
belief in the Gentile politician’s contempt
for business methods. In the fii-st year of
the war Massey did the job by announcing
that the Government wanted oats and hoped
no one would be so unpatriotic as to boost
the price. Of course the price rose like a Fok-
ker. When his turn came Myers kept quiet and
employed a firm of brokers to get offers at
reasonable prices all over the country. When
delivery was called for pillars of cloud rose
all over Canterbury’s dusty roads as the
dealers tore about getting supplies before
cocky dropped to what was doing and put
the pi'ice up.
AT POVERTY POINT.
FOUL DEEDS WHILE YOU WAIT.
[For The Bulletin.]
I murdered a man in cold, cold blood.
He fell ’neath the night-owl’s lonely post.
I wiped the gore from the shining steel,
And 1 cursed him there and I cursed his ghost.
I swore my hate to the wide-eyed owl,
’Neath a lonely tree in a lonely spot;
1 laughed aloud as my victim fell,
And I buried his bones in an unknown plot.
Ah! he was a man, and I loved him well ;
But remorse springs not from the foul blood-
stain ;
For after the show we will sup in wine,
And to-morrow I’ll murder him, over again.
maoriland. LESLIE R. FREEMAN.
Balboni, of the Gonsalez Opera Co., was
ordered from Italy something in this way:
“Send suitable tenor first boat.” No reply
was received from the agent or the selected
tenor himself, and the co. went about its
job not knowing w'liether the reinforcement
was coming or not. Then one morning a
stranger was discovered waiting about the
Princess’s Theatre, Melbourne, with that
look of hopeful expectancy everyone wears
when lost amid strange surroundings. Some-
body at last approached him. “Pardon.
Anything I can do for you?” The stranger
nodded gratefully. “I’m the new' tenor from
Italy,” lie said simply. What a deep, un-
natural silence descends when the trumpet-
ing press-agent is absent!
Steele Rudd is going to make some more
“Selection” money out of “Duncan McClure
and the Poor Parson.” The second-last week
of the long Sydney Royal run turned more
money into the Bert Bailey treasury than
any other week of the season.
Herbert Bentley, who swaggered as
General Augerau in “A Royal Divorce,”
tried for a part in the Great Drama last
week.
Will Rayner, of Fullers’, who had a record
run on that circuit, was amongst those who
got into khaki last week. He was a star
singer of patriotic songs at Sydney recruit-
ing meetings.
“Harrison O.” : An unnecessary first act
spoils whatever chance Somerset Maugham
had of making his heroine in “The Land of
Promise” convincing. From Act II onward
the play deals with the taming of a sharp-
tongued, pig-headed young woman who mar-
ries a small Canadian farmer in a hasty
temper and seems to imagine that obstinacy
after the ceremony will redeem the stupidity
that caused her to rush into it. The lady
is not satisfactorily tamed until she has (a)
smashed the household crockery, (b) slapped
“PEG O’ MY HEART,”
AT MELBOURNE
KING’S.
“Jerry.”
Cecil Brooking
her lord’s face, (c) kicked his shins, (d)
bitten his hand, and (e) attempted to shoot
him. The trouble is that this charming
creature has been introduced to us in the
superfluous first act as a mild, lady-like,
sweet-tempered companion to a shrewish old
maid for whom she has acted as guardian
angel, with patience and gentleness which is
repeatedly insisted upon, for 10 years. This
first act does nothing except explain that the
reason why the heroine goes to Canada is
that the death of her taskmistress has left
her without a job. When in Act II w'e find
the same heroine quarrelling with her
brother’s wife and marrying “beneath her”
in a fit of temper, we at once cease to believe
in the sweetly-patient worm of the previous
10 years. “Consistency, thou art a fool!”
is the attitude of the dramatist determined
to make a thin story last hours (with the
help of three long intervals) or perish in
the attempt.
“De Oriel”: All touring showmen know
the old hall caretaker who, after a meagre
house, explains that the show should have
been on a moonlight night, last sale day,
next Saturday—in fact, any date but the
one selected. The hallkeeper at Nyah, on
the Murray, has an original excuse.
“Always come to Nyah in summer,” he
says; “the people across the river have no
bridge, so they have to swim, and the water’s
too cold in the winter.”
“Kaniva” : “Teddy” Royce, the comedian
who first appeared in Melbourne in 18S6 as
Captain Medway in “Turned Up,” is still
on the active list. His great success was
Blueskin in “Little Jack Sheppard.” Others
were Robert Brough (Jonathan Wild), Wil-
lie Warde (Kneebone), Emma Temple and
Fanny Robina (Jack). Royce was a very
clever dancer, and had been for years a
noted harlequin in English pantomime. His
first appearance was at Covent Garden in
“Un Ballo,” in 1860. Later, he became one
of the famous Gaiety four, the others being
Edward Terry, Nellie Farren and Kate
Vaughan. Hale Hamilton recently had a
letter from the old man, who is still vigorous.
“Ancient Prompter”: Pinero’s name is
soon to be on a musical-comedy bill in Lon-
don. A writer long connected with that
loose and careless class of show, having
arrived at the amazing conclusion that a
good “story” would help matters, has
arranged with the dramatist to adapt some
of his early farces for musical purposes. He
has picked a good one to start with in “The
Magistrate,” the bright and snappy laugh-
provoker done here in the happy Brough
days.
“0.K.”: The story of “The Girl on the
Film,” which deals with the lady-killing
loveliness of one Max Daly, a cinema actor,
is not so absurd as it looks when Workman
plays the hero. No stage actor I ever saw
was half so beautiful as the lover in a pic-
ture-play called “Maria Rosa” shown by the
J. C. Williamson Films. The picture is a
remarkable specimen of cinema art as regards
photography, acting and bellowdramatic
interest. Geraldine Farrar is starred in the
name part, but the most striking feature of
the performance is Wallace Reid as the
lover, convicted of the murder he didn’t com-
mit. He has a tall, slim, graceful figure,
and the handsome, clean-cut face that girls
describe as godlike. Kyrle Bellew, at his
prettiest, w r as an insignificant divinity com-
pared with Wallace Reid on the film. More-
over, he is a master of facial expression, like
all the other men in this particular picture
play. Actors, to my way of thinking, can
control their faces better than actresses
when they are silently mumming before the
camera. The women sometimes betray their
sense of the ridiculous when they are trying
to look lovesick or scared, or a whispered
word tempts them to almost laugh.
. Wallaroo (S.A.), where the copper comes
from, has gone into the moving-picture busi-
ness. The town council has bought its own
machine, given notice to a showman who
had occupied the Town Hall, and declined all
offers for either hiring or buying out.
“Barnstormer”: One night in the early
’9o’s I was at Toole’s Theatre, London,
when Johnny was playing “lei on parle
Francais” for the 4004th time. With us in
the stalls was Mrs. Keeley. It was the
old dancer’s 90th birthday, but when we left
the theatre she danced a pas-seul for us in
the foyer, and then went off gaily to supper
with Henry Irving and Johnny.
“ Jwosp” : The Bluebird Photoplay makers
seem fond of Australians. Rupert Julian,
who used to fall downstairs in “The Breed
of the Treshams,” is with them as director
and star actor; his wife, Australian Elsie
Wilson, appears as a star under his direc-
tion ; Syd Bracy is with the same team, and
Louise Lovely (the sugary film-name of
Louise Carbasse-Welch) is on the pay-roll
as a beauty actress.
“Legirons”: The imitation cat was swung
in hearty fashion before the Australian
camera that filmed the local “Mutiny of the
Bounty.” The pigtailed seamen who came
aft to give Captain Bligh a whiff of the
rotten food are shown triced up presently to
the rigging with ugly-looking greasepaint
marks on their bare backs, and the brawny
flagellator stands off and belts them terribly
with “tails” of curtain plush or twisted butter
muslin. But, whatever the cat’s made of, the
job is realistic enough to any person with two-
penn’orth of imagination. The British Navy
only threw' the cat overboard after 1877,
though the Duke of Clarence (afterwards
William IV.) had moaned as Lord High
Admiral over the Fleet’s quarterly return of
punishments as early as 1827. But the old
seadogs of that time reckoned they couldn’t
get along without it, so the torture went on
for another half-century.

SYDNEY—
Mary Pickford races up a few more rungs
of the ladder of success in her latest picture,
“The Foundling.” now flickering across the
screens at the principal Sydney Theatres.
Mary is still as curly as ever. The same
old dimples remain and the big tears come
just as naturally as of yore. You will like
“The Foundling” even better than “Madame
Butterfly,” because it is a picture more like
the sort that first made this little artiste a
film celebrity.

ETHEL: “Is it true your engagement is off?"
MAUD: “Yes, the brute!”
ETHEL: “But I thought you broke it off!”

The Adelaide Licensing Bench seems to be
coming round to The Bulletin’s opinion,
expressed six months ago, that there is no
room in Australia for a club calling itself
“Allgemeiner Deutscher Verein.” The mili-
tary authorities had then shut it up, but it
wants its license kept in a state of suspended
animation, urging that the members are
nearly all Australian-born. The Bench looked
through the rules, found that “only persons
conversant with the German language shall
be eligible to become active members,” that
“all discussions and business transactions at
meetings shall be conducted in the German
language,” that the club’s object in existence
is “the support and encouragement of Ger-
man sociability,” and that the Hunnish name
of it “shall not be altered.” Ncsbit, Presi-
dent of the Court, naturally thought the
whole matter “shrieked for inquiry” ; and the
club’s lawyer, who had not come prepared to
argue about its right to exist, was invited to
attend in three weeks and do so.
The Wilson Government had hardly landed
on its seat in Perth when it gave orders to
hang up the Esperance Bailway, which was
to wander from the harborless port for about
100 miles and finish in the air. The
old Esperance railway agitation of the
gold-boom days aimed to connect up with
Ivalgoorlie via Norseman; but vested inter-
ests in Perth were always strong enough
to squash the project. During recent
years the section of line which the Scaddan
Government put under way last year aas
been pleaded for as an agricultural-develop-
ment proposition. Labor Ministers and
others not entirely disinterested persons
sketched the lands to be served as bubbling
with milk and honey and other vegetables.
Among the evidence they didn't thrust promi-
nently under the nose of Parliament was a
report by Government Analyst Mann showing
a very high percentage of salt in the soil.
The new Ministers, diving into the mountain-
ous Esperance railway file, brought this salt
to the surface and decided to hang up the
costly construction work until the question
of salinity is disposed of one way or another.
Taxpayers who can’t see where the money for
essentials is to come from applaud Wilson,
but the howl from the handful of Esperance
settlers is heartrending. They can’t eke out
an existence by taking in each other’s wash-
ing, because water is so dreadfully scarce in
the district.
* •» * *
From an Australian girl with the Red
Cross in France: —
I told you before of the South Australian boy who
had been so terribly blown about that three amputa-
tions were necessary. He lost one leg, two hands
and an ear, and had three body wounds. We thought
his case utterly hopeless, but now it is certain he
will live. The vitality of the bush-bred Australian
is amazing. All the doctors marvel at it. After a
long coma this lad opened his eyes on Sunday morn-
ing. He looked about him, he looked at me, and
when he smiled I bent down. He could only whisper,
and he said: “Honor bright, nurse, what’s left of
me ?”
* * * •
L. V. Biggs, chief of staff of the Age,
keeps pegging away at his task of persuad-
ing Cold Tea that the rational solution of
the temperance “problem” is the elimina-
tion of private profit from “the trade.” He
has given notice that when the Anglican
General Sjmod meets in Sydney next month
he will move, —
That the policy known as “Disinterested Manage-
ment” deserves the sympathetic study of the Church
throughout Australia as a valuable plank in a con-
structive programme of permanent temperance
reform.
The idea is supposed to have originated in
Gothenburg, Sweden, where the pubs are
run by municipal officials; but Biggs bases
his arguments chiefly on the experience of
two companies in England—the Home
Counties Publichouse Trust and the Re-
formed Public House Association —which
have been applying the principle to pub
ownership for some years. Houses owned
by the cos. are managed by salaried officials,
who make nothing out of the sale of booze,
but are allowed a liberal commission on
light refreshments and meals. Wherever
possible, the “reformed pubs” have gardens;
and every house has a coffee room, apart
from the bar, with good pictures on its walls
instead of whisky advts., and cards, chess
and other games. The dividends are limited
to 5 per cent. Surplus profits, which have
been considerable each year, are put into
improvements and the purchase of new
houses. After studying the results, London
County Council has approved of the idea,
and though its policy is not to permit the
renewal of licenses on properties owned by
it in resumed areas, it has made an excep-
tion in favor of “disinterested management”
houses. Earl Grey, one-time G.-G. of Can-
ada, is on the Board of one of the cos., and
when in Australia a couple of years ago he
gave the glad hand to Biggs and his scheme.

A Billjim artillery man tells us what it
sounds like: —
If all the tramears in Sydney bumped down George-
street ringing their bells; if all the ferries in the
Harbor sang a Hymn of Hate on their sirens; if
all the motor-ears in the State sounded their'multi-

Parliaments of Australia shrieked at the 14 Speakers
and the 14 Speakers simultaneously thundered
“Silence!” it would be an inaudible whisper com-
pared with even a lull in the artillery preparation
when the Great Push began.

There used to be a saying in Arabia: “See
Damascus and die.” I have seen the Royal Art
Society’s Show, and nearly died. The same old
works without intention, the same array of favored
pupils’ works, the same base imitators of better men.
Yet this Society gets £4OO a year from the public
coffers for the encouragement of Art. Art should
be encouraged—but not the Royal Art Society’s
kind. Art does not consist in mere unintelligent
paint-splashing. It should express . \
Watkins portrait, a Lister landscape, a Rubbo war-
picture express nothing but very average minds, and
express them in a very indifferent technique. This
is not the sort of Art to pay £4OO a year for when
there are so many better ways of spending the

in comfort. That, just now, seems more important
than the expression of the souls of a dozen respect-

“Fortieth”: There’s a boy here, a West-
ralian. He has lost two limbs and an eye,
but has fallen in love with Nellie , and
proposes to her every time she goes near
his bed. She shakes her head at him, but
he persists. The other day he showed her
an article in a paper in which it was
pointed out that after the war there would
be very many fewer marriageable men than
women in Australia. “There you are,
nurse,” he said, “two-thirds of a man for
each woman. D—n it all, old girl, you
don’t want more than your share, do you?”
For the severalth time within the last
half-dozen years the N. S. Wales unions
are making an effort to secure a closer alli-
ance. Past effort was barren of immediate
results, although it showed the organisa-
tions that they really desired the thing
they were seeking. The Labor Council was,
to a great extent, the stumbling block. This
Council, which has grown to unwieldy size,
has become a glorified debating society, use-
ful, no doubt, as a body capable of influenc-
ing the unions individually, but lacking real
power. But it is jealous of its glory, and is
aided in the resistance of new ideas by the
ill-feeling between city and country union-
ists. At last a congress of unions took mat-
ters in its own hands and formed a Labor
Federation; and though, owing to the hos-
tility of the outraged Labor Council this
Federation was buried subsequently, the
One Big Union idea grew all the time,
and half a dozen amalgamations have
occurred during the past year. These amal-
gamations fit in with the Federation idea,
which foreshadows the grouping of kindred
industries under councils, from which group-
councils a central executive with real
executive power will be appointed. The
latest conference commenced operations re-
cently; and although it may be some time
before it can secure the endorsement of the
conferring unions, it seems as if the labor
organisations of N. S. Wales have a pro-
spect of being brought into one solid body
very shortly.

NO CENSORSHIP OF PRAYER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Twas Deacon Jones who wrestled well in prayer.
The small bush Bethel groaned its sad applause,
The while his loud petitions filled the air,
Besieging Heaven from grim and whiskered jaws,
lie prayed for Smith, prayed for his erring soul.
Begging for trimming of a lamp gone dim,
For mercy and for grace to make him whole—
But Smith declared the deacon slandered him.
A thousand little Bethels still are loud,
While kneeling grocers square accounts in prayer,
But under spires select a gentler crowd
Kneel before God with better things to wear;
And there the variegated parson pleads
In rounded periods our mortal case,
Shaping his thought to match the forms and creeds
With all the freedom of the holy place.
No dull restraints are placed upon his tongue,
No censorship his earnestness controls;
His eager words on high are hotly flung
Of peace and war and statecraft, deaths and souls.
While, all without, the press in fetters groans,
The free, far citadel of Heaven is stormed
With speech that eddies round its gates and thrones,
And God is excellently well informed.
So are the people sitting in the pews.
None sleep, but all have open ears to hear,
While with his outstretched hands his holy views
On all events the parson-man makes clear.
Give me a pulpit or an altar rail,
A whitewashed surplice or geneva gown!
In loftiest prayer my soul would fain prevail,
Wrestle with might and get the Censor down.
N.S.W,

“O. I. llatem": The world is very busy
on a big job now, but the wowser still finds
time to mind other people’s business. In
Sydney a week or so back a number of men
and women whose special job it is to attend
to other folks’ morals got together and told
one another how a reputable firm of book-
sellers had “a secret chamber” full of the
most terrible literature. The announcement
was sorrowfully made that an emissary of
the Council of Ten had tip-toed all around
the establishment without finding the
Chamber of Horrors. But hope on, dear
brethren! Perhaps the suspected book-
sellers will one day see the absurdity of
keeping a secret chamber that is too secret
to be discoverable and start to advertise the
abomination. Till then — sli-h !

The Stawell (Vic.) Shire Council wasn’t
going to be caught napping, so when it fixed
the salary of the local president in Septem-
ber, 1915, it gave him £5 extra to be spent in
a maffick if peace were declared during his
year of office. As he didn’t get a chance to
maf he has left the egg in the nest ready
for the next man.

“T.C.”: The gentle, homely geniality of
the French people is as soothing balm to the
soul of the Australian who has seen nothing
but the rough and tough side of life on
Gallipoli and in Egypt since he left his
home down South many months ago. Pro-
bably it explains why, to the surprise of
many, Billjim has become serious since he
arrived here in France. Despite all the rot he
has imbibed from the novel and the stage on
the subject, the modesty and sisterly kindli-
ness of the French women have lured him
into a relationship of respect and homely
familiarity. This is the more remarkable
on account of the ruling war circumstances
which have depleted the country of men and
sadly shaken up the traditions of ante-
bellum days. Yet not to a fraction of the
extent that I found it in London. In the
Big Smoke Mrs. Grundy has been mysteri-
ously “done-for” and I fear it will be a
difficult job after the war to recover her
remains for a resurrection. On the train
journey through, which I still remember
with pleasure, we mixed with both peasantry
and town-dwellers, and everywhere met with
the same gentle kindness. Old men and
women rose from their tasks to greet us.
Some old chaps, more earnest than others,
straightened to military attention, and
with their faces flushed and their eyes
glistening, gave us a military salute that

hold more meaning than any ever given on
a parade ground. On one station a young
officer in the delicate light grey uniform of
his regiment had just arrived on leave by
another train. A little white-haired woman
in black was leaning with her face buried
against his chest. His eyes only betrayed
his emotion; while his disengaged arm
gripped round the shoulder of a grizzled
man standing close to him with unnaturally
severe erectness. But as the lad kissed him
on each cheek, the unseeing eye and the
wooden expression on the old fellow re-
vealed the emotion he thought he was
concealing. The promiscuous kissing of
the old couple, which at one time would
have roused us coarser-grained Anglo-
Saxons to scorn, now seemed quite natural;
and the Billjims about me watched the
incident with looks of solemn understanding
in their eyes.

“Billy T.”: Rain held a Melbourne crowd
under the verandah at the corner of Collins
and Swanston streets, and presently the
regard of all was focussed on a
small, hard, tanned man with an
atmosphere of the bush about him,
who, inspired by a pint or two,
was voicing his need. “I wanta
wife!” he said. “That’s all tha’s
marrer with me—l wanta wife.
Bin’ livin’ alone too long, I have.
Man’s li’ble to get rats, livin’ too
long alone —-hundreds of rats. I
wanta wife. I ain’t p’tickler ’bout
looks or age ’slong as she’s a good
sort. Anybody on? What d’yeli
say?” He looked about him. He
turned himself round for inspec-
tion. The crowd was laughing and
passing gibes, hut I noticed one
thin-faced woman of about 38 look-
ing upon the advertiser with seri-
ous interest. Her lips were set,
but her eyes were wide open, and
there was a grey tinge in her face.
I was certain she would if she
dared. “Look me over,” said the
man. “I don’t mind. I ain’t big,
but I’m honest, and I don’ drink
—well, I don’ drink, as a rule. I
wanta wife.” At this stage a
threatening motor accident drew
attention from the earnest seeker,
and presently, when the people
turned to him again he was drifting towards
a Carlton tram. The thin-faced woman was
bv his elbow. A big cheer followed them.
Somebody whistled the Wedding March.

“Y.Z.” : Don the Dago unbosomed himself.
“I lika not mooch the early close-it. Nota
goot for peezness. Make-a the poor man
mooch disgust. I sella the oyst, the feesh by
the saloon. All ees goot, so clean, so fresh,
the oyst, the cray, the grill-a, the flounder
feesh. I make-a mon’ —twent’ the pound-a
one-a wick. Ver’ nice. I smile-a with my-
self. Then the biga the damfool make-a the
early close-it. Poof! Beezness eeseape.
Netting is to do. The oyst go ver’ seek wait-
ing for the-a custom. He do not-a come.
The flounder feesh iss on-a my hand teel I
no more can bear. The early close-it keel
me so dead. I don’ know for why the teetot’
not-a eat feesh. The teetot’ fellah despise
the ovst. He weel not-a be seen walking
with the cray. One-a drink, two-a drink,
tree-a-drink maka the fellah tink well of the
grill flounder feesh. The early close-it make-a
the gran’ sorrow in the belly. I sell not-a
three cray dis-a wick. The whole-a town
eet steenk with the oyst that ees not eat
that neffah weel be eat. Early close-it -
Gar-r! I not-a care who win-a the w*ar.’
“St. Jimmy”: I travelled
to-day with three flappers
just loose from one of the
most expensive ladies’ col-
leges in Melbourne. One
spoke in such a mumble as
to be unpleasant and difficult
to listen to ■ a second dis-
torted her vowel sounds exe-
crably, and the third pitched
her voice in an absurdly
high key. It set me won-
dering whether the culture
of the voice receives any
attention at our secondary
schools, where so many fal-
lals are charged for. Pupils
may with advantage forget
many things they learn at
school; but their speech will
be an evidence for or against
them as long as they live.
In this connection it should
be somebody’s business to
take note of the number of
quacks who profess to teach
“voice production” without
the faintest knowledge of
the vocal organs and with a
similar equipment concern-
ing phonics.
Nothing worries them. An Anzac from
Sydney who had lost a wing heal’d whilst in
a London hospital that an old mate was
just pulling round after leaving his right leg
and half his left arm in the operating
theatre. He hobbled out to the garden to
find the other victim, and stood grinning
before him. “Strike me, Bill!” he chuckled.
“I thought I was lopped a bit; but you
look like a Moreton Bay fig after the Sydney
Council has done with it!”
Nesbit, P.M., in Adelaide, has done a
logical but unusual thing in sending a
Flossie to gaol for two months. The
law of 8. Aus. says that she shall not
exist. Whether it is a wise law or
not, whether it Ims an unforeseen and
widespread bad effect on the community, is
an open point. There the law is, and Nesbit
takes the ground that he is on the Bench
to see it enforced. If the lady persists in
existing, it is usual for the Crown to become
aware of her now and then and inflict a fine.
Nesbit says that this is liable to be taken as
a sort of license-fee. So he prescribes gaol.
It remains to be seen whether he will be
consistent, and do the same thing for Isaac
McShortodds, who is, in South Aus., just as
illegal as Flossie.

“Aladal”: When the Sydney spoilers
start filling up Farm Cove, as the Minister
for Works proposes, could they not cut
away Lady Macquarie’s Chair and continue
the underground railway, as a surface sys-
tem,. to Garden Island? This could then
be flattened and used as the headquarters
of ferry services to Neutral Bay, Cremorne,
Mosman, Manly and Watson’s Bay. Also
it would supply the Government with a
splendid site for a State pub. Norton Grif-
fiths should be communicated with at once.
They might do something at 15 per cent.

“Podargus” : A new and sinister aspect of
the venereal disease question was brought
under my notice recently by a pal home on
leave from one of the Westralian training
camps. A few fools who have contracted
gonorrhoea, and perhaps worse, seem to
regard the possession of complaints of this
nature as a badge of soldierhood, and work
horrible practical “jokes” on clean-living
mates, the sole object being the infection
of the hapless victims "with one or other of
the curses. An introduction to a firing
squad would not be exaggerated treatment
for these dangerous brutes.
“Seven Bells”: Launched in 1854, a
month or two before the Edina, which still
thrashes up and down Port Phillip Bay, the
old iron screw-steamer Stormbird wss until
she recently foundered on the Wanganui
bar (M.L.) the oldest ocean-going steamer
in the world. She first spread her wings at
Whiteinch, Lancashire, and came to Aus-
tralia under canvas and the command of
Daniel McCallum, father of the Rev. Alex-
ander McCallum, of Melbourne Wesley
Church Central Mission. She was a sweet
thing of 217 tons, and before ending her
days in Maoriland waters traded between
Melbourne and the Gippsland ports. Her
skipper lived long at Williamstown, where
lie was marine surveyor and adjuster of
compasses. In those days the Wesley
Church spire was one of the points from
which bearings were taken, and continued
as such till the Exhibition dome was built.

A CHANT OF ENVY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
N.S.W.
Sylvia has golden hair
(Golden since a year or two) :
She is slim and very fair
Standing in her 4.0 shoe.
And her lips are baby red
(Shade that's 10/6 a pot) ;
Clearly put and briefly said,
She is all that I am not.
Sylvia is good to squeeze,
Greedy is her mouth to kiss,
She is lightsome to the knees,
All the men agree to this.
In my eyes brown devils gleam,
Laugh and dance —they’ve got the pull:
Fair as some drunk poet’s dream,
Sylvia is deadly dull.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Pish to all this talk about Hughes’s habit
of eating one stale biscuit and glass of water
for breakfast, a mashed peanut and table-
spoonful of whey for lunch and a dish of
pap for dinner! When he is thinking how
to get out of his latest tight corner he eats
lightly, of course. So does every other sen-
sible person. But life isn’t all brain-work
for Billy, and he can overtake a grilled whit-
ing or a bit of fillet with the best of ’em.
But because the public, doesn’t see him
sloshing mixed drinks and chasing the Ched-
dar around the table for another scoop, he
is generally accused of doing his work on
a diet which couldn’t sustain a chicken.
GEORGE TALLIS AND HUGH J. WARD
(managing directors of J. G. Williamson
Ltd.).
Arthur Seaforth Blackburn, one of five
Australians who have just won the V.C. in
France, was born 25 years ago at Woodville,
near Adelaide, where his father, now
deceased, was Anglican vicar. Dad was a
scientist as well as a parson, and was the
leading Australian authority on beetles.
Young Blackburn was schooled at St. Peter’s,
and is a barrister. He was in the Landing
as a private, went right through the cam-
paign without wounds or sickness, and won
his star before the evacuation. His three
brothers —one of them a topnotch Sydney
physician—are also in khaki.
H. J. Driscoll, just dead in Adelaide,
was a prosaic collector of rates by day;
but in the evenings his thoughts would go
back to early years up-country, and then
he wrote novels, calling himself “Giles
Seagrim.” He had not much sense of
style; but the books got a value from the
first-hand knowledge they held. “Jack
Halliday, Stockman” was about the best of
them.

An illustration of the British shipowner's
patriotism and liberality. A wealthy Ran-
goon merchant gave to the British Govern-
ment as his contribution to the war funds
the free use of two 5000-ton steamers for
“as long as the war lasts.” One of these
vessels recently arrived at Sydney laden
with sick and wounded Australian soldiers.
The patriotic British shipowner’s name is
Lim Chin Tsong.
Lieut. Bill Denny, the only member of
the S. Aus. Parliament on service, writes
that it is a great life. “We are having
delightful weather, and everyone is in
great heart. Personally I am feeling very
fit. Taking it all in all, I never felt better.
The life, the surroundings, the climate are
delightful. But tell the friends of soldiers
that later on the need for comforts will be
much greater than in Egypt, where the
climate was very mild. Woollen goods will
be most acceptable.”
To relieve Hughes, who apparently is
going to stick to the job of Attorney General,
R. R. Garran is to be Solicitor-General.
Garran. who took an active though non-
political part in the creation of the
Commonwealth, is the finest type of public
servant, and has done excellent work as per-
manent head of the Attorney-General’s
Department. He is the son of Dr. Garran,
a former editor of the S. M. Herald.
Old ’Enry Parkes was a great leg-puller.
When he lived at Parramatta he was sighted
one morning by the station-master just as the
s.m. was about to give the “right away” sig-
nal to a hissing train. The great old man
was then on the overhead bridge, so the
official wirelessed the driver to wait. Parkes
saw it all out of the corner of his eye and
deliberately slowed up. As he came down
the steps, heads of fretting suburbanites
pepped out of the carriage windows all along
the train and the railway men fidgeted. But
Parkes didn’t hurry. And just as they were
expecting him to step aboard and release the
waiting universe he turned away to the
bookstall, bought a paper—and started up
the steps again!
Died suddenly, at 61, Ernest Hanson, one
of the best known Treasury Inspectors in
N. S. Wales. For many years in the Audit
Dept.—from junior to Deputy-Auditor-Gen-
eral—he was a familiar figure in every town
in N. S. Wales where a public servant jingles
public money. His most famous “case” was
in connection with the Charity Department,
which he pulled down about the ears of the
Public Service Board. As a result Hanson
became Director of Charities. But only for
a time; the job did not suit his temperament.
V. G. Childe, who was given a travelling
scholarship for classics by Sydney Uni. on
finishing his B.A. course with honors a year
or two ago, has just been having his head
patted by 1 (xford examiners. A thesis on
"The Influence of Indo-Europeans in
Greece” has been rewarded by permission to
tack “B. Litt.” after his name. Childe tried
to get a free passage to Europe in a khaki
suit, but was turned down for physical rea-
sons. Ilis father, now retired, looked after
the Anglican collection-plate for many years
at St. Thomas’s, North Sydney.
Alfred Pfaff, convicted in Melbourne of
attempting to import German goods, had
been trading for 11 years as the Australian
Produce Co. Prior to the war his chief
Australian producer, seemingly, was Arthur
ITermsdorf, of Leipzig. After the war he
did business with the same old Hermsdorf
per Erdman and Hethe.v, of Amsterdam,
who put him into communication with a
Swedish firm, which “Swedish” firm wrote
to him from Leipzig. It all gives Australia
a hint of the trouble it is going to have
if it attempts to shut out “enemy” goods
after the war while letting other" people’s
goods in.
Lionel Curtis, founder and editor of the
Round Table, who is talking Imperial Fed-
eration in Australia, was born in England 45
years ago, and after getting an M.A. degree
at Oxford went to S’Africa in the late ’9o’s.
He was in a Natal regiment during the un-
pleasantness with Oom Paul, and while the
Transvaal was under martial law he was
given the job of organising a municipal
system for Jo’burg. Later on he had
a hand in framing the Constitution of
the S’African Union, and is now out
J. S. WATKINS, artist, putting a new
pupil through.
to save the Empiah from disintegration.
He sees only two goals to which events
can _ carry the ball. If citizens of Aus-
tralia, Canada and the other “dominions” are
to be dragged into war and back again to
peace without any control over Ministers at
London, he prophesies that sooner or later a
war will come in which one or other of the
“dominions” will declare its neutrality—
and that will mean its independence and the
collapse of the Empire. To avoid this, he
and his Round Table “groups” advocate a
system by which Great Britain is to become
a “dominion,” with a Ministry confined to
the conduct of purely domestic affairs, Im-
perial matters being managed by Imperial
Ministers answerable to voters in Australia,
Maoriland and elsewhere as well as those in
the Cold Country.
Fine, human old George Dibbs could do
blacksmithing as well as electioneering, and
had a forge at his Emu Plains home. This
forge overlooked the Nepean River, on which
he kept a boat. One day a boy and girl came
wandering by. and the boy looked longingly
at the idle skiff. The blacksmith was the
only person visible, and to him the lover
applied: “I say, d’you know who owns that
boat?” The smith stopped work to reply:
“Belongs to Mr. Dibbs,” nodding in the direc-
tion of the house. “Um! D’you think he’ll
want it this afternoon?” The smith looked
thoughtful. “You might chance it,” he said,
and resumed work. The boy nodded his
thanks, led his girl down to the boat and
was away two happy hours. When he came
back and tied up he climbed to the forge,
winked at the blacksmith and passed him
half a crown. Dibbs winked to himself and
went on working.
The Melbourne Museum lias missed a
chance of buying a sword which once be-
longed to Deeming, the gentleman who made
a hobby of burying wives under the hearth-
stone. After Deeming was hanged the
blade was sold with his other goods and
chattels and was bought by George Verdon.
who satisfied himself that it had once
belonged to a Crusader. At present it
belongs to Fritz Wallach. the German who
had the law suit over his internment. This
fact spoiled the sale, for when the trustees
heard that the thing was German-owned
they dropped the idea of buying it for fear
Hughes should set his bloodhounds on them.
The theological students of Trinity Col-
lege, Melbourne, reckon that spiritual
weapons are not much needed just now. and
all but three of them have gone to the Front.
Of the 941 men students enrolled at the
college since its foundation over 300 are
either dead or past military age and over
100 are_in parsons’ rig. Just about half the
rest, 270 altogether, have gone on active
service.
“T. 0.1.”: At least three Australian
cricketers who met the first All England
XI in 1862, are still about —George Moore,
of Maitland, at 96; Fowler, aged between
70 and 80; and Thomas Whiteside, of
Hobart, who is about 80.
Lately-dead Viscountess Caroline Sher-
brooke, the second wife of “Bobby” Lowe,
who was in Australian politics from 1842 to
1850, and then became British Chancellor
of the Exchequer, left an estate in N. S.
Wales valued at £22,874. According to
popular fiction she was the daughter of
Mrs. Jamieson, who was murdered by John
Knatchbuil in 1844. But there is no truth
in that story. In the “Life” of Robert Lowe,
Miss Jamieson is described by Lowe himself
as having married a respectable mechanic,
a furnished freehold house being the wed-
ding gift of the Lowes.
A corporal just reported missing at the
Front carried a lot of Australian history
under his cap. His name is Hassall Mars-
den Harding Kendall. Rowland Hassall
Y\'as one of the first missionaries to Tahiti,
and settled in Sydney when driven off the
islands by the natives. Samuel Marsden
(whose daughter married a Hassall) was
the second Anglican parson in N. S. Wales,
and the first missionary to the Maoris.
Kendall was one of three who accompanied
Marsden to Maoriland as missionary, school-
master and magistrate. He was the grand-
father of poet Henry Kendall.
One of the plans adopted by Captain Cook
for reminding Black Brother of his visit to
Adventure Bay (Tas.) in 1777 was the dis-
tribution of medals. The Tasmanian black-
fellow is only a distant memory now, but
one of his medals was turned up the
day near Variety Bay, a dozen miles north
of Cook’s landing place. It was found
several inches below the surface at what
used to be an old camping ground. Black
Brother, finding that keeping it didn’t im-
prove it as tucker, apparently made sure and
buried the uncanny thing.
Some humorist in the rag trade lately
worked off a wicked one on a Sydney poli-
tical warehouseman who never carried a big
stock of German goods even in the days
before the war. A small advertisement
appeared in a confiding morning paper
announcing that David Topfloor, Ltd., wdliicl
that day distribute free to all comets its
stocks of enemy-made goods bought before
the war. Tramloads of women were caught
by the unauthorised bait and David Top-
floor, Ltd., had a busy morning explaining
that it knew nothing about the dashed ad.
and would not refund tran; fares. You can
catch some greedy old fowls any time with
a handful of chaff.
MARSHALL LYLE, of Melbourne, who,
twenty years after, is still “the lawyer
who defended Deeming.”
“D.F.”: The name of the bushranger who
used to be Martin Cash’s side-kicker ,(B.
7/9/T6) was Laurence Ivavanagh. In his
autobiog. Cash, who freely admits that he
himself was a high-souled and chivalrous
party, laments that Ivavanagh and his other
mate, Jones, sometimes forgot to behave
like perfect gentlemen. They were both
justly punished by coming to a sudden end.
while Martin, after a spell at Norfolk
Island, lived to a green old age on his little
farm and died in the odor of currant-bun
respectability.
TO THE MOTHER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
lie was the handsome casket of the dreams
That bloom in youth, when earth is all in spring,
And Life, where Hope with splendid promise gleams,
Puts hues of gold and green on everything.
And now he sleeps. Though hearts may be as lead,
Let us not say those dreams of ours are dead !
Ilis rounded limbs, his young heart, seemed to hold
Tender remembrance of a hundred hours —-
Reminders sweet of ecstasies of old,
Treasured away like those dear, holy flow’rs !
That lovers’ fingers pluck for loving gifts
To anchor memory before it drifts.
The rounded limbs are still, the young joy slain
That laughed to live as sunshine in the day.
Yet he has lived ; and he shall live again—
When time the hungry ache shall sweep away
With which the heart in present loss is pained—
Joyous and care-free, happy and unstained!
See how the grass grows freshly from the ground
That pain has turned to bury all the past!
The dew of sorrow falls; and, then, around,
The rays of suns of newer days are cast;
And lo! the grass of hopes renewed shows green,
And flow'rs of memory make sweet the scene.
The child has lived. And, as the heartache goes
The thought of his brief days in sunshine spent,
Those fair, fond memories the heart still knows
Of former dreams, will give the breast content.
The child still lives, though strange his silence
seems,
He is the casket of those longer dreams!
N.S.W.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
N. S. Wales Miners’ Accident Relief
Fund. —This splendid insurance fund,
established in 1901, is maintained by a
fortnightly contribution of ninepence which
is deducted by the employer from the fort-
nightly wages of all employees, supple-
mented by an equal amount paid half by
the State and half by the employers. The
ninepence deducted from wages—tip 4i!w r
makes- the contribution compulsory—r|
paid to local committees, which apply* it as.
far as it will go towards meeting claims ;
and it is part of the committee’s duty to
make inquiries and protect the fund from
malingerers. The employers’ and State
contributions are paid to a central execu-
tive, which invests this money and the local
committees’ surpluses, and exercises a
general supervision. This central fund is
of course also drawn upon when the local
contributions do not meet the claims.
Pensions are paid to miners incapacitated
through accident; in case of fatal accident
the widow gets a funeral benefit and a
pension, and the dependent children are
supported.
For the first few years there was of
course a large excess of revenue over
expenditure, and in spite of the earnest
warnings of this paper numerous conces-
sions were made to beneficiaries. That
they were too great has now been suggested
b\ the fact that though in two years revenue
has shrunk just upon 20 p.c.. the outgoings
have actually increased. This shows how
figures have moved :
Revenue. Exnenditure.
1901-5 .. £41,956 £14,564
1906-10 . . 58,661 34,137
1911 . . 63,856 43,390
1912 . . 67,005 45.838
1913 . . 71,900 52,041
1914 .. 66,804 55,426
1915 . . 57,936 52,947
The shrinkage of revenue last' year was due
partly to numerous strikes; but though
that meant a reduction of new accidents it
didn’t reduce the pension and other out-
'goings on account of previous accidents.
This supplies a reason for maintaining a
large reserve: it is extremely likely that
mining—apart from coal-mining—in N. S.
\\ ales may he seriously depressed after the
war, and that means smaller contributions,
Without a corresponding reduction in the
outgoings. On the latter point it is signi-
ficant that, though the average number of
employees in 1913 was 31.272 and in 1915
only 23,477. yet the outgoings for 1915
were actually greater than for 1913. Take
the case of the Great Cobar groun of mines,
for example. Last year they couldn’t make
a do of it and were mostly shut down. Yet,
though miners’ contributions were in conse-
quence reduced to £55, former accidents
called for an expenditure totalling £lO5O.
And two or three Wyalong mines, which
are now right out of the list of contributors,
are still costing about £5OO a year.
The chief of the present benefits are. in
case of fatal accident. 10s. per week to the
widow or other dependant and ss. to each
child under 14. with £l2 funeral allowance;
in case of disablement. 15s. per week, with
ss. per child under 14. John Trivett, the
Government actuary, has lately made a
valuation of the Fund’s liabilities, and,
though it has £330,000 lent to the Govern-
ment at 34 to 44 p.c. and £0289 in cash,
the officer reports :
(1) That as at April 30, 1915, the assets of the
fund were practically just suffhient to provide the
liabilities in respect of beneficiaries then existing.
(2) That the increase in the proportional number
of casualties, together with the increase in benefits,
makes it necessary to increase the contributions of
miners by 20 p.c., or (say) Id. per week, to provide
the benefits in respect of future accidents.
In other words, the politician intruded, and
another insurance or superannuation fund
has just started on the road to bankruptcy.
Whether it will be pulled up hard and
turned the right way remains to be seen.
Mostly owing to the fact that the law
has in the matter of this fund enjoined com-
pulsory service and that the contributions
are collected without cost by the employers,
and partly owing to the fact that the Mines
Department stands some of the office
charges, the expense rate is astonishingly
low. Last year the receipts were £57.93(5.
and outgoings exclusive of allowances to
beneficiaries totalled £lOl7 =£2 15s. lOd.
p.c. of income. The Bulletin knows no
other large fund, covering so wide an area,
which is so cheaply administered.

Things have improved steadily in tiie last
few years, and war hasn’t stopped the
giess. But that is quite natural. Very
nearly half of the funds which the bank
, handles belong to itself: that is to say, on
3)alf of its risks the 1 p.c. or so extra
jpwhich it is now getting is almost all
profit: the only set-oil is a bit of extra
iriCome tax and a few pounds more spent
i;n salaries, etc. Before paying the current
dividend the Ballarat Bank had £179,103 of
its- own and it owed £233,447. Or put it
this way: the bank owes £233,447 and has
£IO4,SOS of cash and liquid assets. Thus
it owes £125,639 for which it hasn’t cash
or liquid assets ; and it lias £307,742 worth
of other assets to meet that liability. So
the depositor who worries over a deposit
in this bank had better see a doctor about
his nerves.
The shareholder hasn’t got much to worry
about either; on the other hand, he pro-
bably hasn’t any golden time to look for-
ward to. The present certainly isn’t a
golden time even though the dividend is
9 p.c.; for the total profits don’t average
5J p.c. on what the shareholders are sup-
posed to own. A while ago a few share-
holders reckoned they could do better than
that with the money if they had it, and
they wanted the bank to sell out. It does
look a pretty wretched return, seeing that
the bank iiv-order to earn even that much
is taking the risk of investing about as
much of other people’s money as it has of
its ow T n; but this is quite a common com-
plaint among Australian banks. Take
Australia’s biggest bank, the Wales. It
doesn’t earn more than 7 p.c. on its capital
and reserves; and to do that much it has to
take the risk of handling over £50,000,000
of other people’s money, most of which it
has to invest, guaranteeing not only interest
but the full return of capital. When you
consider what profit a rag-store with a
decent goodwill makes on its capital. 7 p.c.
looks pretty miserable for a great bank
with an enormous goodwill. And one can’t
reasonably suggest that the return is small
because banking risks are negligible, seeing
that at least half of the banks of Australia
still wear shuddersome scars. There are
two possible explanations of the poor earn-
ings: one that the banks don’t do things
as vigorously as they should; the other
that they don’t “do” customers as vigorously
as they might if they were greedier.

Profits are shown net; that is, after an
amount has been appropriated to cover de-
preciation not otherwise provided for.
Lately the appropriation has been £5OOO per
half-year. But this is probably a contin-
gency fund, because the directors - generally
announce that all expenditure in connection
with the maintenance of plant “in a high
state of efficiency” is defrayed out of
revenue. It is highly probable, therefore,
that the table puts the net profits at none
too fat a figure. On the other hand, one
has to remember that a large part of the
capital is represented by freehold and lease-
hold properties; and there is nothing to show
whether fair allowance has been made for
the depreciation in value caused by six
o’clock and other legislation.
However, the thing that will most interest
shareholders is the manner in which profits
are being maintained or otherwise; for if an
asset produces £lOO a year for the cb. and
will go on doing it, there are not many share-
holders who will worry whether it is valued
at £lOOO or £3OOO. So far there isn’t any
sign of a serious cut in the profits; but,
then, whatever effect six o’clock closing will
haxfe wouldn’t be exhibited in this last half-
ye'ar’s figures. The breweries are, of course,
"‘struggling to'supply something that will take
the place of the alcoholic brew whose sale
is now stopped after six; but it is unlikely
that that will give them any extra money.
The general tendency of legislation will be
to reduce brewery profits. But that very
legislation will keep competition out of the
field, and more or less assure the present
three cos. a monopoly or thereabouts of the
trade of Sydney. It has been so for the last
15 or 20 years. There are no more brew-
eries in Sydney (and fewer in N. S. Wales)
now than there were when this co. was
formed early in 1902. Yet the population
of Sydney at that time was 485,000,
whereas now it is 780;OO0. Some of the
effect of this is shown in a table covering
omy five years :

Gross profits in brewings and sales of
aerated waters and spirits, etc., have thus
increased from £52,291 to £90,432; and
though there may have been some small
changes in bookkeeping during the period to
account for a little of this, the growth of
other figures—stock, for instance, and bbok
debts—indicates a remarkable increase of
business. But don’t expect this to continue.
Holman, Premier and Treasurer of N. S.
Wales, has just owned up to a loan expendi-
ture of £41,000,000 during the past six
years; and on top of that there has been a
Federal expenditure which would have been
regarded as quite incredible a few years
ago. This has meant a perfect blaze of
bogus prosperity; and the brewer is very
liable to get an extra-big cut out of such
unhealthy conditions. The awkward part
of the business is that the wretched slate
has to be cleaned afterwards.

Shareholders can’t reasonably growl at those
figures for the year, especially as provision
has been made out of the profits for income
and other taxes. And see how these items
have moved: 1914, £1010; 1915, £1125 ;
1916, £2115. The idea behind all this extra
taxation is that each citizen shall contribute
his little bit —that his net profit sliall be so
much less. That’s the theory of it. But
in practice we find scarcely a concern that
hasn’t managed to make good the extra
taxation in some way; that is to say, they
have practically all “passed it on.” New,
who is it passed on to? In other words,
who does the paying in the end?
In ordinary circumstances when costs are
pushed up the people who suffer are the
primary producers—the men whose market
is foreign; who can’t pass it on. At pre-
sent, however, the position is extraordinary.
Some at least of the primary producers are
able to pass it on: wool and copper, for
instance, are at prices which would a few
years ago have been considered fabulous.
So it becomes necessary to inquire why
the foreign buyer is giving these abnormal
prices; and it transpires that he is passing
it on—he is paying with lOIJ’s and I’N’s.
He is passing it on to To-morrow. And
To-morrow is going to be cold and hard in
consequence. If we still had all this money
or the goods the money is exchanged for it
would be chiefly a matter of collecting later
on the money we are borrowing and re-
distributing now. But the position is by
no means as easy as that. First we are
creating money, which we are giving to
men in exchange for materials and labor,
and we are destroying those materials and
that labor. So we are left without the real
wealth —the materials and the food, etc.,
which the laborers consumed —and all we
have is the paper, which we have to con-
vert into coin later on. Thus, though it
might still be possible to collect taxes from
the holders of the paper, our actual wealth
is being diminished. And, further than
that, we are pouring money into foreign
countries for munitions, etc., and that
money is beyond our power of taxation.
All this hasn’t more to do with the Mutual
Store than with most other cos. But it
sunnlies reasons why this paper believes
that the nresent prosperity is wholly fieti-
tious and that there is a cold time ahead —
just how cold will depend upon whether the
seasons help us and we buckle to and
produce vigorously.
The Mutual Store, ■Ltd., has a capital of
£125.000, and on July 31 it owed £63,528,
including £40,000 on mortgage. But let’s
lay it out on a side-table: —
1911. 1915. 1916.
Liabilities .. £67,340 £59,330 £63,529
Assets:—■
Property . . 103,625 103,625 103,625
Plant, etc... 12,793 12,107 11,435
Stock . . 73,017 79,545 83,719
Cash, etc. . . 31,035 30,333 34,262
In five years the co. has thus reduced its
debts by nearly £4OOO, while it has added
nearly £14,000 to stock, cash and book
debts. And meantime it has paid a regular
10 p.c. dividend. The past is all right; and
the co. is in a good deal better position to
meet a dull future than it would have been
if the tide had turned five years ago.

This is the Broken Hill co. that does things
differently. The. other cos. have each a secre-
tary or legal manager to run the co.’s office,
and a general manager whose headquarters
are at the mine or the works. The head-
quarters of the general manager of the
North are at the co.’s office in Melbourne;
and there is no secretary. The Baillieus are
the big dogs in the concern; and apparently
the Baillieus’ idea is to have the boss along-
side them —or to be alongside the boss. Also,
the system of book-keeping is different: and
that has to be remembered when comparing
the North’s- figures with those of, say, the
South. Other cos. reduce their profits by an
amount sufficient in their opinion to cover
depreciation of plant. This co.’s mine and
mill plant remains in the balance-sheet at
cost, and on the other side there is a reserve
for depreciation—an insufficient reserve, it
seems to this paper. So, assuming metals on
hand, etc., have been correctly valued, the
true profit for the past half-year was £142,01)2
less whatever was a fair thing to write off
against wear and tear and depreciation. Pro-
bably £22,002 was a fair thing. Provision
was made for income and land taxes, but not
for war-time profit tax. Yet if the co. doesn't
have to pay a stiff war-profit tax it will be
very well treated, and other people will be
very badly treated. It may surely be laid
down that no citizen should be permitted to
make one penny out of the war. Yet what
are the facts? In the past six months this
co. made a not profit of, say. £120,000. It
did this out of 70,200 tons of ore. That is
equal to £1 14s. 2d. per ton. Now the aver-
age profit for many years previous to the war
was not more than £1 per ton. The
Bulletin showed lately that the B. 11.
South, a very similar proposition, had aver-
aged in dividends 11s. Id. per ton for the
past 71 years. Be absurdly liberal and give
this co. £1 as normal profit, and there is
£49,700 directly due to the war.
But. unfortunately, the matter isn’t dis-
posed of so easily as that. It would obvi-
ously be unjust to collar the war profits of a
co. this half-year and let it stand any war
losses that might be made in another half-
year. In the beginning of the war the co.
showed poorer results than it would if there
had been no war. The co. may be entitled to
a set-off on that account. But that doesn’t
altogether get rid of the trouble either. It is
highly probable that after the war things are
going to be pretty bad for these metal pro-
ducers. They have for many years made a
heap of money out of zinc, sold mostly as con-
centrates to German buyers. It is absolutely
necessary for the safety of the nation to cut
those German buyers out. The world can-
not permit the German to get control of the
metal trade again. But let us distinctly
understand that, in spite of all the “solu-
tions” that have been offered, this is likely to
cost us —and especially the producers of zinc
concentrates—a lot of money. Reluctantly
The Bulletin is compelled to say that it
can see no prospect of anybody else being
able to pay us as much as—for their own
purposes, no doubt —the Germans paid us. So
these cos. are going to make war-losses as
well as war-profits. We don’t know what
those losses are going to be. We don’t even

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total, 7s.' 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 2 s. sd.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers. —Advt. [2
