In each half-year £120,000 has been paid in
divs.; and still the co. is approximately
£70,000 better off than it was 12 months ago,
less depreciation not covered by plant and
additions, renewals, etc. These have been very
small in the past 12 months. Hence probably
£35,000 must be cut out of that £70,000 as a
set-off against wear and tear—in other words,
as a plant-renewal fund. The £120,000 paid
in the half-year brings the dividends since the
end of 1912 to £600,000 = 100 p.c. on the
watered capital.
No estimate of ore-reserves is made this
half-year. Six months ago there were, it was
believed, 3,000,000 tons above the 1400 ft.
level : and there was a fine body of ore going
down there. As stated above, the South in
74 years has averaged 11s. Id. per ton of ore
—dividends paid. At 12s. per ton this co.’s
3,000,000 tons would be worth £1.800,000 in
profit. Add to that the £393,155 of liquid
assets in excess of liabilities. There are
600,000 shares. But the co. owes £200,000
raised on debentures to buy an interest in the
Broken Hill Prop.’s smelters; and this has
to be paid off by sinking fund.
“Metallurgist” on Hughes’s “solution” of
the metal problem by handing the small pro-
ducers over to a Fat monopoly:—
The legitimate charges and deductions to be made
by a smelter against the ore supplier are as follows:
(1) A charge for smelting the ore, depending on
the tonnage of the ore and influenced by the com-
position.
(2) A charge for refining and marketing the valu-
able constituents of the ore, depending on the quan-
tity of these.constituents, i.e., on the assay.
(3) A charge to cover inevitable smelting losses of
the valuable constituents.
(4) A charge for the smelters’ profit and deprecia-
tion of plant.
Tlie tariff of monopoly created by Mr. Hughes
does not show all' these in detail, but the following
may be deduced: —■
Smelting charge.—On self-fluxing ores this works
out at approximately £1 per ton. If the co. cannot
smelt these ores for 12s. per ton its metallurgical
staff is not as efficient as the writer, from personal
experience of some of them, would imagine.
alties,” or extra smelting charges, are exacted for
zinc, lead, arsenic, bismuth, etc., in certain quan-
tities ; but the combined supplies of any customs
smelter have these, on the whole, in harmless pro-
portions only, and the penalties exacted on individu-
ally impure ores would be bunce.
Realisation charve.—This is on a sliding scale,
from about 2s. per unit of cooper to 2s. Bd. per
unit, according to grade of ore. As it costs the
same to realise a ton of copper from whatever grade
of ore it is produced, and as (presumably) the 2s.
figure is not framed with any idea of making a loss,
it is fair to assume that the co. makes an average
profit on this item of 4d. per unit.
Metal losses.—ln the earliest days of “wet” copper
assaying it became the fashion to assume that 1.3 of
the total units of copper in a ton of ore (as deter-
mined by “wet” assay) were not recoverable. Now-
adays recoveries of 95 p.c. of the copper contents
are easily possible on, say, 10 p.c. ores ; and on this
grade the smelter would get 9.5 —8.7 = 0.8 unit of
cooper for which it would not pay. With copper
selling at £lOO this would be worth 16s. per ton of
ore. The deductions on silver and gold (i.e., fine
silver being paid for at standard price and fine gold
at £4 instead of £4 4s. llfd. per oz.) are reason-
able: but the small quantities of sold (anything less
than ldwt. per ton of ore) and silver (less than loz.
per ton of ore) not paid for must be worth, on the
whole, something not inconsiderable; probably 6d.
per ton of ore all round.
Now, as to the suggestion that the copper-smelting
monopoly is buying on standard price and selling on
electrolytic, it certainly buys on standard ; and as it
has lately been paying on well over £lOO per
ton, we are fairly safe in assuming it is not
selling electrolytic at £lOO, but is scoring the full
benefit of the difference in prices.
We may, then, summarise the profit the monopoly
is making on a 10 per cent, ore as follows:
£ s. d.
In smelting charge .. .. . . 8 0
In realisation charge . . .. . . 3 4
In metal deduction . . . . . . 16 0
In small gold and silver not paid for (say) 0
In impurities penalties (say) . . . . 6
In difference in copper price, 9.5 units
at £24 per ton .. .. .. 2 5 7
Total per ton of ore .. .. £3 13 11
Of course even a monopoly is entitled to some profit;
but does not Mr. Hughes consider this rather over the
fence ?

“E.D.”: Westralia’s Minister for Mines
was right when he remonstrated witli Prime
Minister Hughes against compelling mine
owners to unconditional) g send their ore to
Australian smelters. Compel smelting in
Australia if you like, but only on the con-
dition that charges are regulated by the
Government. Also, to make quite sure, the
Government had better be represented in
making the assay.
N. S. Wales gold output for August was
£24,140, which is little more than half the
average monthly value of the yield for 1915.
Ever since the war started costs have been
climbing up. In other words, the war has
reduced the value of gold ; and the proposi-
tion that there can be any “war profits”
from the industry is sA untenable that it
will have to be abandoned. Simply because
gold is the standard of value, there is no
gold mine in the world that wouldn’t have
been more profitable had there been no war.
*- * * *
Occidental, Cobar, is now sending ore
from its crosscut at 740 ft. to the mill. The
lode was struck at 116 ft. from the shaft and
values are apparently about the same as at
the 640 ft. level, which has been drawn on
rather heavily of late to feed the battery.
The output of 623 tons in a fortnight is
small for a low-grade mine, but the stoping
area has been limited by the increase of
mining costs, partly the result of strikes.
It doesn’t look like getting on the dividend
list in a hurry.
On June 30 Ivanhoe (W.A.) calculated
it had 1,036,474 tons of 365. lid. ore in
sight, or 327 tons less and Id. per ton higher
grade than the estimate six months ago.
The reserves are distributed as follows:
East lode 698,462 tons, average 355.; Middle
lede 137.434 tons, average 40s. 4d.; New
lode 166.715 tons, average 365. Bd.; Boul-
der lode 23,863 tons, average 745. lid. Two-
thirds of the mine’s visible wealth is there-
fore in the East lode, which on account of
a big porphyry intrusion has been worth
comparatively little for several levels. The
value of this body in deeper ground is still
doubtful.
An attempt is being made to resurrect the
Golden Butterfly, the show at Tampa, near
Kookynie (W.A.), formerly known as the
Gregory. A weak Adelaide co. acquired it
some years ago, but results did not come up
to expectations, and want of funds drove the
co. into liquidation. Now a new find in the
neighborhood has tempted the co. to have
another flutter.

The Victory at Mt. Clifford, on the Mur-
chison (W.A.), has a remarkable record.
When it was thought that everything worth
having had been extracted a tribute party
made a profit of over £20.000 in 12 months.
The latest return from the claim is about
£3OOO from four tons.

Southern Cross (W.A.) is bowed to the dust
with grief at the prospect of the Corinthian
North shutting down. The local pubs, news-
paper and undertakery depended a good deal
on the operations of this co. for a living.
Proposed operations at Mt. Rankin and the
further-afield Broncho Horseshoe and Allen’s
Find will keep the bailiffs out of the town
for a while and enable Bullant Mclntyre’s
“business as usual” flag to flutter in the
breeze a little longer.
* * * *
Oroya Links (W.A.J shuts down its mill
at the end of the month and intends in
future to rely mostly on tributors for a
living. Only the Eclipse section of the co.’s
extensive holding now has any reserves to
speak of, and they are worth but little in
dividends. In 20 years several millions
sterling have been taken out of the other
blocks, but the co.’s predecessor, the Oroya-
Brown Hill, got almost the wnole of that,
handing over the property only when it saw
there could be no further tvindow dressing
for the market. The Kalgurli will probably
be the next on the Golden Mile to cease
operations on its own account, and the
Perseverance and possibly the Associated
may follow suit not long after. The extrac-
tion of “leavings” from the old levels is all
that keeps them alive.
North Kalgurli (W.A.) did not do as well as
was expected last year owing to the increased
cost of supplies and dearth of suitable labor.
£7931 was extracted by tributors, and other
receipts made the total £8519. Expenditure
was £9257; and after allocating £37 for
income tax there was a deficit of £775. The
co. has about £4OOO invested in the War
Loan and has advanced £7OOO on a contract
for the manufacture of munitions. Refer-
ence was made at the meeting to the possi-
bility of the adjoining Kalgurli or South
Kalgurli taking over the property. The
Board expressed its willingness to sell, but
thought it would be better to hold on until
the co. was in a position to determine the
real value of the mine at depth. One shoot
200 ft. in length on the eastern side of the
main workings was, according to the chair-
man, absolutely unproved, and he said it
“might easily contain 140,000 tons of ore
averaging about 28s. per ton.” But. seeing
that a plant costing between £40,000 and
£50.000 would be required to treat the ore,
it is very doubtful whether that tonnage
would justify the expenditure on plant.
Chairman Marker, of South Kalgurli
(W.A.), calculates that an all-round in-
crease of at least 20 per cent, in the cost of
stores occurred last year, and on that basis
the additional cost to his co. had been about
£lO,OOO, approximately Is. 6d. per ton of
ore treated.
The latest addition to the pay-sheet of
Edna May Central is said to be a new assist-
ant manager—Harry Williams, once of Edna
May, where he was taught that silence is
golden. There has been a calm in the
market since Giblin took charge; but it is
said that joy-bells have been ringing in
Queen-s'treet over a report that he thinks
well of the show. The enthusiasm has not
spread to the market, though.
Ajax, Daylesford (Vic.), has for six
months been shuffling along towards the
Ajax North boundary at No. S level. Now
it has got there it has to admit that the lode
on the way is merely a string of patches.
Still, that is better than nothing. The
battery has been fed from some blocks over-
head in the intermediate as well as from
the stopes of No. 1 and No. 2 rises at No. 9
level. As there isn’t much crush stuff left
at either of these levels, and much less at
No. 7, Phil Lewis will heave a sigh when
he gets to work east and west from the
bottom of the new sink at 1210 ft. Judging
by the sandstone he had to go through in
the last 30ft. of the shaft, he has a tough
job ahead. And not very bright hopes to
spur him on either; -for at Daylesford noth-
ing much has been found below 1000 ft. But
the mine has been such a little wonder that
a final flutter has to be made. All the
capital rounded up for the Ajax was £7BOO.
On that it shovelled out £289.600 of gold
and paid over £lOl,OOO in divs. Oddly
enough for a Victorian co. it has a credit
balance of £lBB5. This, with any small
profit lurking in the stopes, will keep the
pot boiling a bit longer.
If plain speaking has any effect, the
people mining in the Chiltern Valley, Ruther-
glen (Vic.), ought to feel a bit sick over
the dusting chairman Rothwell has given
them. On 17dwt. wash not a penny of
profit is being earned, whereas not long since
14dwt. ground paid divs. Of course, mine
supplies are up, and Justice Powers’s wages
log operates. But allowing for all that,
Rothwell alleges that labor is slowing down
so effectively as to mop up most of the 3dwt.
increase in values. Out of £1,200.000 of gold
won the co. has paid labor £594,000 direct
in wages, and £370,000 indirectly for fuel,
timber and other mine supplies, while
returning shareholders £237,000. With a
fair go the mine might last for years. John
Cock., jun., swears to good gold in a tribu-
tary on the north side of the lead, and with
a co-operative party doing well further up
this gutter his guess of profitable work ahead
is probably right. The pilot drives on the
main gutter west are in scoured ground, and
in the Chiltern gutter this has always meant
an enrichment ahead.
* * * *
Dead in Adelaide, at 90, John Tippett,
who came from Cornwall nearly 70 years
ago to erect the machinery at a South Aus.
copper mine, and did the same for mines
and mills past counting.
* * * *
J. Cameron, M.L.A. (Vic.), sees no
reason why the Speck should have all the
electro-metallurgical schemes, and he has
one of his own for East Gippsland. His
idea is that the Snowy River could generate
anything from 20,000 to 60,000 h.p. of
energy to be used in smelting the iron ore
from the deposits between Nowa Nowa and
the N.S.W. border. He says there are not
only big deposits of iron and manganese but
millions of tons of exceptionally pure lime-
stone and all the makings of a big industry.
* * * *
The Coronet Hill (N.T.) Development
Co. is now called upon to do some more
developing or die. The Commonwealth Gov-
ernment, on the strength of a very favorable
report from the Director of Mines at Dar-
win, has agreed to continue giving £ for £,
but stipulates that the co. must raise more
capital as a guarantee of good faith. The
sum in hand would suffice to keep things
going for not more than three months, so the
management is asking for another £5OOO
from the public. When T. G. Oliver made
his report the drives from the tunnel had
gone only 16ft. each way, north and south.
The mine manager, at latest, reports 100 ft.
of driving and 220 tons of ore at grass,
estimated to average 12 p.e. copper, plus a
few ounces of silver to the ton. The show
looks worth further exploitation, apart from
its possibilities of a surprise. Oliver’s report
credits the property with “two miles of good-
looking lode outcrop, from 4ft. to 12ft. in
width.”
* * # *
“E.D.” : Re Karridale, Burtville (W.A.).
In 1906-7 the proprietors worked the mine
to below 180 ft., both the small and “large”
reef. Water- level at Burtville is from 80ft.
to 100 ft. The claim was worked for all it
is or was worth by a small party of lucky,
silly men. The moment water came in they
were too canny to establish a good pumping
plant. They baled the water with buckets
and billy-cans, and when the liquid got the
bi tter of them they plugged their fairly
large profits in their boots and ran for it.
This is the history of most Westralian rich
patch hunters, except that they all don’t
keep the money in their boots. Often the
surplus cash is invested at the nearest pub
or sly-grog shanty. There may or may not
be plenty of good stuff left at the Ivarridale.
That remains to be proved. Samples assay-
ing 6oz. can be picked out in dozens of places
in Australia, but the stuff has the fault of
good whisky. My verdict is, don’t be too
sure about the property.

A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, September 11, 191 G.
My Dear Arini, —
The Women’s Exhibition was well and
truly opened by the Cullen lady to the half-
crown circle that spins at such affairs. When
the usual Is. tariff came into force crowds
began to pour into the Town Hall. Exhibits
included everything from the right way to
curl your hair to the art of tossing scones
into somebody’s patent stove.
Blind soldiers, bravelv facing the dark
road, demonstrated in tuning pianos and
basket-making. Silkworms writhed under
the chaperonage of Mrs. A. E. Anderson.
The horticultural Miss Brace'had her own
parlor in the basement. A model Horticul-
tural College for women and a miniature
settlement for soldiers were exhibited here.
Mrs. Jocelyne, in one of the Red Cross pens,
presided over pickled onions. In one corner
of the basement chickens made their d§but
out of crackling shells to chirp their in-
cubator-maker's praise. Bush book-club
ladies who send carefully-chosen literature
to Bullocky Bill were near by.
A weak place in the exhibition, which was
organised by the Girls’ Realm Secretary,
Grace Burrows, was the tea-room. The
voluntaries couldn’t be depended on. Hon-
orary waitresses came or stayed away as
they chose. When they didn’t turn up, the
hon. manageress Mrs. Norman (always at
her post) had the anguish of seeing cash
leave the premises with its thirst still
unsatisfied.
The National Council ladies got into con-
ference during the early days of the
Exhibition, barricaded behind placards of
“No Entrance” in the Town Hall vesti-
bule. Mrs. Langer Owen took up
the story of the Red Cross. Mrs.
Francis Anderson, touching play for
man, woman and child, asked, “What are we
going to give the working-man to make up
for the beer he isn’t supposed to drink after
0 p.m.?” No one rushed an answer. The
Tom Hughes lady took the chair at one of
these august discussions. Peter, C.M.G.,
the long thin Board of the Education De-
partment, ventured alone into the lionesses’
parlor. He talked of schoolgirls’ happy
days, and suggested spreading a layer of
domestic-science jam over the three R’s.
Amongst the exhibitors the Commonwealth
Patriotic Association deserves a line. Dr.
Mary Booth is president of this show, which
goes on all the year, with Ruth Beale as hon.
secretary. These conspirators are banded
together to barrack for Australian and (if
there’s no hope of Australian) British in-
dustries. They’re sharpening their tongues
and pens to fight the “made-in-Germany”
saucepans that may sneak in after the war.
Did you ever see an Australian girl tatting
with heavy twine? Two of them were hard
at it in the Town Hall. The huge Otter
trawl-net fit doesn’t catch otter, of course)
is the result of this exercise. It looks like
physical culture with a mile or two of tawny
hemp to play with.
Even the old Soft Rag workers for the
Red Cross are not all angels. Some of the
older dames got their backs up at the tem-
porary move from the Town Hall cellar to
the upper floor. It was only during the
Exhibition, of course. But they weren’t
going to be done out of their rights. They
stalked into the cellar —a tea-room for the
time—sat down in great majesty, and
haughtily refused to be “dislodged.” When
the good-tempered secretary said they were
right welcome to sit there, it broke up the
storm, and one by one the Baggers crept
up to the substitute place of bandages.
O.ur State Governor is an engineer wasted.
Still, even a Governor may find occasional
joys in applied mechanics, and lately he
invented a quick-running, labor-saving spin-
ning machine. I fancy his Ex. seized a
piece of gaspipe and a few odds and ends
and tossed the thing together as a surprise
for the blue-eyed Eldest Daughter. Then
she demonstrated with it at the Women’s
Exhibition. The throng hung over the rail
while the spinster pulled out the yarn in a
long, smooth thread. Behind sat the other
spinning Jennies. In charge of all was an
hon. dragon who rushed to the rail if the
public dared to ask how it worked. It’s
twice as swift as the big mill-wheel pattern
that Marguerite toys with in “Faust,” and it
looks like the homely vitals of a sewing
machine with extras thrown in. His Ex',
stole into the crowd to watch the beloved
thing eating wool and giving yarn.
A recent list of killed and wounded in
France contains the name of Brigadier-
General C. B. Prowse, D.S.O. He was a
brother of our Captain Prowse, R.N., killed
in the Jutland battle. The General’s promo-
tion had been very rapid. But that’s pretty
common nowadays. Amongst the latest
V.C.’s is Acting-Lieut.-Colonel A. Carton de
Wiart, D. 5.0., Dragoon Guards. This time
last year he was a captain. That is how
they go up—and out.
The Automobile Club on Saturday gave
convalescent Billjim a spin round the sub-
urbs. After that there was a party at the
club-house in Phillip-street. Malcolm
McEachern, Annie Perry, a lady Knut from
the National who brought the house down
with “Boys of the Village,” and a few more
did their bit on the platform. Engineer-com-
mander Denison and his wife came along.
He may leave Garden Island any time, as he’s
appointed to Pl.M.A.S.—pooh! I don’t care;
you don’t even want to know that the old
thing is still knocking round. J. J. Rouse
gave the boys a two-minutes’ speech of wel-
come, and secretary Morgan, the Lipmans,
A. L. Blythe and li. Minnett helped to push
the show to a glittering finish. Incidentally,
lady voluntaries passed round pyramids of
refreshments.
That long streak of the Peerage, Earl
Grey,, brought his wife and daughter Sybil
here in March, 1914. Nobody except Ger-
many was thinking of a great war in those
easy-going days. So the three Greys got up
with the magpie to peer into the Jenolan
Caves. After that they went further west.
Lately daughter Sybil has had more thrill-
ing trips. She has been working with the
Anglo-Russian ambulance. And then she
got wounded; while practising hand-grenade
throwing—a new pastime for girls—some-
thing exploded before its time.
Dr. Emma Buckley, who made a name
at the Lister Research Institute, is back
from London. She was badly wanted in her.
old post at Prince Alfred Hospital, though
when the shortage of doctors is less acute
she may go off again to the Lister menagerie.
For the next six months, however, she will
be Chief Bacteriologist at the P.A.H.
Major Harper and his wife have been
over from Melbourne, tasting the Harbor
and sunning themselves at Bowral —all in
a. short fortnight. Henry, the one member
of the Robert Harper family with a mili-
tary training, found the signals against him
on account of a long-ago typhoid bout. So
he has been doing local embarkation work.
The Fred Donkins’ place was the visitors’
camp at Bowral; Mrs. Donkin and Mrs.
Harper were daughters of the late parson
Colin McCulloch, of Queensland.
Did you notice that an Australian sailor,
Rear-Admiral Gaunt, son of the old Vic-
torian County Court Judge, and brother of
Mary the writer, commanded one of the
divisions in the Jutland fight? He has been
mentioned in despatches by Jellicoe. But it
was always his habit to get “mentioned”
wherever he was.
This is a char-
acteristic photo, of
artist Da 11 i 1 o
Rubbo —an Italian
whose technique
was acquired under
the famous painter
Domenico Morelli.
Domenico was then
instructor at the
Royal Academy of
Nanles. After a
severe apprentice-
ship to Art, young
Rubbo broke away
to enter the Italian
Army. Hence the
martial tread of the
artist whose studies
of wrinkled age
may be seen in our
National Gallery. A few years’ soldiering
—then back to the brushes. Rubbo’s black
eyes flash at the suggestion of method in
Art. He is an emotional, rather than a
photographic, artist. A visitor to Paris
before the enemy set its hoof in fair France,
he swings towards the Impressionists. He is
the only Sydney exhibitor in the Royal Art
Society’s show to find inspiration in the
great war, and has painted “The Traitor” in
almost his best manner. Like most other
Australian artists at present, he teaches
night and day for bread and butter. In
spare time, when the soap-boiler is lounging
in tennis flannels, the artist works at his
easel.
Public-school children had their annual
outing on the Sydney Cricket Ground on
Saturday. This year brought a welcome
relief from live bunting. Young Australia
formed, instead, the Victoria Cross. Most
timely, too, for in the afternoon word came
of a new list of Australian boys who wear
the coveted trophy. Five of them, Mother
State’s rep. being one of her wonderful bush-
lads—Private Wm. Jackson, of Gunbar,
aged 18£. no less. And, alas! short of an
arm for life.
The Women’s Battleplane Committee is
also fighting for your shillings. At last
meeting the committee’s seasoned warriors
suggested having cash-collecting tables here
and there. The Attorney-General is to be
approached on tiptoe to give consent. Mrs.
Tom Watson —a joint hon. sec. —is scouring
her suburb for the nimble bob.
Southern Maorilanders regret the news of
Flight-Lieut. Jim Dennistoun’s death.
Plenty of the needful and a love of sport
made his life especially endurable. His
father, Geo. Dennistoun, was an R.N. man
who made a success of sheep-raising in South
Canterbury. His mother was a sister of
Knight William Russell, who used to steer
the “Reform” party. They are a gritty
lot and it was characteristic of the breed
that when war broke out Jim worked his
passage home as a seaman simply to get his
muscle up. A brother George, who is in
the Navy, is battle-cruising in West Africa;
he married pretty Beatrice Pyne, of Christ-
church, before leaving for the war zone.
The French-Aus-
tralian League was
born into a w#r-
tossed world in
Jan., 1915. Every
fortnight since then
it has sent about
3500 garments to
the widows and
orphans of France.
The State-school
kiddies of N. S.
Wales are, to date,
responsible for no
less than 30,000
. garments, made
under the eyes of
their teachers. Mrs.
Joseph Playoust,
whose pleasant face
you see just here,
is president of this League. With a son
of her own at the Front, she bubbles with
enthusiasm over “these dear Australian
children.” Mrs. Playoust’s own young days
were spent at Tourcoing. Her eldest
daughter also opened her eyes there, and a
younger girl was at school in the picturesque
hamlet w T hen war broke out. It was 14
long months before an anxious mother could
get her lamb home to the fold.
Lieut.-Colonel Hutton, one-time aide to
his Ex. Liverpool, of Maoriland, and cousin
of her Ex., is returning to M.L. invalided
and thrice wounded. He brings his youthful
bride, who used to be Marie Rhodes, of
Christchurch. A staff job with the Gover-
nor for a time and afterwards more war is
his present programme.
Thelma Maddrell is to be married on the
23rd. Not to the first young man. That’s
“off” long ago. Venour Nathan has the
luck to really win the Braidwood girl.
Dr. Alexis Maclean, a Sydney ’Varsity
graduate, has just been appointed to a Bris-
bane hospital. But she came from Banana-
land in the first place.
The Spencer Bruntons’ daughter Evelyn
goes to England in the Osterley. She hopes
to eat her Christmas dinner with sister Jean
—now Mrs. Adrian Moreing.
The Spring races at Randwick open on
September 30 this year. Clothes are likely
to be of the early-summer, useful washing
variety. For one thing Government House
won’t be interested. Strictly no races for
the castle in Macquarie-street till the dove
flaps her wings.
There was a queer ferry-load of passengers
across the Harbor the other morning. The
old Zoo in Moore Park had just gone out of
business; and the dromedaries and a few
more of the animals that came out of the
ark were getting another sea-trip, this time
to their new home at Taronga Park. They
haven’t very many neighbors yet. The lions
and the tigers have not yet had a house-
warming—as a matter of fact, their boudoirs
aren’t out of the builders’ hands. But
most of the birds are there, and monkeys—
who provide the comic relief —and a few
seals. The seals got out the other morning
and sampled the goldfish in one of their
ponds. Whether the lion will be able to
get out of his den hasn’t yet been deter-
mined. The idea, of course, is not to have
any cages; and whether the lion stays at
home or gives Mosman some excitement
depends upon whether director Le Souef
has correctly guessed how far King Leo can
jump.
A line from “Rosna,” in Britain:—
Isabel Shiell, of Sydney, who has been helping at
Wandsworth, has been up to London to see the
Queen. The capable and charming Sydneysider
wanted to keep her medal of the Territorial Nursing
Association as a memento, but was sternly told
such a thing never had, could, would or should be
done. Considering her fine work, however, they
relented enough to tell the War Office about it, and
the next thing was our Isabel heard that Queen
Alexandra would receive her at Marlborough House,
no less, and present the medal in person. The
monarchy proved most delightful and capped the
interview with the personal gift of a fur-lined cape.
Ren6 Maxwell, who went to London with
the Quinlan Opera Co., recently won a gold
medal for her singing. It was the best thing
the Guildhall School of Music had to give
away to vocal students.
Lionel Curtis, a tall young white-maned
Englishman with a deep gramophone voice,
talks in the King’s Hall on September 14 on
“The Problem of the British Common-
wealth.” It sounds like a truck of iron-
mongery ; but this John the Baptist, though
tremendously in earnest, can make his
evangel interesting. He seems to have got
involved in what he calls the Imperial pro-
blem when he was at the big job of cleaning-
up after the war in S’Africa. He saw that
the four Colonies would drift apart over the
black-labor question, and that there was a
tremendous danger of a repetition of U.S.A.
experience. In those days he was town
clerk of Johannesburg, and he and his
friends got to work and not only laid the
foundations for the S’African Union, but
prepared the specifications as well. Inci-
dentally the larger question of the relations
of the different parts of the Empire came
up, and Round Table Circles were set up
in England, Canada, Australia and S’Africa
to discuss difficulties and find a way of
overcoming them. That’s evidently what
John the Baptist is going to lecture about.
He has already done a good deal of talking
at the University Club.
Brains are banded together to help the
Australian Mining Corps’ Comforts Fund.
Its amazing professor-soldier David, who
seized his geological tools to slay Goliath,
asks for this help. Sept. 18 is the date
fixed for a concert in aid. The ’Varsity
Engineers, the Royal Society, the Institute
of Surveyors and the Institute of Architects
are amongst the backers.
Hats are smaller and larger than they
were. The tiny ones consist of a fluff of
silk and tulle with the smallest flowers in
the world dotted round their crown. Brims
they have none.
The large lace collars of the principals and
a black velvet for everyone are outstanding
features of “Un Ballo.” Also, Cappelli, as
the Duke, is surrounded by incandescent
glare of brilliants. This necklet is like 10-
pounds’ worth of municipal lighting, and the
effect pierces even the uttermost gloom of the
theatre. Little Miss Yisoni, as the page
Oscar, wore a doublet of turquoise blue with
superlative silk tights over shapely limbs. A
small, trussed canary in her stationary
moments, she warbled sweetly when there
was trouble brewing. Mrs. Amelia de Revers
attended the masked ball in heliotrope drap-
erv with her head in a fancy hood. Behind
a fan the size of half a cartwheel she sang
farewell to the Duke —heavily disguised in a
black overall with red-satin ribbons.
At Los Angeles (Cal.) there’s a Gamut
Club which is given to musical parties. A
few weeks since it feasted Leo Cherniavsky,
and hinted that there might be a matri-
monial engagement between the fiddler and
Maud Allan, of Salome fame. Leo said
neither yea nor nay.
The Stricklands are investigating Moree
mud baths and other local, sights as I write
this. There’s an idea that her Ex. might
be benefited by taking a course of the local
waters.
The flyaway nurses’ bonnet, which the
younger Nightingales generally balance on
their back hair, may yet be altered to some-
thing more useful. The war brought us the
military nurses’ workmanlike outdoor garb,
including a becoming felt hat, with a shady
brim, and military fixings. It’s miles ahead
of the sunstroky affair that perches on the
ordinary hospital trainees’ neck.
The Scotney-White pair had the bad luck
to collide with the opera season; and the
chance given of hearing Italian birds sing
the lilts of their own Verdi didn’t help the
concert warblers. Nevertheless, business
improved for the final effort. Soprano
Evelyn, who runs up and down the scale
like a well-trained thrush, gets this town’s
applause. Her basso-profundo, Howard
White, is a vocal heavyweight who didn't
grip Sydney audiences.
Maoriland is still grinning over a recent
letter from its ex-Gov. Lord Plunkett. IP I
said he had visited the Y.M.C.A. building
on the Anzacs’ Front, and was glad to find
it attracted men from “other undesirable
places.” Jack Virgo, by the way, the
Y.M.C.A.’s general secretary, chasing
through the world from his London head-
quarters, will look in here in a week or
two. He was an Adelaide boy, who started
earning a crust in the warehouse run in
those days by our Mrs. Wattle Day Kettle-
well’s father.
There must be some marble in the build
of poor Nordica’s relatives. They sold
even her clothes by public auction.
Catalogued as thus, “One cerise evening
cloak, one black-jet gown,” etc., they were
knocked down to the highest bidder in
Newark (N.J.) a few weeks ago.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

USE PINE TAR FOR COUGHS AND
COLDS.
A SPECIALIST’S ADVICE.
Chronic coughs, catarrh, throat and
bronchial troubles that resist all ordinary
remedies usually yield at once to the sooth-
ing, healing and strengthening influence of
the medicinal properties contained in tar.
pine and wild cherry. All these properties,
as well as several other valuable remedial
agents, are found in a syrup made by dis-
solving half a pound of sugar in half a pint
of hot water, and then adding 2oz. of ordi-
nary bitrate of tar. Stir thoroughly, pour
into a pint bottle, and when cool it is ready
for use. One or two teaspoonfuls of this
simple and inexpensive cough syrup will
quickly overcome a bad cough, and that
annoying tickling in the throat will disap-
pear almost instantly. Children should take
from a quarter to half of the above quanti-
ties several times a day. The fact that
bitrate of tar is entirely free from opium,
morphine, cocaine, heroin or other poisonous
habit-forming drugs to which scientists and
physicians so strongly object, is doubtless
one of the reasons why its use is becoming
almost universal.— Advt.

Motorists—
Why Tax
Yourselves ?
All Motorists in this Country are already
heavily taxed, yet there are to-day Car
Owners who are purchasing foreign-made
Tyres, unaware of the fact that they are
considerably adding to their existing
taxation and increasing the cost of
their motoring. Every imported
cover coming into this Country has to
pay from 1/6 to 2/- (in the case of foreign
tyres) for every lb. weight of cover,
outside of freight charges, etc. Take a
22 lb. cover —on the higher rate —this
mectns £2 2s. a cover or £8 Bs. for a
set of four. These figures explain
WHY Motorists have to pay a
higher rate for imported tyres as against
the Australian-made “ DUNLOP.”
Naturally this Customs charge is added
to the selling price—and passed on to
the purchaser. It is self-evident, there-
fore, to what an extent the purchaser
of imported Tyres taxes himself.
What for?
In Dunlop
Tyres you get
better quality rubber, greater
thickness and strength in casing,
and the most up-to-date method of
construction. In fact a better all-round
wearing cover, for which you pay a fair
price— not plus pounds that was
never spent in manufacture. “Dunlops”
cost considerably more to make
owing to using a Para rubber compound,
and heavier casing than imported covers
of the same size - YET COST YOU
LESS.
This fact means that it costs
you less per mile to motor on
DUNLOP TYRES

and RUBBER NON-SKID.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, September 11, 1916.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Melba’s matinee was a huge success —like
most of her ventures. Guineas and half-
guineas crowded the stalls and filled the
circle to the limit. The G.-G. and his party
arrived early, and the genial potentate was
caught waving his handkerchief to some
stalled friends just before lights were doused.
Lady Helen sat behind a forest of daffodils,
and. her turban-toque with its perpendicular
feather looked like the head-dress of some
Indian rajah. A khaki aide and a blue-
lapelled sample hovered in attendance. State
Governor Stanley came late into the opposite
box where Captain Conant played big bro-
ther to the three Stanley children. The
elder girl showed a mane of very fair hair,
but young Patricia kept her very best hat on
all the time. Mrs. John Sanderson, in won-
derful black-and-white crinolined clothes, was
in the next box, and Mrs. Woolcott had a
party in another roost. But the poor lady
had to send a deputy-hostess, having
sprained her ankle after sending out the in-
vitations. Mrs. Pearce was one of the in-
vited. She wore Dronze silk fitted out with
the new puffy side-panniers and a cute little
bronze bonnet. The daughters of the old
nobility sold programmes. Little Doris
Blackwood was in simple blue muslin with
an innocent rose in her hat amid women
garbed like so many Queens of Sheba. Roma
Clarke was tightly and slenderly bound in
black ; but she wore Gipsy ear-rings and her
hat was crowned with all the wax flowers
and fruit from under the glass shade in the
front parlor. All the sellers wore gold mime-
masks bound to their arms by paddy-green
ribbon.
Melba, as Marguerite in “Faust,” made
up like an 18 year-old. Her own blue eyes
were assisted by a flaxen wig which draped
her like a wonderful shawl and was all
tangled up with loose straw. It was the last
act, but she did without the angels. The
house howled for her at the finish, refusing
to be silenced by the curtain. At last she
made a little speech of thanks and many
bows. Goossens (Faust) sang in a black-
velvet doublet with his bright-green knees
knocking together. Fred Collier was a hope-
lessly respectable Mephistopheles, who
arrested Faust in a red lit doorway, and
spared him the lift-descent into Hades.
The matinee then got on with its vaude-
ville and comic-opera business. The “High
Jinks” people put on a complete act. Madge
Fabian recited in corn-colored clothes and a
bashed blue hat. Some behind-the-scenes man
appeared and apologised for the omission of
Max Selinsky’s name from the programme.
“He will appear noio!” announced the
stranger, and violinist Max strode on looking
thundery. When he really started work he
played everything he had prepared without
giving the house time to clamor for it. Pro-
bably owing to Melba’s timing the pro-
gramme cut-out at exactly five, o’clock in-
stead of stringing along like most charity
matinees towards dinner-time. The proceeds
go to the Distressed Actors’ Fund.
The Overseas Club gave a party on
Thursday afternoon to meet Mrs. Billy
Hughes. The new clubrooms in Collins-
street are glassed in like a conservatory,
and there will soon be a forest of the potted
palms which are so useful to yawn behind.
Mrs. Hughes appeared above some lovely
furs and lace in a tight little fawn-colored
hat She had not long to stay, as William
v.,3 going on from Sydney to Adelaide and
she wanted to pack him safely into the
Adelaide express. Mrs. Pearce came to the
paitj ai.d went off with Mrs. Hughes. She
looked lih. Lady Jane Grey in a large ruff,
black velvet gow.i and a flat bonnety hat.
The Honnessy dame rustled in. hung with
chinchilla furs. Mrs. Fred Eggleston hurried
in for a few moments. She wore navy blue
and paddy green and reported that her
lawyer-husband was down from Duntroon
on final leave. Mrs. James, the major’s wife,
said “How d’you do?” and “Good-bye,” for
she’s off to be nearer the firing-line. There
was some fine singing and playing going on
behind the teacup clatter and the roar of
gossip.
Last week the Toorak Spowers family said
good-bye again to son Alan, who has got
himself fit after collecting numerous wounds
and a Military Cross. He steamed off by the
Karmala for his second helping of shell-fire.
At last the Albert Miller widow is packing
up and buying tickets for herself and a
grandson—the Clive Miller’s son and heir.
Their destination is England. Arrived there,
she will hand the boy over to mother, dash
round to see her new grandson (Marjorie
“Levey” Miller’s fatherless bairn), and then
go off to visit son-in-law Alan Currie, who is
recovering from wounds in a military hospi-
tal.
The Fred Paine matron, who has been in
England since the first gun spoke, has been
seriously ill in Douglas Shield’s London hos-
pital. Her husband, who had hardly un-
packed his bags after his return to Mel-
bourne, hurried back to England at once. The
invalid is now sitting up and taking nourish-
ment. The widow of lawyer J. L. Purves
is getting well at the same address.
Mrs. Charlie Fairbairn slipped quietly
into Melbourne the other day, looking much
slimmer and younger. This patriotic dame
whilst in England hostessed a flat in which
midshipmen, admirals, privates and generals
used to drink tea together. Eldest son
Osborne Fairbairn made a successful flight
over the enemy’s lines and returned with a
very damaged machine and himself severely
wounded, the result being that his arm is
now enriched with gold plates. Second son,
: Jim, has also joined the airmen and has
just received his wings. Ruddy-haired
daughter Esther is working in Doctor"
Shield’s hospital in Park-lane.
Mrs. James, of the Overseas Club, is off
to England on September 27. Her two sons
are fighting in France, and mother feels she
wants to be as close as possible.
The John Madden dame has< emerged
triumphantly from the operating theatre,
but will not leave her private hospital for
a few weeks yet. John was a very anxious
man for days, and all the Cliveden Mausion-
ites were subdued until the hospital signalled
“All’s well.”
Ashmead Bartlett’s insolvency suggests
that all the lovely clothes that made him
such a vision on the platform may have
been bought on credit. The women in the
audiences could scarcely listen to his lec-
tures : they only wanted to admire the
Family Herald cut of him.
St. Ivilda now has a bachelor mayor—
Henry Florian Barnet. Thrice had the shy
but energetic Hebrew refused the fussy
office; but when General Hughes, who is a
councillor, too, ordered out the ambulance to
have himself carried to the chamber, so
that the fourth appeal might be a full-
strength effort, Barnet surrendered, blushing
vividly.
Yarra Glen and Lilydale amuse them-
selves with their Hunt Club races this
week-end. Neighbor Melba will be the chief
decoration.
Lieut. Richard Fitz Gerald went out last
week in France. Every Melbourne theatre-
goer knew this soldier by his familiar stage
name of “Max Clifton.” He played first
with J.C.W. companies and afterwards took
handsome hero parts at the King’s for Bill
Anderson.
Family-solicitor Rupert Wilks, whose
beautiful middle name is do Courcy, has
once more accepted the thankless task of
mayoring Malvern. Thankless indeed when it
is considered that, though State Government
House stands within a stone’s-throw of the
council chamber, all that the municipality
e\er sees of their Hexes is when they
arrive (civic welcome on the city hall
steps, address and bokay) and depart
(civic farewell and bokay). As for the
pallid Rupert’s job, though he is no
speaker, he manages to do the usual flag-
waving at social and political gatherings
without visible distress, while Mrs. Mayoress
is a pleasant, grey-haired body with a nice
taste in things for the cupboard.
Like 50,000 other women whose names are
never mentioned, Mrs. Pearce, the Minis-
ter’s wife, is very busy these days. Her
“bit” eons'sts of visiting military hospitals
and rest-1 omes. Her latest interest is the
new home for consumptive soldiers at
Mac Leod —a beautiful spot in the hills
be.voud Heidelberg. There the inmates have
every comfort; the institution flows with
milk, honey and medicine, and hens oblig-
ingly lay their eggs in haystacks near the
back door.
Mrs. Arthui Kennedy, of Oakwood Park,
Dandenong, has the felicity of calling the
cable a what-d’you-mean. It reported the
death of her A.I.F. captain some time ago.
Since the announcement, however, Mrs.
Kennedy has had a letter from her husband,
who wasn’t killed in the slightest—only
wounded in the foot and taken off by the
Huns.
Sister Adelaide’s weekly confession:—
The latest money-making scheme, with the Red
Cross and Mine Sweepers’ Fund as its prot&gt-es, and
Miss Edith Young and Miss Effie Ferguson as its
organiser?, is “a sale of children’s clothes” at
Rut liven Mansions. It was more than its title
suggested. There were sweets and cakes and flowers
and flower baskets, nursery pictures and home-made
toys, and fancy boxes and silk bags indescribable.
And exquisite stitchery, of course. Miss d’Erlanger
was well enough again to make it possible for Lady
Galway to leave Government House and be at the
opening ceremony, and for the two days that the sale
was open business was brisk.
Talking of funds reminds me that the Caf6
Chantant harvested nearly £7OO for the French Red
Cross, of which only £ 40-odd must come off for
exes.
Saturday’s return to Morphettville for the races
produced an early rash of spring fashions—very
rash, some of them —and a few fat dividends. In
the Hurdle Race 11. P. McLachlan’s Islam gave his
backers £59 14s. for £l. There was a dog show,
too, that day and two deputations and a police
court and no end of 'other allurements.
The Horwoods of Broken Hjll have had news that
their son, who left here in May, 1915, and has since
been in Gallipoli and France, has won a military
medal. It was only last week they heard he had
got his commission—on his twentieth birthday.
From England comes news of the engagement of
Emily Reynell to Lieut. J. W. Walters, King's Shrop-
shire Light Infantry. The marriage is to be soon. Miss
Reynell, who was in England when war began, has
been busy with the V.A.D.’s ever since. Her
younger brother, a doctor and one-time Rhodes
Scholar, who has been toiling behind the lines since
France ceased to be a health resort, was married
only a few months ago.
Harry Bickford lias presented an armed aeroplane
to the War Office, and given orders for a motor-
ambulance to be handed over to the local military.
The stream of passengers to England grows. Most
of them 1 have soldier sons whom they hope to see.
Bayfield Moulden and Mrs. D. 11. Cudmore are
amongst the last to go.
Archdeacon Colin Campbell, who was here as
tutor to the little Kintores, died at Feltwell Rectory,
Norfolk, the other day. His father was a colonel
in the 60th Rifles at the time of the Indian Mutiny.
A new capitalist has dawned on our horizon. The
papers give him the novelistic name of Vincent A.
Zed, and say in an awe-struck tone that he has made
a fortune “in the South-East” in the last five years.
Anyway, he has taken over the properties bought
(or thereabouts) by Paul Joseph, and proposes cheer-
fully to live at Montefiore (the late Chief Justice
Way’s home), and use the foundations of the
City Temple that-was-to-be for a coffee-palace. And
Mr. Zed produces real money, too, which is more
than did the sanguine Joseph.
Lieut. Arthur Gepp, killed in France, was only
20 when the end came. During his last year at
Prince Alfred he led the South Aus. candidates in an
entrance examination to Duntroon. Only one other
examinee in the whole Commonwealth headed him.
Gepp was a cousin of the South Aus. Rhodes scholar,
Henry Brose, who had the bad luck to be holidaying
in Germany at the outbreak of war, and was
marched into an internment camp, where he still is.
“Rosna,” writing from London, tells me
that publisher John Murray’s son, who is a
major these times, is engaged to Helen,
youngest daughter of the Brasseys. Bride-
to-be is the only child of the second wife and
wears de Vere as a second name. The groom-
elect, who served at Gallipoli, was invalided
home from there and is now convalescent.
Marjorie Jeffries, daughter of Alderman
Jeffries, is telling everybody about her
engagement now. For a time the secret was
only given out on trams by her closest
friends; but those days are over. The
lucky young man has just arrived at Salis-
bury Plains Camp in England. He is
Lieutenant Itowe, of the A.S.C.
Like sister Lena and many other women
who have sacrificed their kitehen-and-pantry
careers to follow the prosaic occupation of
platform-singing, Mary Conly has grown—
well, she has grown—in the pursuit of high
C. Still, it is possible to be brilliant though
abundant, and Mary proved it at Melbourne
Orchestral Society’s concert last week. The
affair was a result of Melba’s remark regard-
ing the musical malnutrition of Melbourne,
and mostly consisted of a square feed of
Beethoven and Grieg, with side dishes of
Sibelius and Jarnefelt and a couple of suc-
culent snacks by Percy Grainger. Mary
Conly’s contribution was a Beethoven aria.
The audience, making appreciative noises,
stayed until the end of the meal and wore
quite a well-fed look when it left.
Pergolesi’s “Stabat Mater” will be given
with full orchestral accompaniment by
Melba’s pupils on Thursday evening, Sep-
tember 14, at the New Assembly Hall, in
Collins-street. It is the 148 rd students’
concert the Oonservatorium has blessed. And
what has b“oi mo of the shrieks of yesterday?
The Repertory Club party on Friday
afternoon is to be a gorgeous affair. Mrs.
Geoffrey Syme and sister-in-law Mrs.
Oswald are to be the hostesses, and Sara
AH good and the other Peg o’ My Hearties
have promised to attend.
The postponed Pageant of Countries will
happen in The Playhouse when the Novem-
ber races are raging, starting probably on
Derby night. The costumes are said to be
gorgeous.
Professor Osborne is to read a paper on
“The Goethe Legend” on Wednesday, Sep-
tember 27. If you don’t want to find your-
self amongst the brainstormers and the in-
evitable “discussion,” keep away from the
Repertory Club at eight o’clock.
Maurice Farkoa has died in England.
Maurice was the tuneful Frenchman with a
wisp of white in his black hair who ap-
peared in the short but merry season with
comedian George Huntley. He left only a
few of the many thousands he made while
he was the rage in London. Lovesick
maidens doted on him at a distance, but
among the women of the musical-comedy
world he was noted for never making love
except as a matter of stage duty.
The G.-G. duly opened the exhibition of
Mather’s pictures in the upper room of the
Athenaeum and spoke feelingly of the late
artist, who had been his friend and bailed
from the same part of Scotland. Then his
Ex. caused a red label to be gummed on
“The Bush Track at Fernshaw” for old
times’ sake.
The Arts and Crafts are at it again. On
Friday they had a Jug Day, when all sorts
of locally-made pottery was shown. Some-
body demonstrated clay-throwing on a
potter’s wheel, and somebody else showed
how to model.
Another sign of these moving times has
been erected at Brighton by the C. of E.
Diocesan Mission —a Babies’ Home, where
no candidate for admission is to be barred
owing to the absence of relatives. It is
to be run by experienced sisters on up-to-
date principles, of which No. I is a well-
ventilated milk supply.
The committee dames of a suburban cr&che
were thrilled the other morning by the arri-
val of one of their regular clients clad in a
gorgeous fur coat. As it seemed a bit thick
for fur-coated charladies to use an institu-
tion intended for the hard-up, the social in-
telligence department started on the trail
and found that the charlady’s street had
pooled its cash for the purchase of one fur
coat, which was to be worn in turn by the
various subscribers!
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

ABORIGIN-

“Bumnyong”: There’s a pub in Grafton
(N.S.W.) whose sign is “Stroll Inn.’’ After I had
sampled the beer I wondered why the place wasn’t
crowded.
IN THE FOREFRONT.
OLD GENT. : “I hear your son is displaying great ability as a footballer, Mrs. McLusky.”
MRS. McLUSKY: "Sure, an' he's hopin' to be picked for next year's team as wan of the
drawbacks."
“Dead Finish”: “Barcoo” (B. 27/7/’l6)
should plug up some of the holes in his hat when
he says that nearly everyone in Queensland is install-
ing electric shearing gear. There has not been one
such installation within the last three years in the
North. Undoubtedly the electric should be the best,
but until we produce electricians who are both
competent and willing to go into the back country,
owners of jumbucks will stick to the friction gear.
The trouble in getting men who understand the elec-
tric plant is a hundredfold more than in getting
experts for the older machine.
* * * •
“Try Fluke”: Can South Sea or Malay
fishermen lick this Maoriland big-net record? Old
To Pokiha, better known as Major Pox, the tattooed
and be-medalled boss man of the Maori kainga of
Maketu, Bay of Plenty, bequeathed among his fam-
ilv treasures for preservation by the tribe a huge
fishing-net made of raw flax just over 1700
yards in length and 30 feet in width. With all its
stone sinkers and ropes it weighed nearly four tons,
and when it was put into the water it required the
whole man and woman power of the tribe to haul it.
the ancient brother had it composed in preparation
for a big tribal feast, and the first haul was some-
thing for fish-loving Tangata Maori to remember.
It panned out 20,000 kahawai , besides sharks galore
and stingarees no end. It took more than 200
Maketuters, in canoes and ashore, to round up
that shoal of kahawai and get the net ashore. It
was so grand a net that it was even given a name
of its own, to wit, “Puku-nui,” meaning “Big
Belly. Last time I heard of “Puku-nui” he, she
or it was stored in a big whare at Taheke, on Lake
Rotoiti.
— * * * *
F.N.T. : Frank Allan, who for many
years was Victoria’s chief vermin-inspector, and has
been the most persistent advocate of the apple-and-
strychmne idea for killing rabbits, finds that he has
to get out of the chimney-corner to argue with people
w o declare that native birds have suffered severely
thiough eating baits. Allan claims that he has done
for rabbits by the million, and in all his career has
found only two dead parrots and one dead magpie
beside the apple-trail. The Allan method of rabboli-
tion is to spill unpoisoned apple-cubes along a furrow
for bunny to eat and “get the habit.” Same proce-
dure for the next two or three nights. Then the
cubes are strychnined, and bunny has his final meal.
The main point is to see that enough apple is given
tj feed the whole “herd” and leave a little over. The
result will be that in the last guzzle every animal
will get all that’s coming to him. It is a thorough
and ingenious scheme, but it seems remarkable that
native birds which would ordinarily eat apples should
know more than Bill Rabbit when it comes to sam-
pling the strychnined offering.

“Bindieye”: “Sassafras” has either had
the bad luck to get hold of useless mokes or else he
does not know his subject when he says the Aus-
tralian horses of to-daj' are not equal to those of the
old days. I have a pony mare, about hands,
that in one day in January, 1914, I drove in a sulky
with two men in it from Young to Frogmore
(N'.S.W.) and back, a distance of 95 miles, with the
thermometer in the neighborhood of lOOdeg. in the
shade. And I alone am 16st. 41b. We were back at
Young before seven o’clock in the evening, and the
mare was none the worse for the trip. She was stable-
fed and working every day, and is still working.
“Rag”: Ivangaroo-rat grilled on the coals
is as delectable as any tucker the Australian bush
affords. Don’t bother to skin the beast. Simply
hold it over the fire until the heat distends the car-
case and all fur is burnt off; then eviscerate, and
wash the body and put it, dissected into four parts,
on the coals.

“D.F.”: The fungus mentioned by “N. or
M ” ( B - 10/8/’l6) as a specialty of the Tasmanian
beech forests also attaches itself to the closely-
related beeches of the bottom end of South America.
If it isn’t the same fungus it has a strong family
resemblance, and, like the beeches themselves, it is
one of the arguments in favor of the theory that
Shackleton could have walked all the way from
Buenos Aires to Hobart if he had started soon
enough. The natives of Tierra del Fuego eat the
fungus in plural quantities, but I never heard of
anyone trying it as a titbit in the Speck.
* * * *
“Nil : About this “homing instinct.” It
is an instinct, of course, and like all instincts
depends upon certain co-ordinated nerve reflexes.
Most animals have it —one may say all, in varying
degrees. Humans have it—as a rule in direct pro-
portion as they revert to the primordial type. I
have it. Civilised men have it also, but mostly
to such a small extent that it is not to be demon-
strated. Now and then one meets a human who in
this respect is a throwback, and retains his
pure reflexes uninterfered with by conscious reason-
ing. Now, as to one theory regarding the nerve
mechanism. In the make-up of the internal ear are
—on either side of the head—three so-called “semi-
circular canals.” If you take two little stiff-bound
books and open them to a right angle and place
them on the table in front of you so that their
heelings are opposed to one another and are per-
pendicular, and so that the hinder part of the right
book is parallel with the fore part of the left book,
you will have a very good schema of the position
and relation of the “semi-circular canals’’ in the
head. It is not quite accurate, but ’twill serve.
If the table were hinged in the middle and doubled
up fore and aft, like the roof of a house at a low
pitch, it would be more correct. Imagine that each
of the planes of each book and also each plane of
the table, or “roof,” contains an almost circular
canal, and that the three right-hand ones join at
the point represented by the Jowest part of the
heeling of the right book, and that the same
arrangement applies to the left-hand system, and
that these tubes are filled with a mobile fluid and
supplied with nerve-ends to take note of and inform
the subconscious brain of every movement of the
fluid. Now, when the head moves to any extent in
any plane or direction, the aforesaid brain has a
corresponding impression and registers the same.
The rest you see for yourself. Given a starting
point at rest, after twisting and turning for any
length of time the brain unconsciously works out a
surveyor’s “traverse,” and the sum of directions—
the “bee-line”—is established. By simply reversing
the direction and keeping straight on the individual
will eventually reach its starting point. In the
same way adjustments will be made for any neces-
sary deviations on the homeward journey. I thought
this out 35 years ago. It is probably rot. But a
German professor rediscovered it later: so perhaps it
is not.
“Eryander”: Malay blood is not confined
to the S.E. coast of Papua (B. 17/8/’l6). In the
N. W. corner are non-Papuan peoples whose ancestors
certainly crossed from the Moluccas. The people of
Salwatti Island and the Sampson River country are
light brown in. color and straight of hair, and speak
what is practically Moluccan Malay. From Mount
Nusa Hali, in the island of Ceram, can be seen the
coast of Onin, the people of which, though their
tongue is three-parts Papuan, are Mohammedan in
religion and entirely Ceramese in appearance. Fur-
ther south, in the Baaik district, I was ethnologi-
cally knocked out by finding a people who, though
physically identical with the Minahassans of north-
ern Celebes, spoke a dialect like that of the west-
Bornean Dyaks. “Papua,” by the way, is itself a
Malay word, best rendered in English by “frizzy.”
“A neb”: A word to confirm “Ojo” (B.
10/8/ 1G) that agricultural and pastoral shows
throughout Horstralia are conducted in a very casual
manner by the judges. Even at a late Melbourne
Show a grey animal was exhibited, and won prizes
in the following classes: Lady’s hack, heavyweight
hack, lady s hunter, heavyweight hunter, lightweight
hunter, and lady’s horse over hurdles. Dinizulu, 30-
odd stone, on the back of a 14-hand Basuto pony,
didn't look more ridiculous than a lady going to
hounds on top of a heavyweight hack. Harness
classes were funnier, if possible. In the single
buggy-horse class—perhaps the most genuine Aus-
tralian utility animal—the beast was tied into con-
veyances that varied from a family double-seater to a
spider-wheeled pneumatic-tyred vehicle that would
have been luxury to a Colombo rickshaw-puller.
Definition wanted: What’s a buggy at an A. and P.
show? And when will the judge for hunters be
competent to place the horses after one exhibition
of jumping the course? The idea of jumping the few
best until some make a mistake is unfair—chiefly to
the judge whose incompetence is exposed to the bulk
of the onlookers.

“Toby Collins”: Advise “Taurua” (B.
3/8/’l6) to try the following' on his aquatic bull.
Fasten stout leather or metal collars round the fore
and aft fetlocks on the same side, run a strong side-
line between, adjust same to cause a shortened
stride, and make fast. This will not only stop his
Majesty from swimming over the river to spoon with
the neighboring girls, but should any of them ven-
ture to call at his private address to reproach him
they’ll have to be content with a helping of glad
eyes and promises.

A SONG OF YANKO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, Devil-may-care for the Wanderlust,
For Wanderlust and me;
Oh, Devil-may-care for the Wanderlust,
In the days when we both are free
To wander at will by the salt sea sand,
Or climb where the mountains pile ;
For I have a home in the Western Land,
Three Hundred and Sixty Mile.
Oh, Devil-may-care for the Wanderlust,
The Wanderlust and I,
While we can drink in the city depths
We knew in the days gone by,
And drink to the depths of the days we knew,
Every once in a while;
For I have a home in the Western Land,
Three Hundred and Sixty Mile.
When my soul grows sick of the cares of State,
Of the just and the most unjust,
When I weary awhile of the hens and pigs
I go with the Wanderlust;
We go by “sleeper” and first saloon,
And we do the thing in style;
For I have a home in the Western Land,
Three Hundred and Sixty Mile.
When the fruit is sold and the verse is sold
And the money’s been paid away,
And there’s none to borrow for half a year,
We worry it out for a day.
(For “the Wanderlust” is a mate named Jim,
And Jim is a rare old file;
And we both have homes in the Western Land,
Three Hundred and Sixty Mile.)
Then we take the gun and the lines and leave
The pumpkins and corn to grow;
And we take our swags and we take the track
Where the western rivers flow ;
We shear a bit if we’re feeling fit
Just to help the billies to bile
And the oven to bake and the pans to fry
At Three Hundred and Sixty Mile.
There’s a rose-colored rose by the eastern porch,
The last of the winter there
(They die ere the summer can come to scorch
All things that are fresh and fair) ;
But the channels shall fill and the fields and farms,
In spite of the drought shall smile,
Where I have a home in the Western Land,
Three Hundred and Sixty Mile.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.

“Eddyson”: A marriage ceremony went
to pieces at Tinbiddy (Vic.) the other day as a
result of the bridegroom neglecting to haste to the
wedding. Walter Walters was to have been welded
in the bonds with Miss Virginia Street, and Virginia
was at the appointed place bright and early, wearing
veil and sprays, and pa and ma supported
her, while her brother Tom attended to see fair play.
The performance was advertised for half-past 11.
At that hour Walter was horribly conspicuous by
his absence. At a quarter to 12 his failure to par-
ticipate was agonisingly obvious. At 12 he was
still flagrantly elsewhere, and at half-past 12 his
detention in parts unknown was a crying shame.
Virginia was having energetic hysterics in a pew
and brother Tom was inviting the clergyman to
register his terrible vows of vengeance. At a quarter
to one Chadwill, who was to have been the best
man, raced in, covered with dust, and intimated
that he had been rushing up and down since 10
a.m. and had failed to locate Walters. The bridal
party loaded itself into Samson’s American waggon
and drove home. Chadwell appears to have spent
nine hours on the run, hunting Walter Walters,
before he succumbed to exhaustion, and then there
was quiet in'Tinbiddy. One hour and a half later,
when Finn and Farawav’s film entertainment was
progressing in a neighboring township called Cat
Hill, a disturbance arose with the suddenness of a
flight of bombs. Somewhere in the darkness a man
was trying to get in and meeting resistance.
“Lemme in!” he howled. “Lemme in! Whar yeh
mean keepin’ a man from his own weddin’ ? Lo, the
(hie) bri’groom cometh. Lemme in! Can’t yeh
leraman get married ? Poo poo poo ho poo ho!”
He roared a bar of the “Wedding March,” and
charged the doorkeeper again. This time the dis-
turbing element broke down the foe’s resistance, and
he streamed into the hall, whooping in the darkness.
“Here I am!” he shouted. “Wheresh me bonny
bride? Wheresh the (hie) objec’ of me ’fections?
Pompom pompom pompom!” Another slab of
the “Wedding March,” and then the misguided
stranger grabbed a lady. “ ’Sail ri’,” he cried. “I
gorrer ! On wither weddin’! Lerrergo!” The lady
screamed, and then, to facilitate the capture of the
disturber of the peace, lights were introduced, which
disclosed the rabid unknown embracing the middle-
aged spouse of a prominent citizen of Cat Hill. The
man looked ’ amazedly at the matron in his arms for
a few oppressive seconds. Then he said: “Ellen-
tommy! I gorrer wrong bride.” He dropped the
lady and looked about him in sore perplexity.
“Wha’s more,” he muttered, “I’m in the wrong
church!” They led him out. He threw an eye over
the moonlit landscape. “Why, isser wrong town!”
ho said. It was Walter Walters, the missing bride-
groom.
“Professional Gardener”: Anyone who
recommends that Australia grow no wheat for one
season in order to get rid of wdieat rust knows little
of the parasite (B. 3/8/’l6). I am with Massee, of
London University, who thinks that the fungus is in
the first of three distinct periods of growth when it
is found on wheat. It also attacks other plants, and
takes on different colors and forms, and in some
cases is difficult to recognise. On apple and pear
trees it is “black spot”; on lemons and oranges,
“collar rot”; in apricot, “shot hole fungus”; on
carnations and sweet peas it is the same as that on
pears and apples. But it comes to maturity only on
the well-known Darwini and other species of the
Berberis plant. Here the rust blooms and fruits and
then broadcasts its seeds into the atmosphere, to be
brought down on to various vegetation by the fine,
misty rains of November. My remedy is to wipe
out the Berberis plant that harbors the nuisance.

“Warre”: Horses gnaw the bark off trees
for the same reason that cows chew bones and sheep
feast on defunct rabbits—to satisfy the craving for
phosphates. When one considers the enormous weight
of bone, flesh and wool, representing largely 'calcium
phosphate (or phosphate of lime), which has been
THOUGHT THEY WOULD NEVER GO.
JONES (to couple planted outside his back
fence): “Say, old man, ask the milkman
to leave an extra pint, will you?"
taken from the Australian soil during the last cen-
tury without an effort being made to restore the
loss, it is a miracle that the bovines and ovines, in
their hunt for bone essentials, do not eat the dam-
phool landowners. Let “The Hum” (B. 10/8/’l6)
try a little bonemeal in his moke’s tucker and note
results. The wheat cookies of Australia use nearly
£1,500,000 of imported phosphates every year, and
export it all, or nearly all, in wheat. Yet four-fifths
of the total phosphates in wheat is contained in the
bran and pollard which could be kept here as a
stand-by in drought times if we gristed our wheat
and exported the flour.

“E.T.”: A horse belonging to a farmer at
Serpentine (Vic.) was recently posted missing. It
had not been seen for nearly a week. Then a vigilant
search was made, and it was discovered standing
upright beneath, and supporting the full weight of, a
straw stack which had blown over it during a gale.
The unfortunate moke had been buried for five days
and six nights; but though it was a little weak and
almost blind for a time it soon recovered and has
resumed work.

evolution of bush fences “Werri»tnai‘ks on the

in charge of 10,000 wethers running bo I was

sheep used to make for a stretch of burnt country
where there was always

and persuaded my new-
chum manager to put up a
light fence around the
burnt country. In due
course the light fence
arrived from Sydney in
the form of two miles of
single-width calico. A
couple of grinning station-
hands drove stakes into
the ground about 14ft.
apart, tacked the calico

boundary-ride the horror.

and they were frequent,
most of my time was

disentangle the fence from

to sprint after an old
milker and haul a few
yards of my fence from
her interior in a more or
less digested condition.
Swaggies 'would sneak up

for a tent and then pass

tidings. The gaps in that
accursed fenefe increased
steadily and I got tired
of filling them in with
bushes. At last, when I
discovered that all the
coekies for miles around
were sleeping between
sheets and their kids were
prancing around proudly
in new white shirts, I
decided to let the fence
rip. After a while I got
a letter from the new.
chum manager asking me
to report upon his fence.
I reported that it was
greatly admired by the
neighbors, but it was too
light to keep the sheep
back. Thereupon he wired:
“What do you recom-
mend ?” To which I re-
plied with a collect wire:
“Local experts agree that
the fence is satisfactory
for summer months, but,
as winter is approaching,
strongly recommend two
miles of blankets.” I
don’t know if the recom-
mendation was adopted, as
I wrapped my belongings
in the last panel of the fence and departed.

“Mitchell Grass” : Re the lariat on cattle
ranches. After 33 years’ experience on large runs in
Queensland I have come to the conclusion that cattle
which are not yarded and herded as weaners are
difficult to handle at gates, are wild on the run, and
are bad for drovers to handle, especially at the
trucking yard. “Barcoo” (B. 27/8/’l6) says that
Davenport Downs carries 45,000 cattle. I have my
doubts, and I know the place well. The use of the
lariat on distant parts of a run is very useful, but
the best that can be said for its general use is that
it is cheap.

“Splodger”: Jonesmith was a good talker,
who loved his own voice. One of his favorite tricks
was to pause at the critical moment and say:
“Gentlemen I I hope I do not bore you ?” The
proper response, of course, being, “No, nol Go onl”
This time he was spellbinding a bar-parlor, and was
letting himself go on Anecdote No. 724 when he
paused and sounded: “Gentlemen! I hope I do not
bore you?” The man in the corner caught his eye.
“Oh, no!” he yawned. “Anything to pass the time
away 1” ,

“D.F.”: People will tell you that a good
many of our native berries are poisonous, but I have
eaten pretty well all that grow in the Speck and
most mainland ones, and I haven’t been measured for
a coffin yet. Maybe some of them would not be good
as a steady diet, but that kind are not very attrac-
tive specimens as tucker, anyway. There is a legend
that the big bell-shaped blue berry, which the botan-
ists call fabillardieria for short, is particularly
deadly. It is pretty wooden, but I haven’t found it
poisonous. About the only really satisfying edible
fruits in the Tasmanian bush are the native
cherry, the cranberry (which is good but mostly
stone), the wild raspberry, the berry of the Mac-
quarie Harbor vine (much like a miniature passion-
fruit), and perhaps the wild currant, though most
people don’t like its flavor.
“Dux”: A dozen wharfies had just lifted
their pints and were in the act of raiding the
counter-lunch when Joe, of the Carbine Stables, blew
in, and, taking in at a glance the team of pot-and-
lunch champions, cried cheerily: “Going to have one,
boys?” It sounded for all the world like an invita-
tion to breast the bar, so a dozen pintpots were
quickly hoisted skywards and as smartly banged upon
the counter. “Wish ter Gawd I wasl” concluded
Joe, and ducked out.

THE BATTLE OF PRAWNS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The glasses filled themselves, and flew
To quench my thirst; in row on row
They stood in splendor in my view.
I took some seventeen or so,
Until the last one spilled itself
And whispered it was time to go.
iVr-door slammed behind my back,
And, as°d to say that it was rude,
It roused' gzagged in my track,
I would have siP an an S r y moocl ’
I , ! t - «l it, but it dodged
And got away frSE j stoocl .
A theatre walked over me-
lt seemed the only thing to c.
I shook an actor by the hand
Whose evening clothes, somehow, I k., vv . ;
Then took a seat, to hear awhile
A tenor singing, “Oysters two 1”
It seemed the dullest kind of show ;
And so I called an aeroplane.
But, as I sought to make a flight
, Above the white, foam-covered main,
A nasty voice remarked to me:
“You must not do that thing again!”
I stared, amazed, as well I might:
A prawn stared rudely in my face.
I felt the insult, unavenged,
Would be a lasting, dark disgrace;
But as I seized it in my hands
Prawns by the dozen filled the place.
I spilled the table to the floor
As from the mess I struggled free,
And half a dozen shining plates
Went spinning through infinity ;
I swore to fight with any prawn
That dared to lay its hands on me.
The fight was awful and intense;
One hand of mine with gore was wet;
The prawns went down in slaughtered heaps;
The oysters in great piles were set:
And to the man who held me up
I said, “We’ll beat the damned things yet.”
The struggle was of Titans, sure.
One flying missile struck a star,
Which vanished with a crash so loud
We heard it plainly from afar;
And shortly pieces of the smash
Fell, sounding like a breaking bar.
A lobster struck me on the head —-
My head, in fact, is aching still.
I do not feel inclined to fret
At vague remarks about a bill;
But there was blood upon my hand....
Now who, I wonder, did I kill?
N.s.w.

“The general depression in the building trade
continues and has affected the year’s turn-
over and consequently the profits,” the direc-
tors say. And this is not likely to be merely
transitory. It is not only that the amount
of building will be less for some years to
come than it has been during the artificial
prosperity of the borrowing boom, but prices
will be cut as well. It has been possible to
get almost anything for some classes of tim-
ber during the past few years. Moreover,
there are other things beside the stoppage of
borrowing to affect the building trade. The
Fair Rents Court may not have done much
in giving people cheaper houses to live in;
but it has certainly given builders a feeling
of insecurity. However, Goodlet and Smith
are old hands in this business; they know
that, thanks to our tendency to go on furious
borrowing bursts, the building trade pro-
ceeds by fits and starts, and they have made
arrangements accordingly. They took ad-
vantage of the same boom which made build-
ing brisk to get rid of their excess lands, and
they have also worked down their stock. The
results can be seen in this table: —
1906. 1910. 1914. 1915. 1916.
£ £ £ £ £

Properties 100,670 93,670 ]
Machinery & ) 100,274 79,647 66,343
plant, etc. 89,867 89,837 j
Ships . . 2,200 500

debts, etc. 32,177 55,251 122,433 145,263 154,619
Thus liabilities have been reduced to next
door to nothing ; and all but £10,381 of the
capital (£165,000) is represented by “sundry
debtors, cash and shares in other cos.,” which
sounds very safe, though a good deal depends
on that item “shares in other cos.”

“Exile”: We think the Bendigo pipes
deep at 4000 ft., but • California boasts a
gold producer at a depth of 6300 ft. The
mill is turning out £lB,OOO a month of gold
from a high-grade ore. Shareholders have
received £975,400 in divvies from this North
Star mine. In Nevada at a depth of
8000 ft. the Estelle Co. is driving a tunnel
7400 ft. into the mountain. The present
face is 6900 ft. from the mouth; already
five veins of silver-lead ore have been cut
and three more are expected. The high
price of metals has caused a revival of
mining in Nevada and Arizona, and quick-
silver, antimony, wolfram, zinc and silver-
lead mines long abandoned are being re-
opened. By the way, barytes is being
mined on a big scale, because barium is
the source of an extremely smelly poison-
gas the Allies have begun to use. This
ought to be good news for Tasmanian
holders of barium deposits, who should go
to work and export stink-bearing ore to
Europe.
ip * * *
Burma Corporation, in which most of
Lake View and Oroya’s hopes are centred,
last year estimated ore reserves at 1,310,000
tons containing 19.50 z. silver, 25.2 p.c. lead,
and 21.6 p.c. zinc; now they are calculated
at 2,300,000 tons, assaying 25.40 z. silver,
27.5 p.c. lead and 22.2 p.c. zinc. Recent
developments at Nos. 3 and 4 levels are
declared to have added a further 300,000
tons.
* * * *
South Africa has given German trade
another blow. Prior to the war three-
fourths of the cyanide required for the
treatment of Rand residues was made in
Germany and only one-fourth in Britain.
Now Britain per medium of the Cassel Co.
has a five years’ contract to supply almost
the lot.

“Hambidge”: The girl and I bad found a
good place on the beach before I remem-
bered the usual box of chocolates had been
forgotten. It was a good half-mile to the
nearest shop, so I hailed a small boy. “Here,
son, get me a box of chocolates with this
bob, and when you come back I’ll give you
another.” He made off in the direction of
the pier, and we waited an hour, but the
small boy never returned. At last the girl
shook the sand out of her skirts and said it
was time to go. That night the absconder
accosted me on the pier. “Well,” I said,
“do you reckon you’re a sport?” He eyed
me with contempt for a moment. “ ’Struth.”
he said. “Yoit don’t eat chocolates, do yer?”
“No,” I answei'ed. “You knew they weren’t
for me.” “ ’Course I did,” he agreed. “An’
didn't I save yer a bob?”
A Murwillumbah (N.S.W.) railway porter
took up a dozen acres of land, planted
bananas, and last year paid income tax on
£l3OO. If everybody who is now planting
bananas up there realises bis hopes it will
be necessary in five years’ time for every
person in Sydney to consume 11 bananas on
week days and 15 on Sundays.
* * * *
The other day the G.-G. added his mellow
tones to the paean now gradually swelling
in praise of Australian furniture timbers.
A Melbourne firm recently made a suite
from the plebeian stringybark, seasoned at
the State works, and the result was so. hand-
some that Government House was given a
chance to add its delight to the chorus. The
G.-G. let her go in a most generous manner,
declaring that he “had never seen more
beautiful timber.” It all helps.
