paj-ing the premiums.
*, * * *
“Wild Cat” note on A.I.P. Co., Unlimited :
Tiie Bulletin hasn’t the space to present the
figures in tabular form. The concern is rather less
than two years old, and has given some notable
results, though the first location of the works was
not a happy choice. After consulting together and
obtaining the best advice the directors finally decided
to get out and make a fresh start elsewhere. The
change of address was accomplished at very slight
cost to the co., and it is now understood that useful
work is being carried on in a district where better re-
sults can be looked for. However, the costs are propor-
tionately higher, and, in order that the co. may not
be hampered in its operations, the Australian direc-
tors are understood to be consulting again with a
view to getting in more capital and providing
adequate reserves. - The present employees of the co.
are anxious to see the scope of the young firm
extended, and are ready to welcome new workers.
The provident fund is in a healthy state, and new
workers coming in participate immediately if they
are unlucky enough to be disabled. The ambulance
arrangements are first-class, and in every respect the
co.’s works are looked upon as amongst the best
equipped in the world. It is understood that a
sufficient amount is written off for depreciation of
plant, and the goodwill, though immensely valuable,
does not appear in the balance-sheet. The A.I.F.
Co. was formed to meet a sudden emergency, and,
though the expenses are enormous, the profits are
expected to be on the same scale. It will not be
possible to estimate the exact return to patriotic
investors until the close of operations—or even for
some considerable time afterwards; But even at this
early stage the visible results of the operations are
amply sufficient to justify the formation and exten-
sion of the co.

“Warre”: The Wheat Board is doing the
right thing in refusing to accept wheat in
second-hand bags. When the Chapman sack
comes along after a second tilling it has to
be treated with tenderness and care to get
it over even the railway weighbridge. After
that the lumper, possessed of no tine feel-
ings, usually dumps it into the middle of
the stack, where it gives up the ghost and
lets the grain run to earth. But instead of
confining its attention to the sack when it
has done one job, why doesn’t the Board
keep an eye also on the philanthropists who
run the jute business with a view to getting
a decent article in the first place? Some of
last season’s goods were simply disgraceful.
The Maoriland cocky lias a fine commer-
cial instinct. x\fter refusing John Bull’s
good offer of Bd. per lb. for cheese (a price
better than ever he got before the war),
Teatsqueez gathered himself into a crowd
and protested against the increase in freights
charged by the shipping companies, the
ground of protest being that the extra
charges were likely to embarrass the Empire
in the prosecution of the war! Then
Teatsqueez dispersed looking for someone
ready to give an extra £5 an acre for a farm
at Maungawahinekaiponga.
It took an imported architect to realise
the beauty of the Geelong building stone,
and £40,000 worth of it is now being got
out for the new R.C. University buildings
in Melbourne. These were designed, by
W. B. Griffin, the artistic Yankee who isn’t
a bit like the dollar-hunting Yankee.

A milkman prosecuted in Melbourne the
other day for watering his goods pleaded
with marked impatience: “Well, we got to
make the milk go rtfund.” A public bene-
factor, clearly; yet they fined him the usual
amount.
* * * *
“F.C.” : Some further illumination on the
doings of that little household utensil, the
flour-sifter. As I said before, the price
used to be Bs. 6d. per doz. wholesale. In
those days all sifters came from W. Wilson’s
country, but when the price reached 325.
per doz. a Melbourne firm started making
them, and doing it a shade better. This
firm, or rather the Associated Wholesale
Ironmongers, charged the same price as for
the imported, so apparently 12s. per doz.
paid all right. Since then the price of the
Yankee article has steadily mounted until it
is now 10s. Gd. per doz. —and the local
article has kept step with it and is now
IBs. Gd. too. I don’t know whether the
Wholesale Ironmongers (under a strictly
honorable understanding) or the local
manufacturers are snavelling the extra 4s.
6d., but somebody is. This is, of course, the
sort of thing that a Permanent Tariff Board
given the proper amount of authority could
correct in one act and keep corrected ; but
such a thing doesn’t yet exist.
* * * *
Away back in 1469 or thereabouts the
King of Norway was in need of cash and
pawned the Orkney and Shetland Islands to
the King of Scotland for 60,000 florins.
Kaiser Wilhelm, so the yarn runs, wants
the islands for a naval base, and is bar-
gaining with Norway for the equity of
redemption. Maybe if he buys them he will
settle down there after the war. Anyway, if
compound interest is to be paid on the
60,000 florins that Norway raised, the price
will run to about £20,000,000,000. Wil-
helm’s idea, no doubt, is to pay it in paper.
* * * *
“F.N.T.” : The ironbark, “threatened,” so
“X.Y.” says, “with early destruction
throughout the N.S.W. North Coast area”
by an insect pest (B. 24/8/T6), has other
troubles. Read these uncomfortable words
of the Inter-State Commission on the same
subject: “The supply in N.S.W. is nearer
exhaustion than that of any other hard-
wood. The timber trade looks to the Bel-
linger and Nambucca rivers to supply the
bulk of its requirements in the future It
does not regenerate well. It is being cut
for girders in the immature. stages and
before it has commenced to seed freely.
The conclusion is that within 10 years the
supply of ironbark on the Bellinger River
will be exhausted.” This Bellinger iron-
bark is the Eucalyptus paniculata —the best
of the several species. Apparently the
Forestry Department has spent too much
time around the office, with its nose in the
sporting news and its feet on the fender, _ to
be aware of what’s really been happening
in the northern forests.
A POSTAL ECCENTRICITY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The lost bags and several others were being
thrown from the Milson’s Point wharf on to the ferry
steamer at 9.30. During this rough-and-ready
method of transference two bags were tossed over-
board, and now repose on the harbor bed. Up to
the present no effort has been made to secure them.’
Alicia wrote a loving line to Jack,
Forgiving him with kisses crossed in ink,
Beftring the wanderer to come swiftly back
Before she took to drink.
Five creditors with fury wrote demands
On William Smith to hurry up and pay
For goods delivered by their generous hands
Before next Saturday.
Tom, in despair, his wealthy aunt approached
For cash required in more than urgent haste;
Failing relief his latest egg was poached—
No time had he to waste.
Dawkins, by stealth, obtained a stable tip
And sent the thing at once to Billy Cob:
“Straight wire—you put your shirt on Bumbleflip ;
Invest for me five bob.’’
Porkhampton wrote his broker urgent word
About the sale of certain mining shares;
And Jimpling told his agent ’twas absurd,
That charge for roof repairs.
All in the lottery we call the post
They dropped the variegated inky screeds,
With others of the mighty scribbling host
Who link swift words to deeds.
The postman gathered them with special care
And sealed them in a bag with wax and string ;
The merry day was warm and bright and fair,
The year was at the Spring.
The whole Department was as blithe and gay
As lovers kittling in a wattle arbor;
It tossed the post-bags round in merry play,
Then left them in the harbor.
N.S.W.
MARY McC.
TOOLS OF TRADE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sail, the compass and the oar,
The roaring gun that reeks of war,
The axe, the trowel—these and more
Were made for living men.
But wigs and mitres, wands and gowns,
Regalia, sceptres, orbs and crowns,
Are only trappings fit for clowns
Within a circus pen.
A king would rather go to gaol
Than use his sceptre on a nail;
A bishop in a storm afloat
With mitres would not bail a boat;
A judge would dance on air a jig
Ere he would scrub-out with his wig.
On useful work they’re all afraid
To use their foolish tools of trade.
So, rather than a golden crown,
An orb, a mitre or a gown,
A wand, a wig, a ’broidered train,
A rabbit-robe or golden chain,
A feathered hat upon,my brain,
Silk stockings only fit for Jane —•
I’d rather sweat and dig a drain!
Come, gentlemen! Be not afraid
To own a humble tool of trade!
The time is done for lordly mirth !
AVhat claim have ye upon the earth ?
K.s.w.

Pay will be at the rate of £l9O per
annum inclusive of all allowances except

mandant, Keswick Barracks, Adelaide.

Melbourne
POLITICAL
POINTS
Last week, according to the daily papers,
a duck with two heads was hatched at Wind-
sor (N.S.W.). That bird was born to be a
politician in Australia. It will be able to
say Yes-No on any question.
Australia’s bill for war pensions is already
on the brink of half a million per annum.
The pensions granted so far are 11,542;
and we still think we can support seven
Cocked Hats, fourteen Parliaments and
Norton Griffiths besides.
This proposal of Holman’s, to bring up
the pension of the N. S. Wales incapaci-
tated soldier to £3 a week, is very popular,
of course —any proposal to spend money on
soldiers gets three cheers just now. But
this is a Commonwealth business, and if
the State butts in, why not also the muni-
cipality? Alderman Gubbins,- the green-
grocer, who ought to be giving attention
to the culverts, will be shrieking presently
for re-election on the ground that he favors
making the pension up to £3 ss. out of the
rates, and Blackhead, the plumber, will try
to beat Gubbins for his seat in the council
by outbidding him and offering £3 10s
THE PERILS OF THE SEA.
WELL-MEANING PARTY: “And what happened
when the cruel (Hermans sank your
ship?”
J ACK: “We went down all hands, Mum.”
A just-issued return shows that, compared
with July, 1915, exports have declined by
£230,519, while imports have increased by
£506,369. The principal imports include:
apparel, £2,316,469; tin plates, £174,277;
and iron and steel rods, £142,296. It is com-
forting, however, to know that we have not
imported either wheat or rabbits.
If little Dr. Maloney’s head were as well
served as his heart he’d be a genius. The
other day he proposed to move—“ That the
House resolves that Australia’s cost of the
war, including all pensions, which will in-
evitably amount to over £300,000,000, must
be paid by Germany, together with adequate
compensation for loss of lives.” Certainly.
And the same also with regard to Belgium,
France, England, Italy, Russia, Portugal
and Roumania. And while he is paying
the bill he is not to be permitted to do any
outside trade. He is to take in his own
washing, so to speak, and pay his debt to
the world with the proceeds. The people
who talk of shutting up the German and
also demanding a vast indemnity had better
think it over.
The poor prisoners of Victoria have been
granted a Defence Bill which may or may
not prove a blessing to the guiltless. It

legal aid for his defence. This is good—pro-
vided it doesn’t lead to the establishment of
a Poor Prisoners’ Defence Brigade, enrolled
from among the incapables of the legal pro-
fession. “Legal aid” is not bound to be
aidful. Under present conditions many a
prisoner who could have won the jury’s vote
by his own plain statement has been kept
silent by an assertive muddle headed lawyer
whose defence put his client in the soup.
Sydney Telegraph, appalled at the news
that the price of coolie labor in India may
go up, asks to be told what tea and rice
and cotton are going to cost if these people
get short hours, frequent smoke-ohs and a
decent wage. It looks a fearsome problem ;
but does the Telegraph think that if it
hired an editor at 15s. a week it would be
so much more prosperous that it could afford
to sell itself for a ha’penny?
In N. S. Wales the party politicians have
begun to discuss the finances. It is plain
that the public ought to have a simple,
honest statement of the country’s business.
But it will get nothing of the kind. Holman
started off by painting everything pink.
Wade being absent, ill, George Fuller took up
his brush, and his business was to paint
everything blue. So the blessed document
will be nicely decorated with stripes, and the
truth will be quite hidden. Heaven only
knows what would happen to a private con-
cern managed by directors, part of whom
were kept busy proving that every wrong was
right and the other part proving that every
right was wrong. No doubt Heaven knows
what will happen to N. S. Wales, too.
Officially, Australia, outside its lunatic asy-
lums, is sane; yet with an area of 2,11(4,581
sq. miks and a population of five millions,
part of it is gravely discussing a proposal,
made in Victoria, to drain Lake Corangamute
at heavy expanse in order to provide the con-
tinent with 65,000 acres more cow-country.
While the Argus believes that every young
man should fight for his country, it objects
to him having an equal share in the manage-
ment of the place for which he is expected
to be willing to sacrifice his life. ■ The other
day the old news-sheet opined that “a true
reform would be to raise the voting age for
Senate elections to 30 years.” Certainly;
with an abdominal measurement of not less
than 71 inches.
The curse of boomed land values is bring-
ing colored labor in to roost on Maoriland
farms. During the last five months 43
Hindu coolies have arrived in the Dominion,
and most of them, as well as numerous
others who were inside before, have got jobs
on farms in the Waikato and other Northern
districts. The mortgage is a nightmare on
many of these holdings, and as the interest
bill has to be met somehow, the hired labor
has to suffer.
“Gnat Spry”: Yes; in his prime O’Rorke,
of Maoriland, was unrivalled as a ruler of
Parliamentary debate.
But the old man was
losing his punch towards
the finish, and his autho-
rity was never again the
same after the night he
bowed his head before
stubborn old Jock
McKenzie, Seddon’s Min-
ister for Lands and the
backbone of the Ministry.
Baited by the Opposition
during an angry scene,
McKenzie, a fierce grizzly
bear of a man, made use
of an unparliamentary
phrase and was ordered
by O’Rorke to take it
back. Jock refused, and,
when the House ad-
journed to feed a few
minutes later, threatened
Seddon that he would
resign unless the ruling
was withdrawn. Seddon,
who wanted a Cabinet
crisis less than anything
on earth, went to
O'Rorke and implored
him to give way. He
did. The reason? Well,
he was old and poor.
Everyone was sorry
for him—perhaps even
McKenzie, though that is
doubtful, for he was a
dour, hard old warrior.
O'Rorke seldom spoke as
n private member. When
he did he was amazingly
prosy. When he intro-
duced the Onehunga
Cemetery Bill he rose to
the occasion with a
rotund delivery and
weighty sentences worthy a declaration of
war on Europe.' But behind the Mace and
decorated with a wig he was Great.
Interesting note from “8.D.” :
The Federal proclamation prohibiting the sale of
enemy goods after November has filled Melbourne
shop-windows with an -alarming array of stuff.
Tempted by the reductions which are being offered
on the Hun exhibits —30 per cent, to 50 per cent. —I
invested in a set of woollens, which I was assured
were being sacrificed at half-price. On examining
them later 1 found they were manufactured in Aus-
tralia.
This supports a comparatively old Bulletin
contention —that, after the war, instead of
pushing German and allegedly-German stuff
into the background, there will be plenty of
traders who w r ill push it to the front and-
catch trade by announcing that, being
“enemy” stuff, they have to sell it cheap.
There is no way to stop the sale of foreign
stuff of ami kind except by building a tariff
wall so high that it can’t climb over.
The Vic. Education Department is after
the war-time economy prize. A country
teacher undertook to see what was the mat-
ter with a pupil who turned out to have
meningitis. Being a contact the teacher had to
be laid off work, and the Department decided
that, as he wasn’t on duty when he made
the examination, it could not pay him for
the time he was laid off. Of course he
should have allowed the child to bring the
infection to the school and examined him
there. Then 20 little corpses could have
been tied up nicely in Red Tape, and all
would have been quite right.
The South Aus. Government is anxious
to assist in the manufacture of lanoline,
which is a by-product of woolscouring, and
advertises that it will submit details pre-
pared by its Department of Chemical
Research. Hargreaves, the director, says
that costs would not be more than £ls' a
ton, though the pre-war price was about
£75. Is anything doing? If not, why not?
That s what Australia wants to know. It
is not much good having a Department of
Chemical Research if, when it discovers
something, the people in the next-door in-
dustry don’t explain why they cannot take
advantage of the discovery.
Jack Scaddan is shivering out in the cold,
without a Parliamentary seat to sit in; and
he had better be left there for a while. He
had a seat, but when his Ministry was
thrown out he resigned in order to fight
Robinson, one of his successors, for his seat.
Robinson beat him; but meantime his own
seat had been filled, and now he seems quite
hurt because the occupant refuses to move
out. Scaddan is trying to play the game
with a two-headed penny. To all intents
and purposes he wanted to remain member
for Brown Hill and be a candidate for
Canning at the same time. That isn’t
“sporting,” and Jack, as an ancient Ben-
digo footballer, wants above all things to be
considered “sporting.”
Fleming asked in the Reps, the other night
whether the Government would appoint offi-
cers to give military training to members at
such time and place as would fit in with
the performance of their duties. Frank
Tudor, in the absence of Pearce, asked for
notice of the question. If we had Conscrip-
tion there would be none of this nonsense.
A proper authority would decide on the ser-
vice that the citizen should render; and
drill-sergeants’ time wouldn’t be wasted on
people whom the Defence Act says are exempt
from service because they have another
national job to do.
The Tasmanian hopgrowers are still ham-
mering away at Customs Minister Tudor,
but have got nothing from him save a hint
that the duty may be increased some day.
He told them that if they could prove that
German hops are trickling in via U.S.A.
the Federal people would do something, but
though the growers have their suspicions
it is hard to bring positive proof. All they
know is that they are being throttled ; and
they reckon they can be just as dead if the
string that’s tied round their throats was
made in the U.S.A. as if it were made in
Germany. So they are getting busy with
the grub-hoe and will plant something else.
Watch the machine at work: —
P. ,T. Minahan, Labor member for the Sydney con-
stituency of Belmore, has been defeated for the
selection. Seven years ago, on the death of E. W.
O’Sullivan, there was a strong local feeling in favor
of John English. Despite this, the P.L.L. Executive
ran Minahan, a boot-manufacturer with money to
burn. English bottled his wrath, but cleared decks
for the general-election selection which .was then
approaching. Again he was turned down by the
Executive, this time on a technicality; and so
started the row that has raged ever since. English
at once entered the lists, and called on all lovers of
fair play and other things to rally round him.
Minahan, of course, called on—well, no matter,
seeing that he beat English by 60 votes in a big
poll. In 1913 the same pair were at it again, and
this time Minahan accounted for his man by lopo.
English then appears to have tired of the “Indepen-
dent” game, and gradually got himself accepted
once more as a real dyed-in-the-wool Laborite. His
followers, too, got the Executive (It. D. Meagher,
president) to remove the embargo, and they became
No. 2 Belmore League. Incidentally, Aid. Mick
Bourke became Aid. John English for the purpos<
of the contest. Though Minahan was making rapid
marches, speaking nicely to everybody, and spending
all his screw on charity, No. 2 battalion was kept
busy tearing out tickets of membership and gradually

HELLAS AWAKE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now by the boaes of old Leonidas,
Whose spear made terror in "the Asian throng,
Whose death-smile Treason-captured pass
Gave the free world the spirit of a song,
Greece rises with the warcry of her sires—
The Teuton’s last intrigue pales and expires.
Sparta and Athens, Argos, Arcady!
Was the old light blown out that once could shine
Clearer than white stars on the isle-set sea?
Was the hope dead, the old Homeric wine
Drugged'with strange herbs that lulled the blood
to- sleep,
AVhen Freedom rose her last blood-tryst to keep?
r .
Men wondered. For. Greece seemed a supine thing,
Forgetful of man’s rights and ancient shames.
The'-triek and'guile of a small alien king
Held!her inert amid a world in flames.
Men sorrowed for the great name of a race,
Sudden in honor, slow to brook disgrace.
And now the ancient Hellas seems to wake!
The petty German kingship pales and fades.
The loud shields clash, the spears of Sparta shake,
Ambracia forms in phalanx her brigades ;
And, ’mid the clamor that old Pyrrhus knew,
Our faith grows strong—the soul of Greece is true!
n.s.w.

THE growing activities of our second year's hostilities
find Avon Tyres in increased demand for H.M. Trans-
port. Tried and proven in the fierce flame of War, as they
have been tried in every sort of civilian service, Avons emerge
as Seasoned Campaigners worthy of the highest place in the
esteem of the motoring public.
C. We are told that this is a war of attrition or exhaustion. If tyres
are tried out to the last limit of endurance we may count upon
British Avons being found among the surviving fittest. They are
running in all theatres of the War, on all sorts of motors—from
H.R.H. The Prince of Wales’ Staff limousine to the humblest
regimental runabout.

The Uganda Rubber and Cacao Estates Ltd.
(In Liquidation).
TO THE EXECUTORS OF W. H. WATT, DE-
-1 CEASED, whose last-known address was 7 Bent
Street, Sydney, New South Wales:—
NOTICE IS HEREBY GIVEN that a sum of
Rupees Four thousand due to the aforementioned
deceased as dividend in connection with The Uganda
Rubber and Cacao Estates Ltd. (In Liquidation), is
now lying in my hands as Official Liquidator of that
Company.
Correspondence addressed as above has not been
answered. Unless this amount is claimed by 31st
December, 1916, it will be handed over to His
Majesty’s High Court of Uganda for custody. Any
claim for the above sum must be supported by
an affidavit and documentary evidence of identity,
and must be addressed to
CYRIL E. SPENCER,
Chartered Accountant,
Box 97, Kampala, Uganda.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
There are any number of sports and others
in N. S. Wales who reckon the tote has just about
arrived. The Holman Government's need of new
revenue is beginning to grow desperate, and it is not
considered likely that this source of income can be
much longer left untouched. Anyhow, several indivi-
duals who are not usually in the habit of tossing
their money away, and are suspected of being close
enough to the political stable to hear any whispers
that may be floating around, last week suddenly
found themselves anxious to acquire shares in Total-
ling Mechanisms, Ltd., at a substantial premium.
There is a yarn, too, about the company haying a
very favorable contract or option or something in
connection with the right to work the mechanical
odds-calculator on metropolitan courses. All this
has convinced most of the MacShortodds brethren that
a new. fielder is coming into the game sooner than
they expected. Questioned in the Assembly on the
subject, Holman fenced with his questioner.
* ** * *
Tattersall’s Soring meeting, always one
of the most interesting of the year to the Sydney
owner and trainer and punter, drew a big crowd to
Randwick on Saturday. Most of it went to get a line
on the big A.J.C. meeting next month. What it
succeeded in doing was to present Israel with a fair
win and contract a deep suspicion that the Epsom
and Metropolitan winners are not going to be too
easy to find. Apart from the Steeplechase (and in that
it was only a half-favorite that won; the other fell
down with three others) not one favorite lived up to
expectations; two rank outsiders finished where Ikey
wanted them to be ; and several nags heavily backed
for important events to come gave supporters a lot
of encouragement to expect the worst. Three of the
winners were imported, and, still excluding the
steeple, all the others were of lately-imported blood.
Out of 18 place-getters 13 were by sires bred out of
Australia.
Polycrates (Bst. 111 b.) put up a great performance
in the Tramway Handicap, seven furlongs. He showed
temper at the start, and got away badly. It was not
until the field were round the bend that he com-
menced to move up. At the Leger the race looked a
gift to either Colugo (7st. 121 b.) or Panacre (9st.
lib.) ; but over the last half-furlong O’Connell
brought Polvcrates along with a run which buried the
other two and put the leader a couple of lengths
ahead at the post. It was an impressive perform-
ance, and nothing transpired to suggest that the
people who had backed the imported horse for the
Epsom felt sorry for themselves. Colugo will have
41b. the better of the weight compared with Satur-
day’s tonnage, but if Polycrates repeats his form this
will not nearlv bring them together. Nor will the
difference of 51b. in favor of Merrimee, whose effort
over the last bit was nothing to get excited about,
Obliterate the difference of the form displayed. Pan-
acre did well, and may do better. Garlin will possi-
bly do even worse if that near hind leg of his gets
any stiffer. He finished last but one. Polvcrates is
weighted at Bst. 61b. in the ..Epsom, and on the
strength of Saturday’s gallop must at least be
reckoned to look better than anything which finished
behind him. But a few of those that have to be
beaten in the mile-race were not against him on
Saturday.
Chelmsford Stakes (a mile and a furlong, w.f.a.)
turned out a good thing for the ordinary-looking
Maorilander Sasanoff, one of the team sent a cress
by AV. G. Stead. The gelding stuck to his job like
a tradesman, and had a sharp tussle with Woorak
(9st. 111 b.) at the finish. The flying chestnut was
giving the winner 2st. 51b., and that was just a
little too much. There was a good field in this
race—-among others Cetigne, Kilhoy, Thana. AVolaroi,
Wallace Isinglass, Bee, Hopfield and Kingsburgh.
Wallace looked well, and after getting bumped ran
like a steer. Of the three-year-olds Thana was in
front at the finish, but both Kilbov aud AVolaroi wore
putting in good work. As usual AVoorak streaked
away on his own and made light of his burden until
collared. He is a wonderful little horse, and if
the race had been a furlong shorter must certainly
have won. In the Fpsom he will not have so far
to pro, but he has another half-stone to hump along.
It looks a lot. Apart from that the race threw
no light on the A.J.C. events; but when it was
discovered that Sasanoff had been accommodated with
6st. lllb. in the Caulfield Cup, the Bar A’ons refused
to take any liberties with him and he bounded to the
top of the list. For the present aud on paper the
Maorilander looks better than anything else in that
event.
There was a barrow-load of cash for Meroo
(7st. 81b.) in the Spring Handicap and at the
death threes was the best available Tb<-re was
money, too, for Trinobantes (Bst. 101 b.) and Bridges
(7st. 91b.) aud a little for Rnshford (Bst. 81b.). The
thud could be heard a mile away. None of them
were in it or locked as if they were going to be.
The imported gelding Fantarie, who last season ran
two good races at Bnndwick, outpaced the Mal c ter
horse Three Star and leaded the bacon to the
accompaniment of a hallelujah chorus from the
fielders, few of whom had been asked to even put
a price on him. There was no excuse for Meroo.
He was well placed at the turn, but declined to
come on. R'shford was never properlv in th°
procession. Th’s page last week reckoned him well
in the Metropolitan. This week it suspects itself
of having spoken in a hurry.

The Chosen were hooked on the chin nt
Randwiek on Saturday. After the last race they
started as usual to bet on the Ensom, Metron., the
two Cups—in fact, anything on which a wager eo”ld
be made —and were doing it till an order went out
that operations must cease. No matter what may be
alleged to the contrary, the sole reason for this
action is to be found in the smell left behind by
some of the recent figlits here, especially one in which
the lamentable Buck Crouse, povv homeward bound
to Dollarland, had figured. And Crouse got “sealed,”
too. The Children will have to tall on the push
behind this sort of thing or lose favorable chances
of carrying on their legitimate business.

The South Aus. three-year-olds cannot
settle which of them is the best. Hamilton’s Bour-
lang has it sometimes, and at others Hawker’s
Boontree, while Mostyn Owen’s Marmur is always
handy to support, or divide, thp others. Boontree
is a hard-working filly, having had 18 races as a
two-year-old, and thrived on them. At Adelaide
Tattersall’s meeting, the Hurdles winner paid a
a. 59 14s. dividend (in a field of 15) ; and then
there was left 275. as a consolation for each backer
of the bad second. Other pleasant remarks made by
the totalisator on the same afternoon: £2l 18s.,
£l3 35., and £7 11s. for a second.
* * * *
Adelaide Hunt Club is going to miss its
annual race meeting this year, for the best of all
reasons —-that practically all of its riding members
are at the Front. The club has been in existence for
just on half a century, and has never had a break
till now. Just as the anti-sport party began to
rejoice over this bit of news the club undertook to
run a Patriotic meeting instead. All the other
race-bodies are helping, and it will be a much
bigger thing than the “picnic” fixture abandoned.
“T. 0.1.”: A name of note has dropped out
of the racing lists through the death of Albert L.
Yuille. He was the younger son of the late W. C.
Yuille, owner of Flj'ing Buck, winner of the first
Champion Race of Australia at Flemington in 1859,
subsequently a sporting writer of note under the
pen-name “Peeping Tom,” and the founder of the
stock-selling firm of W. C. Yuille and Co. The senior
was a very old Victorian colonist, having come over
from Tasmania in 1837; he was at the first race-
meeting at Batman’s Hill, where Spencer Railway
Station is now. In 1840 he went to Maoriland, and
was present when Captain Hobson took possession in
the name of the British Government. The son, just
dead, was a partner of the firm of W. C. Yuille and
Co., Kirk’s Bazaar. In his young days he was a cood
cricketer, later on a golfer, and between times the
owner of some well-known horses.

“0.K.”: An interpleader motion just
decided in Hobart should serve as a warning to all
possible winners of Tatt.’s sweeps. A recruiting-
officer in Adelaide drew Amata in the last sweep on
the Newmarket Handicap. Being unlearned in racing
matters he hastily wired to Tatt.'s requesting that
ever-ready go-between to lay the owner of Amata
£lOOO to nix against his horse. The mare was. then
quoted at 10 to 1 in the betting, so the recruiting-
officer was practically chucking a hundred golden
jimmies at squatter Landale. The owner and stable
had backed Amata; the mare was reckoned as good
a thing as she proved to be; and the bribe of
£lOOO to nix was quite uncalled for. Nevertheless
Tatt.'s instructed their Melbourne agent to find the
wealthy owner of Amata and make him a provi-
sional prrsent of the £lOOO which the unwealthy
ticket-holder was ready to sacrifice. Tatt.’s might
have told him not to be a darned fool; but instead
of offering this good advice they aided the juggins in
his foolishness. The agent couldn't “approach”
owner Landale, that gilded sport being in England,
so he placed the gratuity wager with Mr. Baillieu,
who represents friend Landale. Meanwhile somebody
in Adelaide had warned the ticket-holder of his
follv, a"d he wired to Tatt.'s countermanding his
“lay-off.” Too late! Mr. Baillieu had already in-
structed Tatt.’s agert to return thanks to the jug-
gins who had chucked a lot of morev at a rich man;
but, it appears, the thanks of Baillieu didn’t come
to hand in Hobart until the countermanding tele-
gram had arrived. Tatt.'s paid the £lOOO to Lan-
dale. ’The holder of the ticket proceeded against
Tatt.’s. But the Judge, on interpleader, ordered
Landale to be substituted for Tatt.'s in the princi-
pal action; and that’s as far as the matter has gone
at present.

“Mock”: Old nags, is it? Well, the
year-old moke mentioned bv “Wart” (B. 17/8/T6)
was cut off in its prime. Nineteen years ago I was
present at the execution, and subsequent obsequies of
a mare that had lived a useful life of 32 years and
five months. She belonged to mv father, who bred
her and fired the last shot. If “Wart” plods through
the files of the Warrnambool (Vic.) papers of '6B and
’69 he will find in the racing news of tho=e years
records of a bay mare Marv Ann, by Mariner, re-
turned as winner of more than one steeplechase at
Warrnambool and district meetings. In one steeple
Joe Thompson offered £3O to a shilling against her,
and Dad rushed in and had a counle of bob’s worth.
Mary won by half a furlong after that fine old-time
Western District ’chaser Lion had broken his neck at
the last fence. After that mare was 21 I rode her
over fences on the old Wimmera farm.
* * * *
“Hambidge”: From “Davy’s” yarn (B.
31/8/ 16) it appears that he considers 35 years a
good age for a horse. Let me bring Pollv into the
ring. At one time she was the best hack in the
district. She would do two horses’ work all day, and
at night scrape a living off a paddock that a cater-
pillar couldn't have found breakfast on. Besides this
she had a wonderful soul. She would stand and look
at nothing for hours with a faraway, sad look in her
eyes, and a kind smile round the corners of her
rrionth. Polly had a family of two. One tried to
push a barb-wire fence down and died ; the other we
shot five years ago because, being then 34, he was too
old to work. Pollv, his mother, had been pensioned
off in a paddock, but climbed a 4ft. fence and found
her way to the pound. There she was sold, and six
months ago she was delivering chops for a butcher.
» # * *
Darcy had Jimmy Clabby for an enemy at
Sydney Stadium on Saturday, and the fray was fully
as exciting as a sewing-bee. The crowd went to see
a scrap for the championship. What it did see was
a desperate struggle by Clabby to get possession of
Darcy's gloves, and he kept on doing it from the
first round to the last, except when Scott dragged
him away, and then he started to do it again. As
an exhibition of boxing the thing was just plain
farce. Occasionally the crowd gave it the count, most
of the time it cursed the early-closing arrangement
that had cut the beer off, and in between it veiled
advice to the referee. There were whole rounds in
which Clabby scarcely struck a blow, and in which
Dare.v did nothing hut wrestle to get his hands free.
Sometimes the disturbance opened un for a few
seconds, and on these rare occasions there was only
one in it. In the thirteenth the Yank had a torrid
time, and was only saved by the hell. In the last
he was so weak that he could scarcelv stand. He had
not won a round. It was an inexpressibly dreary
evening.

“Silas Snell”: This is how Bob told it:
“Me an’ Juddy was trapsin’ on the sand, nothin’ t’
do, when Juddy spots a dainty darlin’ chuckin’ chips
in the sea, an’ he gits to it. Juddy’s big, an’ he’s
bulky, an’ his ’air .curls. He’s an intoxicatin’ lot
with women is Juddy. He puts his ’at a little over
his ear. ‘ ’Ow is it V sez he. ‘Bit iv a nice, fine
day fer a gad round with one yeh love,’ sez he. The
girl looked him over. ‘Wash out yer earsl’ sez she, an’
starts off. Juddy is after her, an’ I’m with Juddy.
Juddy keeps- up his pleasin’ talk, tellin’ her about
her pretty ’air an’ all. Sez she: ‘Me ’usband's at the
boat’ouse. He’ll about stiffen you.’ Does Juddy fall off ?
Not he 1 Juddy tells her about her neat heels, an’
sez her waist’s somethin’ lovable. ‘Me ’usband’ll
rough the 'ead off yeh!’ sez the lady. ‘l’m lookin’
forward to it,’ sez Juddy. Well, we comes to the
boat’ouse, ’n’ she ducks in. Juddy looks the scenery
over, an’ whistles like a bird, an’ out comes me lady
with the ’usband. S’elp me two-’n’-tuppence, he’s
five-foot-five, an' much too thick fer active hostilities.
Juddy an’ me larfed our ’eads off at the idear of this
objec’ beltin’ Juddy. And but fer one thing the
thort of that little, fat elder misusin’ Juddy would
still be givih’ us a joy fit.” “What was the one
thing, Bob?” I asked. “Why, the cow, he rolled in
an’ well done it!” said Bob.
REFEREE QUIRK praying for guidance
(Melb. Stadium).
Young Walter Lindrum, whilst practising
for his forthcoming match with brother 1- red, put up
a great performance the other afternoon. He made
a break of 354 in about twenty minutes. Then he
began afresh with the balls at the top of the table,
and kept them there whilst he was scoring a break
of 820. By this time he was dead tired, and
broke down at an easy shot. Walter’s technique is
though: to be not quite so correct as his brother’s,
but the proof of the style is in.the scoring, and a boy
who can run up a break of 820, with 40 consecutive
nursery cannons in it, is a law unto himself.

Iv. Harbison, an East Maitland lad, did
some fine lea >ii.g at the combined High School cham-

At Sydney High School athletic champion-
ship competitions last week R. F. Cripps (Newington)
won the 100yds., 220yds., 440yds., 880yds. and the
broad jump, and finished second in the high jump
and 120yds. hurdles. The 220yds. in 24sec. was a
particularly smart effort. Cripps has been the New-
ington top-notcher for three years. E. W. Carr
(Sydney Grammar) won over 100yds., 440yds.,
880vrls. and broad jump. L. F. Claremont (Sydney
High) was first over 100yds., 220yds., broad jump,
and was second in 120yds. hurdles. R, Honner (St.
Joseph’s) had a day-out, too, scooping 100yds.,
220vds., broad jump and hop, step and jump, and
finishing second in four other events. Still the fool-
ishness of allowing young athletes to run excessive
risks needs curtailment. Athletes develop lung and
muscle —and sometimes play Old Harry with the
heart. Unless a curb is put on some of these young
enthusiasts their future will not extend beyond school-
days. The A.A.A. would do something useful if it
gave this phase of the matter a little consideration.

Bob Spears, of Dubbo (N.S.W.), con-
tinues to stop the Yank riders from acquiring aU the
prize-money on their cycle tracks. After finishing
second to Frank Kramer in the world’s championship
Spears turned the tables in a special match at New
York; and last week he and R. J. McNamara
took the cash of the 100 kilo. (63J miles), teams
race, with other Australian batches in second and
third places. Spears’s crust seems to have butter
and jam on it these times. There are better men
eating bully beef. _

* * * *
N. S. Wales Cricket Association’s decision
to carry on premiership cricket during the season has
found no favor in other States. In Vie. and S. Aus.
the game will struggle along on last year’s lines,
which mostly means the accumulation of moth and
rust. It looks as if cricket will have to start again
from somewhere near the beginning.
* * * *
The attenuated Apperly has writhed his
way through the final of another golf championship,
adding the scalp of Tom Smith, of Kensington (Syd-
ney), to the string already at his belt. On the long
course Apperly smote ’em with tortuous sweeps. Also
he unwound himself after each drive without serious
complications. If Apperly gets a shred thinner or
longer or adds any more loops to that swing of his
he will, for a certainty, do himself up into a plait
that nobody will be able to untwist. Meantime, he
sits on the top rung of the N.S.W. golfing ladder.
* * * *
That widely-known old salt, the late
Captain William Kenney, of Kenney’s Baths, St.
Kilda (Vic.), passed on his name to a son and a
grandson. The son still runs the old baths on the
Esplanade. Grandson Bill, of the 6th Field Ambu-
lance, was killed at Gallipoli. Now tlie news has
come through that Arthur Kenney, Bills only bro-
ther, went missing on the strenuous 19th of July.
Several generations of St. Kilda early morning dip-
pers will call around to say they are sorry.

He was lounging in a Bourke-street back
bar. He is an actor, but the public has not
yet arrived at the conclusion that he is
excelled by none, and his appearances on
the stage are rather inconspicuous. He was
talking as he leaned on the bar. He usually
is. He was toying with a cigar. His air
was that of a man of means very kindly
disposed towards comparatively humble sur-
roundings. “Yes.” he said, “the great num-
ber of forged five-pound notes about is a
public menace. Do you know, the boss tried
to give me three in my salary this morning!”
“De Oriel”: While the film censor argu-
ment rages I sampled a picture show re-
cently and found the screen occupied by a
film depicting the life of an artist’s model.
After many double exposures and fading
effects, showing the nude lady in a rather
ghostly manner, I suddenly sat up. Young
men in the audience craned their necks, old
fellow’s stood and elderly ladies made click-
ing noises. There upon the screen, and
without any humbug about it, the lady
stood wrapped simply in thought. And
the method of advertising was almost as
daring. All the time the film was showing,
a young person dressed and undressed
unceasingly behind a shadow-screen in an
adjacent arcade.
“Hamer”: Australia now sends away
£400,000 a year to the U.S.A. for pictures.
Occasionally an effort is made to put for-
ward an ambitious Australian film —like the
just-released “Mutiny of the Bounty”—
and the local work compares favorably with
the imported article. Financially, however,
the results are not encouraging, just as an
attempt to run a grocery store for two days
in a year might prove disastrous. The
only hope of re-establishing the business is
to give it very much greater Protection, for
the production of the £400,000 worth of
films, or even half of them, would supply
the local actor and operator with experience
and the boss with a steady enough demand
to make the business pay even at much-
reduced prices.
Another of the chaps from Bert Bailey’s
selection lias accepted an engagement to
play a part in “The Worst Kaiser in
Europe.” George Kensington, who origin-
ally hailed from M.L., goes into camp when
the Sydney season comes to an end. Fred
White has gone forward already; Beresford
Fowler is in khaki in Victoria.
It seems in human nature- for vocalists
to quarrel with their accompanists. In the
Warragul (Vic.) train the other night a
black man was warbling to the music of a
concertina played by a white brother. Of
course they quarrelled over the rendering of
the melody, and Abdallah was fined 40s. for
giving his fellow artist an eye as black as
himself.
“Yarra Ben” : While admitting that some
plays have been “let down” easily of late,
I can’t go arm in arm with “Edson” when
he says (B. 24/8/T6) : “Criticism of stage
plays in Melbourne is such that no man need
fear to be smitten with anything harder
than a puff ball.” Here is the opening of a
critique on the latest Williamson production
in one of the dailies: “Possibly the fact
that the company has been seen in Mel-
bourne in three very poor plays—the second
worse than the first and the third more
deplorable than either —accounted for the
only moderate house on Saturday night,
when ‘The Land of Promise’ was produced.”
One or two more “puff-balls” like this and
the managers will feel hurt.
The curtain has just fallen on Theodore
Hamilton, one of the first U.S. actors to
come this way. That was in IS7B. In
“Ingomar.” “Macbeth” and heavy plays he
was a convincing -mummer, as much like
the new school of slick Yankee drummer
actors as Billy Hughes is like Daniel Belte-
shazzar, the gentleman who didn’t revoke
when he was thrown into the den of lions.
“Jean S.”: Evidently the Yank comedians
of “The Follies of Pleasure” in Melbourne
are feeling the strain of providing unillumi-
nating cross-talk to fill in the intervals when
the comely chorus (which is the backbone of
the show) is off stage changing its clothes.
One of the visitors hints in the press that the
girls are too long away for his comfort.
“The Australian.” he groans, “cannot com-
pare with the American for smartness in get-
ting in and out of costumes.” In view of the
dash of the average Australian chorus girl,
the only explanation can be that the Yankee
chorus girl has much loss to get in and out
of.
SURGE OF SPRING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
VICTORIA.
My nib is gliding swift and free,
My pile of copy paper
Is trim, and tall, and fine to see,
My brain' is free of vapor.
I feel I am infused to-day
With Springtime’s doping leaven—
I’ll write the great Australian play:
1 will, so help me Heaven!
And not the looked-for play alone,
The long-expected story,
And sing a song in such a tone
They'll catch the strain in Glory.
I want a pen in either hand
To spray my vital forces ;
For Pegasus a prancing band
Of Wirths’ performing horses.
I feel disposed to rock the range,
To grasp the peaks, and twist ’em,
To lash the sea to antics strange,
And crack the solar system.
What rapturous creative throes
This season’s stir affords man!
The penman while the red ink flows
Is mightier than the swordsman.
I’ll write an ode to thrill the horde
As bulky as a stilton ;
I’ll turn out epics by the cord,
And do in Mr. Milton.
My fountain-pen's exuberant flow
Would drain the Bay of Biscay.
Heigho! That’s finished. Now I’ll go
And have a peg of whisky.
EDWARD DYSON.

How to Treat Your Hair and Complexion
A Few Simple Beauty Hints.
By Mdlle. GABY DESLYS, the well-known Parisian Actress.
YOU ask rue for a few hints on the
treatment of the hair and complexion.
Well, the less “treatment” you give
the skin the better. I do not
believe much in massage, but a
little cream to the face is neces-
sary to counteract the effects of
wind or sun. What cream
would I recommend? Well, I
advise you to us 6 a little mer-
colised wax every night and
again in the morning, after-
washing the face. Ilub it
gently into the skin, then wipe
off any superfluous wax and
dust a little barri-agar over
the face. You will find that
this will be the only “treat-
ment” necessary and will keep
your face fresh and youthful-
looking for all your life. The mercolised
wax removes all the dead outer skin, so
that you have always a fair, fresh com-
plexion like a girl’s.
For the hair, the first and most important
Photo: 11 'rather it Buys.
thing is a good shampoo. Never use any*
thing inferior to wash the hair with. Get
some good stallax from your chemist and use
a teaspoonful in a cup of hot
water. Then rinse the hair
well and it will look bright and
glossy.
A tonic is necessary when the
hair is inclined to fall out more
than it should, and is always
good to use during the spring
and autumn. Then the hair
needs a little —what do you call
it?—stimulant, and for this 1
would advise you to get a packet
of boranium and mix it with
some bay rum ; dab this into the
roots and it will not only stop
the fall, but make your hair
grow long and thick, (live your
hail - a good brushing every night, and that
will be all that you need do.

Blackheads Fly Away.
Instantaneous Remedy for Blackheads, Greasy Skin and Enlarged Pores.
A practically instantaneous remedy for
blackheads, greasy skins and enlarged pores,
recently discovered, is now coming into
general use in the boudoir. It is very
simple, harmless and pleasant. Drop a
stymol tablet, obtained at the chemist’s, in
a tumblerful of hot water. After the effer-
vescence has subsided bathe the face in the
liquid, using a small sponge or soft cloth.
lii a few minutes dry the face and the offen-
sive blackheads will come right off on the
towel. Also the large oily pores immedi-
ately close up and efface themselves natu-
rally. The greasiness disappears and the
skin is left smooth, soft and cool. This
simple treatment is then repeated a few
times at intervals of four or five days, to
ensure the permanence of the result.
drey Hair—Home Remedy.
An old-fashioned Recipe restores Youthful Appearance.
There are plenty of reasons why grey
hair is not desirable, and plenty of reasons
why hair dyes should not be used. But, on
the other hand, there is no reason why you
should have grey hair if you do not want
it. To turn the hair back to a natural color
is really a very simple matter. One has
only to get from the chemist two ounces of
concentrate of tammalite and mix it with
three ounces of bay rum. Apply to the
hair with a small sponge for a few nights
and the greyness will gradually disappear.
This liquid is not sticky or greasy and does
not injure the hair in any way. It has been
used for generations with most satisfactory
results by those who have known the
formula.
To Kill Roots of Superfluous hair.
The most Effective Formula ever Discovered.
Women annoyed with disfiguring growths
of superfluous hair wish to'know not merely
how to temporarily remove the hair, but how
to kill the hair roots permanently. For
this purpose pure powdered pheminol may be
applied directly to the objectionable hair
growth. The recommended treatment is
designed not only to instantly remove the
hair but also to actually kill the roots so that
the growth will not return. About an ounce
of pheminol, obtainable from the chemist,
should be sufficient. 4'
Good News for Fat People.
Something New in Obesity Cures.
A London chemist says: “The latest
method of reducing obesity certainly is far
more pleasant and convenient than all pre-
vious methods. It consists merely in eating
clynol berries. The fat person who wants
to reduce without the usual rigid diet, exer-
cise, sweating baths, etc., now puts a few
of these little brown berries in his or her
pocket and eats three or four each day.”
Clynol berries not only eliminate fat from
the body, but also correct the tendency,
which is usually constitutional, to create
fatty matter. No discomfort whatever is
caused by their action; in fact, except for
the loss of superfluous fat and the feeling
of “fitness’-’ so created, you would not be
aware that these little berries were doing
their work.
Local inquiry shows that clynol berries are
not very well known in this country, but
the demand is increasing daily, and any
chemist can quickly procure them if specially
requested to do so.

Efficiency and economy! Premier Hol-
man, for instance, opened an Aviation School
at Richmond (N.S.W.) last week, and
incidentally provided cold poultry and trim-
ming for 450 of the Best Fed. The national
advantage of feeding the Best Fed at the
, public expense is, of course, clear to every-
one.
“Assam”: The idea of extending the
early-closing principle to drapers’ shops
deserves support. In our district a fur coat
is called a ‘‘soldier’s allowance.” Damsels
who before the war were modestly-habited
young tilings are now unrecognisable as
they pass down the street —women of
fashion and frill, either married and living
on their absent soldier, or unmarried and
waiting to see if their choice comes back
unmaimed, and sharing his six bob a day
in the meantime. What? No nice girl would?
They’re all nice to the draper. In one
hcuse near here, where there are still sev-
eral eligibles in work, the mother draws
her one enlisted son’s pay, and most of it
goes in glad rags. The daughter is “en-
. gaged” to a young man at the Front. She
gets a whack of his pay and spends it on
pretties—or what she considers pretties.
And at the shack in the district where most
often tin* “benevolent” visitor used to call
before the war, the walls are now festooned
with fal-lals, bought by the money of a
couple of relatives who are in the shell-
z< ne. The fighting man goes away to do
his bit for Australia; and the girl lie leaves
behind him does her bit to make things as
bad as she can for Bill when he comes home.
For that, of course, is what the purchase
of fal-lals with borrowed money amounts to.
Poor old Bill will have to pay the Nil
presently—the things are really only got on
tick.

Phillips Brooks —that man of keen perceptions
—once said: “A school song in the heart of a
child will do as much for his character as a fact
in his memory,or a principle in his intellect.” In
other words, music, as a means of culture, is a
potent factor in human civilisation. This being
true, how essential it becomes that the young
mind should drink in nothing but the best! Let
him practise, certainly, but not rely on his own
puny efforts as a stimulus to imagination.
& BEALE PLAYER PIANO
combines in one instrument the best means
of attaining ideal results. The finest compo-
sitions in the musical world can be played on
the roll and used to impress on the child’s mind
the full beauty of correct rhythm and tone-
color. Aside from the enjoyment you will
personally derive from the instrument this
educational value is a highly important
feature of the “Beale” Player Piano. And at
all times it is available for hand-playing.
The “Beale ” Patent Tuning System ensures
an instrument always in tune—and this
despite the great amount of use a Player
always gets. If you will call at our Show-
rooms, we shall have pleasure in demon-
strating the instrument.

Legal Notices.
U/ HEREAS by an Order of the High Court of
vv Justice Chancery Division made in the matter
of the Estate of FREDERICK AUGUSTUS LAW-
RENCE deceased and in an action of Lawrence v.
Fitch & Others (1916 L. 820) the following inquiry
was directed namely:—
“1. An inquiry who upon the death on the 9th
May 1915 of the Intestate Frederick Augustus
Lawrence deceased became beneficially entitled to
any personal estate of his as to which he died
intestate and if more than one in what shares and
proportions and whether any such persons are since
dead and if so who are their legal personal repre-
sentatives.”
NOTICE is hereby given that any person or
persons claiming to be entitled under the said
inquiry is or are by himself or themselves or by his
or their Solicitors on or before the 16th day of
December 1916 to come in and prove their claims at
the Chambers of Mr. Justice Sargant and Mr. Justice
Younger at the Royal Courts of Justice, Strand,
London, England, and to enter their names and full
particulars of their claims in a book kept for that
purpose in Room No. 299 at the said Royal Courts
of Justice or in default thereof they will be peremp-
torily excluded from the benefit of the said Order.
Thursday, the 21st day of December 1916 at 12
o’clock noon at the said Chambers, Room No. 297, is
appointed for hearing and adjudicating upon the
claims.
Dated this 25th day of July 1916.
RICHD. WHITE,
Master.
NOTE: LAURA LOUISA MORLEY, a sister of the
said Frederick Augustus Lawrence was living, in
Athens in or about the year 1899 and has not since
been heard of.
EDWARD LAWRENCE FITCH, a nephew of the
said Frederick Augustus Lawrence left England in
the year 1884 and went to reside in Australia. He
has not been heard of since 1897 when he was then
residing at Roebourne West Australia.
FINNIS, DOWNEY, LINNELL & CHESSHER,
Solicitors, 5 Clifford Street, London, W.
Or TLET
Deep Well Pumping Engines
Cook’s Deep Well Pumping Engines
for Non-flowing Artesian or Tube Wells
have the following special features:
No Dead Centre —will always start when the steam is turned
Perfectly Uniform Motion, due to separate adjustable valves
for up and down strokes.
Flow of Water Steady and Continuous, the pump bucket
discharging on the upper stroke and the top plunger dis-
rharging on the down stroke. .

There’s no doubting the honorable inten-
tions of this advertiser in a highly-respect-
able old paper at Ipswich:—
WORKING MAN, 42, wishes to correspond with
Woman, 80 to 35, view maternity. Address: ,
this Office.
Queensland Treasury returns show that
the State is £183,000 worse off at the end
of August than it was at the corresponding-
time last year. But that doesn’t matter.
The other day the Treasury committed itself
to about half a million for the purchase of the
Chillagoe railway—a dubious asset —and
about £200,000 for the purchase of Cecil
Plains, which may easily prove another
Jimbour. Financial ability in the Northern
State consists mainly in a delightful uncon-
cern as to the significance of figures.
Fihelly’s Bill in the Bananaland Assembly
to abolish capital punishment is Kingstonian
in its brevity and directness. “The sen-
tence of punishment by death shall be no
longer pronounced or recorded, and the
punishment of death shall be no longer
inflicted,” is the simple, unadorned clause
that does the trick.
There is trouble up North, too. Minister
John Adamson accepted the position of vice-
president of the Universal Service League
and has been carpeted by the Caucus.
Abandon the League or the portfolio —that
is alleged to be the machine’s ultimatum.
What sort of a Labor party would we have
had to-day if the workers had put a muzzle
on the minority 25 years ago? Every re-
form that the world ever saw sprang out of
a miserable minority; and the moment you
shut the mouth of minorities that moment
progress begins to stop.
Bowen has become joyous now that the
erection of the big Elliott copper-refinery has
begun. A smelter is also to be erected to
treat the refinery residues. When the coal
deposits on the river are worked Bowen will
be well on the way to become a busy indus-
trial port. Townsville, full of jealousy, is
gnashing its teeth and anxiously looking
around for a benefactor with the old Philp
pull. But alas! times have sadly changed.
How worthless are the penalty clauses in
the Arbitration Acts has been demonstrated
once more by the Maekay wharf-lumpers.
A few dozen of them struck over a small
matter, and 20,000 people were reduced to
the verge of starvation. Of course, it was
nobody’s business —least of all the Minister’s
—to intervene in such a situation. The idea
that the Government represents the com-
munity and that a community has rights
never seems to occur to Ministers when a
handful of employees and employers start
choking one another.
The Referendum Bill before the Assembly
is full of possibilities There is a provision
giving the early-closing movement priority
of hearing. But as the licensed victuallers
were first in with their petition signed by
86,000 electors, the question arises whether
they are not entitled to put the first question,
in which case Bung by clever manoeuvring
might force Cold Tea into combating his
proposal instead of merely clamoring for six
o’clock.
While in Brisbane Australia’s forester
G -G. had a heart-to-heart talk with the
people in the timber trade. Incidentally his
Ex. supplied the information that his shack
was almost entirely decked out with red
cedar. “Polished,” quoth he, “it is a
beautiful wood ; yet your settlers here build
sheds with it and smother its graces with
paint!”
Some genius in Queensland wanted to
build a house the other day, and he reckoned
he would do the thing properly and hire
three architects instead of one. It might
have answered if the three had worked
together; but he hired one to design the
foundations and another to attend to the
first floor and the third to put his ideas into
the second storey. As they didn’t consult
with one another there is just the sort of
mess that might have been expected. Any-
how, if nobody was mad enough to build a
house that way, it is exactly what has
happened in connection with the sugar in-
dustry. Somebody fixed the rates of wages,
and somebody else fixed the price of cane,
and a third party decided what sugar should
be sold for; and incidentally there are
troubles over the wharf-lumping of sugar, and
the whole business is bubbling and seething.
THE INSULT.
SISSY AH W ONG: “Give its a bit.”
BILLY AN SONG: "Will I?”
SISSY AH WONG: “Go on, yer dirty Jew.”
“Itau” on the vegetable Hun: —
Queensland Department of Lands now admits that
prickly-pear is its master. Officers told the Public
Works Commission the other day -that every known
poison had been tried, and that the only visible
hope now lay in Oliver Cromwell Roberts’s gas. The
official estimate of abandoned country is a trifle under
4,000,000 acres, and a further 14,000,000 acres will
soon have to be included. Roughly, 1,000,000 acres
are captured by the curse every year. With
Roberts’s gas there are drawbacks; it is not very
effective during dry weather, and the war has cut
off the supply of an essential in its produc-
tion. The Under-Sec. was asked if the build-
ing of railways would help, but he instanced
areas which had been abandoned quite close
to existing lines. The real trouble is that the pear
fight has not been enthusiastically conducted. Fur-
ther, nearly every district has its peculiar features,
and these must be considered when planning attacks.
Clearly one of the first jobs of the Federal Bureau of
Science and Industry should be to see if the pear
cannot be made commercially useful. It is known to
possess certain elements which can be extracted.
Certainly something must be done. Even if only the
million acres now eaten up every year were saved
it would be a great achievement.
The State purchase of Cecil Plains recalls
the palmy days of Bananaland squattocracy.
It and Jimbour, close at hand, were centres
of political intrigue in the Mcllwraith-
Griffith days. It was there, too, that about
a dozen men were drowned during a hood,
one of the survivors being the present Min-
ister for Lands. The run is to be used by
the Government in an attempt to supply
Brisbane and other centres with a cheap
State chop. Drought hits the district hard
at times, but Ministers say they are going to
show what can be done by fodder conserva-
tion.
“Paddymelou” writes a page of history in
advance: —•
About the year 1918 the pricklv-pcar problem
became intolerably acute in Queensland. The State
in desperation resorted to an old trick and imported
an “expert” from the U.S.A, When he arrived the
expert set to work to peruse volumes of press clip-
pings on the subject in order that he might learn
something about his job. Eventually he decided to
put the curse through a chaff-cutter and drop the
minced pear into a silo. Then he gave forth the
startling discovery that prickly pear made good
ensilage for pigs. Queensland is now the greatest
hog-producing country in the world, and pear land is
at a premium. It has since come to light that the
Yank’s plan was suggested by a writer in the
Brisbane press in 1912, but as he was merely an
Australian nobody listened to him.
Died at Innisfail, Francis Meyrick
Armstrong, one of the men who really did
something to develop the North. Armstrong
lived on the Johnstone River for about 30
years, conducting the agency of the A.U.S.N.
Co., goldmining and sugar farming, the
latter extensively and successfully. He
had a good heart and was one of the best
all-round sports up yonder.
The owners of Cecil Plains, the big Dalby
freehold, made a sort of coupon deal with
the Ryan Government. The price to be
paid is £2 per acre; but the vendors are
lending the Government the purchase money
for 25 years at 4 per cent., and are also
providing £lOO,OOO at 41 per cent, to help
build a railway to the property. So the
Government, in a way, fulfils one portion of
the Labor programme by nationalising land
and simultaneously smashes another plank
by performing the trick with a P.N. The
P.N. is the recognised currency of Labor
Governments now. They get rid of rent,
which could be reduced by taxation, and
fasten on to the shoulders of the people
interest, which can’t be reduced by anything.
Returned.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When low 1 bent in the Anzac trench
1 pined for a glass and the sight of a wench;
I damned myself for the fool I’d been
To leave the Downs for a warlike scene;
And vowed that when I got home again
You’d take my lot of the risk and pain.
I’d have my share of the girls and fun,
And you could collar my cursed gun,
And all the honor that was my due
I'd hand it on as a gift to you.
But now I’m back on the Downs once more.
And smoke my pipe with a genial score,
There’s something peggin’ away inside,
A curious thing that I thought had died.
It says: “Get back to your mates, old man,
And do your bit, as you know you can;
It's better to share in the shot and shell.
And taste the broth that is brewed in Hell
Than dodge the brunt of the Teuton-Turk
And laze at home with the crowd who shirk.
“It’s better to die for the boys you knew
Than loaf at home while they die for you.”
QUEENSLAND.
P. AIREY.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, September 9, 1916.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
I wouldn’t have had a thing to tell you
to make your hair curl or your flesh creep
if on Saturday Lady Goojd-Adams hadn t
opened a Wistaria Fete in aid of the annual
appeal for the funds of the Salv’army.
Other and similar outbreaks occurred in
most of the suburbs; so you can guess what
an exciting time we’ve had.
The open-air concert, which was post-
poned from last week in consequence of the
tearful state of the weather, happened at
the Exhibition yesterday, and was about
tne last spasm of a long-drawn-out “Do
Without Week.” There was a big crowd,
which made the Patriotic Fund Ist. lolb.
heavier.
The Residential Club for Returned Sol-
diers and Sailors will soon be hatched.
Arrangements have been completed for the
purchase of Corona, Dr. Peter Bancroft’s
old house —a valuable property in a central
position. Meantime, while waiting for the
present tenant’s lease to run out, the com-
mittee is arranging a carnival, to be held in
October. The weary old ‘‘Queen” business
is being worked over again. On Monday
Lady Goold-Adams is to open a Japanese
tea-house in Market Square, to help run up
the cash takings for the Citizens’ candidate,
Mrs. Hetherington.
A story comes from France that a Ghurka
was describing the temple of some Australian
cobbers. “A large iron pipe hangs down,
with a round end, full of holes. When they
come from the trenches all the Australians,
one by one, strip off their clothes, and wor-
ship the iron thing, from which water spurts.
They repeat this prayer: ‘Oo’ Ah. By
Cripes. Bosker!’ ”
Nerida Brown, who has been distinguish-
ing herself as an amateur singer with more
than ordinary possibilities, left for Sydney
this week, and will study at the Con-
servatorium. We think Miss Brown is a
clever actress, too, and we are going to
hear more of her, or blame you for spoiling
her.
Passed out last week, the widow of
Robert Cran, who, away back in the ’6o’s,
started a sugar-refinery at Yengarie. When
things began to peter out on the Mary he
migrated to Bundaberg and laid the founda-
tions of the big Millaquin mill. The old
lady, who shared her husband’s vigorous
pioneering, had survived 94 years of North-
ern climate.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.

“S.S.” : I overheard it in Port Melbourne.
“Now, there’s a ’appy marriage, if you like.
I’ve known ’em for seven year, and they
always get drunk together.’’
The stripped hulls of the two ancient
torpedo-boats, the Lonsdale and the Nepean,
relics of the days when Victoria had a navy
of her own, are lying in the mud at Swan
Island. _ Tenders were some years ago called
for their purchase, but nobody seems to
want them.
A lady with a name like Victoria Plan-
tagenet, charged with having no “lawful
visible,” seemed to have a sporting chance of
acquittal the other day in a Sydney court
until the Law produced the last straw.
“Have you,” it asked, “the picture of a man
tattooed on your leg?” “I have,” modestly
admitted Victoria. That settled it. The
mind of the wavering Beak was cleared of
doubt and he handed out the usual. This
raises some alarming apprehensions of what
may happen to a very great potentate if he
happens to be arrested for, say, watering his
garden. Naturally he would deny the
allegation, and then the Force would ask him
if he had tattooed on himself a . But,
thank Heaven, the press of Australia knows
its duty and would draw a veil over the
dreadful details.
The Gott-fearing Kaiser lately advised his
people to repair to church and pray for vic-
tory. Now we know why he has made a
hobby of destroying cathedrals. He has an
idea of cutting off his adversaries’ communi-
cations with Heaven.
From London:—
Dr. Golding-Bird, Bishop of Kalgoorlie, is still
playing his favorite rOle of episcopal cadger and
libeller of the land that has given him an apron.
The latest issue of the London Church Times con-
tains a long wail from his pen regarding the “very
low standard of morality” in the Westralian town-
ships and the poverty of his diocese. In one mining
township he found “only one family witnessing for
righteousness” and providing “the only bit of religion
in the place.” If he only had more money and men
Westralia would not be going headlong to Hades
in this fashion; but his diocese would immediately
“collapse” if the annual grant from the Society for
the Propagation of the Gospel were withdrawn. So
here is an Australian Anglican bishopric depending for
its existence upon a precarious dole from a British
Society. Who is responsible for the running-up of
these jerry-built bishoprics that cannot maintain
their perpendicular unless they are buttressed by
British charitable donations ? As a matter of self-
respect and with a patriotic regard for the good name
of Australia abroad, the Church of England people of
the Commonwealth ought to put an end to this
scandal.
“Adelaide” : “Sapper,” who saw Kitchener
speeding by in a fast motor (B. 10/8/T6),
had better luck than half of Adelaide’s best-
known people. They secured invitation-cards
for the Saluting Base, and waited in the
front seats under the flag to see the show.
The great man came on to the vast flat sur-
rounded by officers. He gave a fond atten-
tion to the veterans, had a good look at the
cadets, and then—-with other appointments
waiting—rode round the main body of the
local army, instead of asking it to march
past him. Then he was gone, having never
been near the Saluting Base at all. The
crowd saw lots of him, the invited guests
nothing. So Democracy scored again.
An Australian firm has lately made and
sold 100 small farm-engines of a type pre-
viously bought by the same concern in the
U.S.A. Now the second hundred are well on
the way. The magnetos are imported, but
all other parts are made in Australia of
Australian raw material. Under the exist-
ing tariff, before freights went sky-high, it
was possible to import and sell the little
Yankee engine at £35. The Australian en-
gine costs £4O. It will be a howling injus-
tice to a lot of people if the inadequacy of
the post-war tariff permits the squashing of
activities like these. Yet Frank Tudor, to
judge by his growing tendency to jest upon
public occasions, isn’t fretting his soul over
the indifference of his brethren in Parlia-
ment to a matter to which, at last general
election, most of them promised personal
attention.
How a Maoriland woman is a sister to
Billjim :—-
Ettie Rout, who startled officialdom in Maoriland
by organising a volunteer nursing sisterhood and
left the Dominion without the blessing of the Coali-
tion press, Was, when Egypt wrote last, cooking in
the Soldiers’ Club at Tel-el-Kebir, with the thermo-
meter at 100 deg. in the shade. Billjim is.fond of blanc
mange and Miss Rout writes: “On Sunday the boys
excelled themselves by eating nine barrelfuls of
fruit salad (beer barrels), and two of blanc mange;
also a boiler of custard in the afternoon. This
included the evening's provisions, so at 5 o’clock I
made a boiler of sago custard —50 eggs, a bucketful
of sago, half a cup of vanilla, and water to match.
They went through that in five minutes. It was
’like watching a Are in the tussocks.”
A lately deceased down Brighton way was
followed to the grave by two weeping widows.
No, he was neither a Mormon nor a
Mohammedan. He had divorced his first
wife, but she bore him no malice, for when
he dropped off she not only put on mourning
and went to the funeral, but took her No. 2
along as well. So there was a square party.
Up Rochester way, where a “shire queen”
competition is raging, they have a cheap and
easy way of raising vote money. About
30 gunners foregather at an appointed place,
arrange themselves in a semi-circle and then
march over the paddocks shooting every hare
that scuttles before them. The fur is
gathered by a buyer who follows the battue,
giving up to Is. lOd. a pair for the bag,
which goes into cold-storage. And that's
where Toorak gets its “turtle” soup from
when it has had a win at Flemington.
It is doubtful if the owner of a cough ever
felt so embarrassed as one did the other
evening at a lecture given to a select
audience by the wife of a Melbourne
doctor. The cough, though persistent,
was so apologetic. Still, it seemed to
get on the nerves of the lecturer, who, about
half-way through, with an “Excuse me.”
dashed over to the chairwoman’s table. Fork-
ing something from a bag she held it towards
the owner of the cough, who stepped meekly
forward to receive the gift. It was a packet
of cough-drops.

The Story of the Lamp
>3
ifiSu
:igr
In Ancient Babylon, 3000 B.C.
The men of the “stone age” period used
lamps fashioned in stone. The earliest
known lamps were those discovered by
scientists amongst the excavations in Baby-
lonia and Assyria, on the Mesopotamian
plains. The lamps unearthed were of un-
glazed terra-cotta and excellent workman-
ship, taking various forms such as birds,
hands, and feet. It is not to be expected
that the Babylonians could produce a
burning oil so perfect as
Laurel Kerosene
The soft white light
Laurel gives a soft but brilliant light,
perfectly steady, without smoke or smell,
and is very restful to the eyes. Laurel is
our new trade mark name for the highest
quality American Kerosene ever sold in
Australasia and replaces our old brand,
White Rose.
Insist on Laurel
Vacuum Oil Company Pty. Ltd.
Throughout the Commonwealth and New Zealand

The Inky Way.
Frederick Charles East, who went to press
the other day in Christchurch (M.L.), at the
age of 71, had been writing his name on the
pay-sheet of the Lyttelton Times for 50
years. Before the end he had become chief
reader and ready for a rest. East rode the
winner of the second Derby run in Canter-
bury (Azucena, in ’63), was in the first
volunteer company thereabouts, was the local
pioneer in the use of photography for the
press, and had about the finest amateur’s
collection of ferns and orchids in the neigh-
borhood.
* * # *
T. J. (“Hoppy”) Kolian, who got in the
limelight lately in Sydney as plaintiff in a
case in which he sued other members of
the Board of the Typographical Association
for assault and for excluding him from
meetings, went into the Ass. when it took
over the Publishing Employees’ Union. He
looks after the publishing affairs of John
Haynes’s Newsletter. Kohan has oeen asso-
ciated with the Labor movement for years,
and earned a solid reputation for loquacity
at congresses and conferences. The last
time he ran as a Labor candidate was at
Petersham, where he gave J. J. Cohen a
fair run in an electorate that seemed hope-
less from a Labor standpoint.
* * # *
War is indeed Hell! London Sporting
Times, known for 40 years as the Pink
’Un, on account of the color of its paper—
it has long since registered the name as
part of its title —now has to come out on
virgin white stock, like its contemporaries.
There was, by the way, a dreadful two
months during which it was feared that
even The Bulletin’s cover would have to
cease to wear its bright blush. But an
ei ergetic agent in England grabbed paper
dyes promptly, then set to work to exchange
the dyes he didn’t want for the cerise that
The Bulletin did want; and so the im-
possible was made possible.
* * * *
The Christchurch Press (M.L.) is out
to give the birth-rate all the encouragement
it can. It has been running its birth ads.
under the heading ‘‘Roll of Honor.” But
being a journal which keeps an eye on the
morals of the local inhabitant, it has
thoughtfully added a column for Marriages.
* * * #
The war lias made some changes in the
Pinkstone family, ancient plodders on the
dusty Inky Way of rural N. S. Wales. Old-
man Pinkstone, who started the Cootamun-
dra Herald 40 years ago, recently retired;
and his eldest son, W.H., has put his own
paper, the Hawkesbury Herald, into the
hands of a manager while he takes charge at
Coota. in the interests mainly of four
brothers who have gone to the war.
* * * «
“F.N.T.”: “Spell-oli” (B. 17/8/’l6)
reckons the Australasian .Journalist a
“deplorable publication.” Nowadays the
Journalist is strictly a trade-journal of
20 pages. There are about 800 Australian
members of the A.J.A., and the price of the
journal is 3d. This means £lO, plus a trifle
of advt. money, to meet the cost of producing
the paper. Yet it pays its way. As regards
quality, the current issue contains about
7 per cent, of reprint. The balance is contrib-
uted matter —editorials, letters, reports, an-
nouncements, personal notes and a few
general pars, and technical witticisms. As
perhaps. 50 different pressmen are repre-
sented in those contributions, while the
publication is edited by a man who had a
good job on a Melbourne daily until he
became gen. sec. of the A.J.A., it looks as
though “Spell-oh” doesn’t know a good trade-
journal when he sees one.
* * * •
E. Dwyer Gray has retired from the job
of editing the Hobart Daily Post after a
few strenuous and stormy years, during the
hitter part of which the ship has had some
difficulty m keeping afloat. Sub-editor
McArthur is now telling the Kaiser what
the Post thinks of him.
SPRING AND THE REPORTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Intense cold followed on the spring-like weather
of yesterday. —Newspaper par.
Spring came creeping on a day at end of winter,
Peeped into the gardens and the orchards, ev’rywhere
Touched the naked branches —she’s a wondrous leaf-
bud tinter —•
With specks of living emerald and warmed the chilly
air.
Spring played hiding. Past the hedge-rows few
could see her,
Laughing through her tresses at the cold folk passing
by;
Now and then a poet stared, musing: “Can it be
her—
Little Spring, at this old time, and giving the Glad
Eye?”
Came one mortal (he was braver than his fellows),
Spied the leaflet-jewels, and so tracked her where she
lay
Soft in naked loveliness—a bed of greens and yellows
Underneath her whiteness—while her great eyes
laughed in play.
Spring, sweet-smiling, is a joy to make veins quicken.
’Spite his throbbing senses, our reporter—yea, in
truth !
Took out book and pencil ere his sullen thought
could sicken
Of its mundane purpose, and addressed Eternal Youth.
“My impressions?” quoth the laughing, luscious
maiden;
“Tell, was ever question a reporter would not ask?
Place too softly-sacred for his tread ?” “Ah, Blossom-
laden,
Help thou the unhappy; it is all his daily task.”
Spring sate, toying with her tresses’ raying goldness,
Rippling: “I’m the Bad Girl of the Seasons,” through
rich lips;
“Laughter-loving, living am I, tempting righteous
coldness—
And I've scribbled my ‘impressions’ on the branches’
budding tips.”
Spring, so saying, spread her hidden wings for flying,
Flashed her fairy limbs in air, and fluttered far
away.
Mortals guessed her going, for as home the youth
went, sighing,
He pulled his collar closely up—and cold winds blew
next day.
N.S.W.
HELEN BURKE.
