THE Hotel Carlton, completed at a cost exceeding £lOO,OOO,
and furnished sumptuously throughout by David Jones, Ltd.,
has, since its opening on March 30, 1916, been thronged
with crowds of delighted visitors -!
While its exquisite appointments—its perfect cuisine and courteous
service more than commend themselves to travellers used to every
comfort —the Hotel Carlton can hardly be described as being
merely the leading hotel.
It is more than that !
It is from every point of view the most desirable place in Sydney
to stay.
Every detail in the perfect attention to visitors’ wishes has been
thoroughly studied, and reflects the careful thoughtfulness of the
management, who have had a wide experience in catering for the
best class of patronage : !
One of the attractive features of the Hotel Carlton is that every
bedroom is naturally ventilated, unusually spacious, has hot and
cold water installed, and is artistically furnished.
It is absolutely fireproof, having marble staircases and concrete
floors throughout.
The general appointments include amongst others: Magnificent

and comfortable lounges, reception, smoking, writing and reading
rooms, also private rooms for weddings, dinner-parties, afternoon-

This Australian hero writes
All Australians prove themselves when put to the test. “So does
Rexona.”
I left Australia 11th May, 1915, and went to Gallipoli. After
months in the trenches at Quinn’s Post I was blown up by a mine exploding.
I also received nineteen (19) shrapnel wounds in my legs. I, like all our
Boys, received a Christmas Billy, and enclosed inside was a tin of Rexona.
\\ hile in the No. 3 A.G.H. at Heliopolis, Egypt, I used it frequently on my
wounds. While other remedies failed, Rexona completely cured them.
I am quite well again and able to take up my duties in life as before,
many thanks to the unsurpassable, healing and soothing powers of Rexona.
I assure one and all, any soldiers at the Front will be pleased with a tin of
Rexona. If he uses it at once he
will always use it.
I can recommend with confi-
dence Rexona, as I have given it
the fullest of tests, and, further-
more, I have sent several tins to
friends now serving in the A.I.E.
Forces.
Yours truly,
Ij.-Cpl. H. N. BROOKES,

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A HUSH came down, as of brooding
wings,
When the ripple of music stole
To the darkened hall, where the lights
burned low,
For the grey-haired wizard who held the
bow
Flayed not alone on the quivering strings;
He played on the human soul.
It sang as the ripple of waters sing
In the glimmer of dawn ashine;
A glad spring dawming of skies serene,
\vith woods, dew-spangled, of gold and
green,
W hen life itself was a glorious thing,
But love was a gift divine.
Of the great green forests at dawn it told,
And the song of the radiant rills;
Of the sunlight golden on slopes of green,
And the long cool shadows that fell between
lill the heart went wild for the blue and
gold,
And the wonder of the hills.
II sang of the glory of Summer ways
For the feet that could never tire,
Of the things that the spirit may not for-
get,
Till the women lifted their eyes, tear-wet,
And wandered again for a little space
In the garden of Heart’s Desire.
They set their feet by its shining streams
And the music called from sleep
One golden hour from the long ago,
The hour most perfect that earth can know,
The Dream, divinest of all earth’s dreams,
That the longing soul can keep.
The veil dropped down, and the past stood
clear
From the shadow of all the years;
But the memory clung, with a passionate
pain,
To the old dream, never to live again:
And the music quivered and faltered here,
Bike a human voice with tears.
But to some there came from the long-dead
past
Orim spirits, with brows afrown,
Who said, “We are yours, though you
buried us deep;
But the Wizard called, and we woke from
sleep,
And the royal roses and lilies massed
Were powerless to hold us down.”
Living the life that their own hands made,
They had honor and wealth, and yet
Love came but once—oh, her face was fair,
But the long grass tangles above her hair!
Yet never on earth will her memory fade
Or the spirit quite forget.
Then the music changed till it soared and
sang
As the voice of a soul made free,
When the heart’s desire is attained at last.
And storm and passion and strife are past;
And the royal rapture rose and rang
In a marvel of melody.
Then the bow sank down like a magic wand
And the music died away,
And the lights flared up in the silent hall,
And the listeners woke from the magic
thrall
As a man comes back from a golden land
To a cheerless world and grey.
N.S.W.
NELLIE A. EVANS.
Warnings and Prohibitions.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE ferry steamer was slipping merrily
down Sydney Harbor to thin music
and the busy beat of the engines. On
the glassed-in upper deck two working
mothers flanked by picnic baskets sat gos-
siping about flannel, bills, husbands’ beer,
operations, hats, sales, death, childbirth and
other matters too numerous to mention.
Three of their children were romping hap-
pily ; the fourth sat apart moving nothing
but his eyes, and glumly waiting for some-
thing or other, or nothing at all.
“W’y don’t yer run about an’ play,
hrankie?” asked the kind lady who didn’t
own him.
Frankie’s mother asked the same question
with pepper added. “ ’Ere! W’y don’t yer
run about like the others?”
Frankie rolled sulky eyes at both of them
and sat on his hands. “Th’ captain mightn’t
like it!”
mot^ler clicked her tongue impatiently.
. That’s ’im all over! Real fool of a kid, ’e
J.S.”
Her lady friend leant forward and gave
the law-abiding child the “look pleasant”
smile. Oh, no he wouldn’t, dear! You run
about an’ play with the others —go on,
there’s a dear!”
I rankie shook his head stubbornly.
Oh, leave ’im alone!” whispered his
mother. ’E’s jus’ like ’is farther—got no
spirit! Look at ’im now!” The friend
turned quickly and detected Frankie in the
act of deciphering a notice: “No Smoking
This End of Steamer.”
“Yes,” snorted his parent, “an’ w’en ’e’s
learnt that orf by ’eart Vll sneak over an’
„ rn j ‘“t ° ne about no pedlars bein’
allowed to offer their wares, an’ the other
one about not travellin’ on other subscribers’
tickets-—penalty five pound. Fair makes
me sick, ’e does!”
Seeing that the subject of Frankie only
annoyed Frankie’s mother, Frankie’s mother’s
harked back to hats and operations.
Hraln* th6 j *u lk , flowed again in pleasant
drains, and the band and the children played
happily while the glum Frankie studied the
by-laws.

“I’ll give you a smack over the ear if you
don’t be’ave yourself!’’
Frankie knew from long experience just
when to stop. He stopped now—but only
under protest. His eyes were wide with
apprehension, for he saw he was being led
into a beautiful ambush studded with white
notice-boards covered all over with written
and signed threats against his life and
liberty. As the little party hurried across
the plank and gained the wharf he sighted
another one and intoned very gravely: “.Rub-
bish Must Not Be Deposited on This Beach.
By Order.”
His mother deftly caught him by the shoul-
der and swung him round so that his young
face was within two inches of her armor-
piercing nose. It was a trick of hers to
make herself more terrible to her own chil-
dren by forcing them to regard her out of
focus. “Now, look ’ere! I’ve ’ad enough o’
you ! If you read out another of them things
I’ll give you an ’idin’! Now re-member
that!” And she released him with a push.
“That’s the way ’e always goes on,” she
sighed to her friend as the party resumed
the advance.
It wasn’t long before they had taken a
pretty summer-house by assault and consoli-
dated their position with all the food and
eating gear they had brought with them.
After dinner Frankie was ordered, on pain of
death, to “go an’ play with the others —don’t
keep pokin’ round ’ere.” He wandered off
like a dog that has been ordered out of the
house into the rain.
Frankie put up a poor pretence of being
interested in “tiggy” while nosing round for
further painted edicts, proclamations, by-
laws and orders-in-council. By strict atten-
tion to business he discovered that he mustn’t
(1) light fires elsewhere than in the fire-
places provided by the trustees, (2) scatter
broken glass, (3) deface the buildings or
fences, (4) bring in dogs, (5) swim horses,
(6) remove sand, (7) pick flowers or shrubs,
(8) fish off the wharf, (9) climb trees. He
also found that any complaints he had to
make must be put in writing and lodged at
the trustees’ office, and that strawberries
and cream could be obtained at the tea kiosk,
price 6d. That gave him an idea, and he
made his way back to where his mother sat
twiddling a few poor wildflowers and telling
her friend about her life’s romance.
“Mum, I want some strawberries an’
cream, Mum!”
She pounced on him with startling
rapidity. “You’re a greedv little beast, an’
if you don’t clear out”
“Pickin’ flowers is prohibited !” he chanted,
solemn eyes fixed on her bouquet—the in-
spiration of the old love story.
She shook a lean fist at him. “I’ll pro-
hibit yer, yer little wretch! You min’ yer
own bizness, an’ don’t be a tell-tale!” A
ticket fell out of her glove and his sharp eye
noted its fall.
“Mum, can I have the ticket? I won’t
lose it, Mum!”
“Oh, all right—all right!”
He snatched up the scrap of colored card-
board and walked away, while his harassed
mother sat down and picked up the broken
threads of conversation.
There was peace for quite half an hour.
Then piercing yells of anguish issuing from
the scrub hoisted both mothers to their feet.
“Somethink’s ’appened !”
“ ’E’s ’urt ’imself! It’s Frankie’s voice!”
Followed a skirted rush like a Highland
charge.
Frankie came staggering from his ambush
with all his limbs on and no blood showing.
But his cheeks were dripping with tears, and
his howls scared the seagulls from the beach.
“I want to go home in th’ tram!” he blub-
bered.
His frightened mother tried to cuddle and
comfort him; but in the extremity of his
terror he kicked her shins.
“What’s the matter, Frankie? Tell mother,
there’s a good boy !”
“Want—go—home in th’ tram!”
“Oh, nonsense; we’re all going ’ome in th’
nice boat afterwards!”
“I’m frightened. DON’T go home in th’
boat!”
“Don’t be silly now ! What’s wrong with
th’ nice boat?”
“I’m frightened,” he bellowed and started
an agonised war-dance.
“But why are yer frightened of it? Didn’t
yer come down on it?”
It’s on th’ ticket!” sobbed Frankie, and
squealed and kicked again. “Want to go
home in the tram!” His mother snatched
the grimed ticket from his paw and read
slowly-
This ticket is the property of the Poodle Bay and
h. S. F. Co., Ltd., and if not delivered up on date of
issue it may be sued for or seized and destroyed by
the Company if and when subsequently presented.
Iry Chordle’s Sixpenny Champagne—You Will Never
Regret It.
Frankie threw himself face down in the
sand. “I don’t want to be seized an’
destroyed!” he yelled. “Take me home in
th’ tram!”
Seized an’ destroyed,’ ” murmured
I rankles mother, and referred again to the
ticket. It puzzled her for a moment; but
when she had repeated the sinister phrase
two or three times she saw the origin of
Frankie’s terror and burst out in a flame of
moral indignation. “Nice sort o’ thing for a
nervis child ter read. Can’t a person have a
tuppenny boat-ride without bein’ threatened
with seizin’ an’ destroyin’! Bless me soul,
ye d think them mis’rable little dirty steam-
ers was battleships the way they treat
customers—frightenin’ th’ life outer their
children an’ all!” She rammed the ticket
into her bag and said, with great determina-
tion : ‘l’ll ’ave a word with th’ capt’in about
this when we’re goin’ back!”
And she did. When the enormous job of
getting Frankie aboard had been completed
and the little ferry was smoking back to
Sydney, the captain in his steering-cabin was
amazed to see a woman’s face appear at the
window. The brisk evening wind was snatch
ing the words from her lips, and he had to
incline his ear and make signs for her to
repeat what she had said.

KODAK.
A Visit from the Parson.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SPRINGTIME at Mugga Flat!
Whilst it is yet night the house-
hold is aroused by the ringing of the
alarm-clock, planted under the kerosene-tin
at the head of dad’s bed. Roany kicked dad
on the ear once, and he doesn’t hear too well
now. Mum is already astir, and presently I
hoar the crackle of the kitchen fire.
Dad comes thumping down the passage on
his way to get the cows.
“Are you awake, Sissy?” he yells, ham-
mering at my door. Dad is a bit of a
humorist at times.
It is beastly cold, but I tumble out of bed
and finish my toilet by the fire, where little
Bill, dazed-looking and only partially
dressed, is crouched against the hob. The
warmth of the room thaws the frost on the
roofing. Drops of discolored water splash
upon the butter and scones laid out on the
table. Dad intended lining the kitchen this
year, but he postponed doing it till
Jim and Bob come home again. Dad also
wanted Jim and Bob to put up the new pig-
sties and get the ferns cut before going into
camp; but Bob reckoned they had better get
away before the spring campaign. He
probably meant the spring campaign at
Mugga Flat.
Macguire, sitting on an empty kerosene
case, had played for Jim and Bob’s farewell
social—until someone had whispered in his
ear that there was champagne for supper.
Then Macguire had fallen over Cyril Marks
and Hannah Bly, who were seated in the
dark part of the hall, in his haste to ascer-
tain the truth of the whispered rumor.
Afterwards the ladies of Mugga Flat had
chased Jim and Bob into a corner, and kissed
them on the lips for luck; and Jim (made
bold by the champagne) had embraced Bob
in mistake for Sally Sparks. Jim wasn’t
used to the taste of champagne.
The day Jim and Bob went away mum
sat in the kitchen with her face in her
apron, and dad stood over by the boundary
fence and watched them till they passed the
lean, white saplings that shut off the road.
By the time we have made a hasty break-
fast and mum has cut little Bill’s lunch,
the cows come lowing do'wn the race —big-
uddered cows with their teats caked in the
mud of glorious spring. In caravan-like
style we straggle down to the shed, and
begin dragging the warfen, squelching milk
into the buckets. Little Bill keeps one eye
on dad and the other on the sun. When he
suspects it is nearing sdbool-time he crawls
through the gap at the head of the bail.
Nancy pulls back, and jfumps'on dad's toe.
Dad, mistaking the caose, asks (mildly)
who let the hens out before the milkin’s
done.
The fog has lifted from the gully when
we hang up the legropes and put the last
cow out through the slushy race.
Mum calls out to dad to keep some milk
back from the separatin’ for the use of the
house; but I don’t think dad hears —ms
sound ear is .turned towards the machine.
Dad issues from the separator room with
an old flour-sack tied like an apron about
his loins, and staggers down to the pigsties
with a tin of feed. Sally sees dad coming,
and lies ambushed behind the trough. When
dad passes she rushes out and knocks him
down. The hot potatoes form a crust
over the top of dad. and Sally and her
progeny imagine him to be a new kind of
saveloy.
Finagan arrives when I am washing the
separator. Finagan and I are going to have
a separator and some cows of our own after
the war. Finagan is a reject, and isn’t at
all concerned about it. He seems, indeed,
to glory in his infirmity.
As usual he insists on helping me, to wash
the buckets; but he will keep gazing up at
me the while, and slops the water all over
the floor.
I remonstrate and he retaliates by
attempting to kiss me —and I slap him over
the mouth with the rag dad has used for
oiling the separator. /
Dad comes up from scraping t’he yard to
know if Finagan has heard anything fresh
of the war. Finagan is throwing pebbles in
the fowls’ tin, and doesn’t hear d.ad; so dad
prods him in the back with the shovel to
attract his attention. Finagan tells dad
that he believes our troops have forced the
Dardanelles. Dad is exultant. He leans
back on his shovel and says that where
there’s work to be done Jim and Bob can
be trusted to do it.
Dad had another yarn when Jim and Bob
were on the farm.
Finagan declines to stay to dinner. He
inquires if dad has seen a red cow with a
crumpled horn about the place. Dad replies
with a grin that he hasn’t, but he’s noticed
a lanky bloke with a dirty mug knocking
around. Finagan goes red and draws his
sleeve across his mouth.
At dinner dad is inclined to be morose.
Mum has made a batch of scones rand has
run out of milk for the tea. She asks dad
what he did with the day’s milt.. Dad
expects Sissy gave it all to the poddies, and
if mum wants more she had better run down
a cow.
Mum ladles out stew in silence.
Dad makes plans for the afternoon. Sissy
could give him a hand at the pigsties. Sally
tore the fence down this morning. (Dad
was after her with the shovel and the head
of a pick-axe.) , , .
Mum wants to know if dad has had the
packing-needle—the sick cow needs rugging,
and last summer Jim and Bob thrashed
grass-seed on the cow-rug. O’ course dad
ain’t seen the packin’-needle.
Dad demolishes a large chunk of bread
and treacle by way of desScrt and goes off
to put fresh bedding in the calves’ pen.
Presently I hear him yelling for the nails.
He is on his knees in the pigsty and wants
me to hold the rail steady. Dad has just
hammered his finger nicely when the gate
behind creaks open. Dad concludes that
Sally has come home again. Seizing the
mallet he hurls it through the gap in the
sty; and then, clutching the axe, he puts
his head out of the hole.
The Rev. Mr. Darey-Woods, rubbing his
shinbone and looking very flustered, is
standing by the fence.
Dad is apologetic. The Rev. Mr. Darey-
Woods is polite. He has called to have a
little chat with dad. but expresses concern
at having disturbed him in the course of his
labors;.
Mum perceives the approach of the Rev.
Mr. Darey-Woods and drops Brindle’s rug
behind the flour-bin. When dad and _ the
parson reach the house she is sitting (in a
state of agitation) sewing at a grey flannel
sl.irt for the Red Cross.
The Rev. Mr. Darey-Woods seems to be
under a nervous apprehension that dad may
escape to the pigsties; but mum makes a
sort of Morse-signal to dad, and he sits down
with his eyes on the clock. It is getting on
for cow-time again.
Mum hands the parson a cup of black
tea. Before he can ask for the milk dad
remarks that a poddy broke into the cream-
house and drank up all we had. The Rev.
Mr. Darey-Woods assures us he revels in
black tea ; but I notice he nibbles round his
scone like a particularly genteel mouse at a
cheese-rind and leaves all the butter on his
saucer. His imagination presents him with
unpleasant suggestions of the poddy that
broke into the cream-house.
It is only half-past three, but dad puts
his cup on the table and reckons he will be
getting after the cows. The Rev. Mr.
Darey-Woods gulps down the last of his
tea (gurgling it in his throat) and follows
dad out to the verandah.
“And when did you hear from James
last?” he asks mum for the third time. He
lets dad get round the corner and then calls
him back.
“What the h —l,” dad begins, put off his
guard for tne moment.
The Rev. Mr. Darey-Woods clasps his
hands together as though about to suppli-
cate; then he tells dad (in the manner of
a parrot making a recitation) that his son
James has been killed, but dad must look
up and rejoice.
Then he takes his leave and neglects to
peg the gate, so that Sally comes in and
starts rooting up the mangels.
But dad is leaning against the verandah-
post and doesn’t seem to notice Sally.
E. M. K.
The Quest.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HE call of lands beyond the hills,
And islands set in splendid seas—
That wander lust that youth instils
With restless yearnings—broke my ease;
For flight my cagM soul was bent;
My home was walled by discontent.
Each little lane and road I knew;
No mystery held field or wood;
But o’er the far horizon grew
Strange things to see, and glamor stood
About them all. I took the track
With no regretful glances back.
I fed the hunger of my soul— _
A rich great hunger, looking high;
With distance ever as my goal,
I roamed the world with eager eye,
Through lands of strange and tropic grace,
But never found a resting-place.
Through islands splashed in vivid hues
Upon the blue of sunny seas,
Where life the wine of languor brews
And joyance grows to careless ease,
I merely passed; my lure was still
O’er spreading sea and distant hill.
I climbed where mountains reared their
heads
In snow-tipped, proud magnificence;
And, where her wonders Nature spreads,
I, passing, gave my reverence;
But only paused—a restless guest
Of all the splendor—in my quest.
The cities, life and laughter lit,
And with a myriad stars ablaze
When o’er the earth night’s shadows flit,
I met, exulting in the ways
Where human arts in beauties smile;
But only stayed a little while.
Oh. there were lands where Nature seemed
To take the brightest hues earth knows,
And splash them wide where sunlight
streamed;
Fair countries scented like a rose,
With that sweet incense that proceeds
From orchards rich, and flowered meads.
And there were places where the tide
Of human life moved great and strong;
To martial music seemed to ride
The triumph of its battle-song;
And for its feasts of joy and mirth
Was culled the best of all the earth.
And there were villages of peace,
Green bowered in their garden-land,
Where, blessed by Nature’s sure increase,
Content and plenty seemed to stand;
A wanderer from spot to spot,
I saw content, yet found it not.
Home I returned, to rest awhile.
What strange, new beauties met my
eyes!
Familiar landmarks seemed to smile
With some quaint charm. ’Twas Para-
dise !
Oh, foolish heart that once was blind —•
This was the place I left to find!
